Factors That Influence Domestic Violence Practice Self-Efficacy: Implications for Social Work

Author: Danis, Fran11 School Social Work, U Missouri, Columbia danisf@missouri.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61384026?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article discusses the results of an exploratory study to identify facilitating factors and barriers for practice self-efficacy with battered women. Based on self-efficacy theory, personal factors such as professional experience, academic preparation, personal experience, and continuing education training were explored with a random sample of licensed and degreed professional social workers. Professional experience (b=.61; t=9.54, p=.000) and academic preparation (b=.26; t=4.29, p=.000) were the strongest predictors of self-efficacy. These findings suggest that to advance the capacity of social workers to respond to domestic violence, social work education, practitioners, and researchers have a number of important steps available to take. Adapted from the source document.

Addressing the Problem of Domestic Violence: How Far Have We Come?

Author: Pyles, Loretta1; Postmus, Judy L1 School Social Welfare, U Kansas, Lawrence lpyles@ku.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61496578?accountid=14925

Abstract: The social work profession has a complex & arguably problematic history in relation to the problem of domestic violence. Through a review of abstracts of social work articles published from 1985 to 2000, the authors sought to determine whether anything has changed since Davis's (1987) seminal study regarding the way in which the profession addresses the problem of domestic violence. The findings indicate that although researchers have done a good job of listening to the voices of battered women, there has been scant theorizing in the literature about the sexist origins of the problem. 44 References. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Inc., copyright 2004.]

Wife Abuse in the Countries of the Former Yugoslavia

Author: Copic, Sanja11 Instit Criminological & Sociological Research, Belgrade

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60490349?accountid=14925

Abstract: This paper contains some of the most important results obtained from surveys on domestic violence in Serbia & Macedonia, as well as from a survey on violence against women in the Federation of Bosnia & Herzegovina. These surveys have confirmed the results of other research showing that wife abuse is one of the most serious yet at the same time most hidden forms of victimization. It represents the manifestation of power & control over the victim, leading to a loss of trust & a threat to safety. Bearing that in mind, the aim of this paper is to examine the prevalence, structure & some features of wife abuse in the postwar societies of the former Yugoslavia, based on an analysis of the results of these surveys, with particular emphasis on the impact of the economic crisis & war on this phenomenon. 29 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=Wife+Abuse+in+the+Countries+of+the+Former+Yugoslavia&amp;title=Feminist+Review&amp;issn=01417789&amp;date=2004-01-01&amp;volume=&amp;issue=76&amp;spage=46&amp;author=Copic%2C+Sanja

Towards Understanding Domestic Violence: Reflections on Research and the 'Domestic Violence and Minoritization' Project

Author: Pratt, Rebekah1; Burman, Erica; Chantler, Khatidja1 Sub-Dept Clinical Health Psychology, University Coll London, UK Rebekahjpratt@hotmail.com

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60465551?accountid=14925

Abstract: Presents key findings of the "Domestic Violence & Minoritization: Supporting Women to Independence" project carried out from Sept 2001 to July 2002 in Manchester, England. The study addressed issues of minoritization & domestic violence by examining the views of abuse survivors & service providers. The term, "minoritization," is used to describe how unequal power relations between minority & majority groups are revealed in systems/structures that perpetuate privilege, especially structure barriers & complex dynamics that keep women in abusive situations. Minoritization is seen as a way to discuss broad political/social processes that reinforce the consequences of minority group identification. Specific issues discussed include how domestic violence is conceptualized; structural barriers to services or independence; cultural issues that serve as barriers to services; the impact of domestic violence on children; the difficult transition from an abusive relationship to independence; & support groups for women who have experienced domestic violence. The critical importance of research & practice in the area of domestic violence is discussed. 6 References. J. Lindroth

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=Towards+Understanding+Domestic+Violence%3A+Reflections+on+Research+and+the+%27Domestic+Violence+and+Minoritization%27+Project&amp;title=Journal+of+Community+%26+Applied+Social+Psychology&amp;issn=10529284&amp;date=2004-01-01&amp;volume=14&amp;issue=1&amp;spage=33&amp;author=Pratt%2C+Rebekah%3BBurman%2C+Erica%3BChantler%2C+Khatidja

Author: Tuerkheimer, Deborah

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60291934?accountid=14925

Abstract: Tuerkheimer explores the rupture between women's experiences of battering and the remediation offered by the criminal law. She concludes that statutory descriptions of battering are inapt and require an overhaul to capture the practice of domestic violence.

Elder Abuse: Spouse/Intimate Partner Abuse and Family Violence among Elders

Author: Lundy, Marta; Grossman, Susan F

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60524246?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article examines the experiences of 1,057 victims of domestic violence, age sixty-five & older who sought refuge, support & services from domestic violence programs between 1990 & 1995 in a large mid-western state. Specifically, the various circumstances of these elders in relation to age, gender, disability, types of abuse, relationship to the abuser, source of referral, & service needs are described. Although it is generally considered unusual for women over sixty who are victims of violence to seek services through domestic violence programs, this study substantiates that at least some elders who are victims of violence do so. The implications of the findings related to the unique needs of this population are discussed. 4 Tables, 29 References. Adapted from the source document. Dating Violence: A Qualitative Analysis of Mexican American Youth's Views

Author: Black, Beverly M1; Weisz, Arlene N1 School Social Work, Wayne State U, Detroit, MI

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61561534?accountid=14925

Abstract: This study reports the findings of focus groups examining the thoughts & attitudes of Mexican American youth about dating violence. Youth in the focus groups expressed concern about the wide spread use of violence, & many reported the common occurrences of violent behaviors in their lives. Moreover, the youth voiced the acceptance of the use of violence in many situations especially when violence is used to retaliate for a peer's lack of respect or infidelity. Males expressed a willingness to intervene on behalf of female friends who were victimized by violence. The males reported that they turned to friends for assistance when they were confronted with violence. Females involved in relationship violence stated that they preferred to turn to their brothers for assistance. Implications for developing culturally sensitive prevention programming are discussed. 1 Table, 50 References. Adapted from the source document. Adolescent Dating Violence on School Campuses

Author: Ely, Gretchen11 Coll Social Work, U Kentucky, Lexington gretchen.ely@uky.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61518447?accountid=14925

Abstract: This paper reviews the adolescent dating violence literature in order to synthesize the risk factors that are associated with dating violence in school-aged adolescents. A review of the adolescent dating violence literature indicates that risk factors occur in three domains: individual, familial, & societal. Research has shown that up to 43% of students report dating violence occurring on school grounds. School social workers & other school personnel must develop intervention programs based on empirical evidence to prevent adolescent dating violence on school campuses. In addition, schools must implement policies to protect victims from such violence. 60 References. Adapted from the source document. Battering and the Poverty Trap

Author: Brush, Lisa D11 Dept Sociology, U Pittsburgh, PA lbrush@pitt.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61557648?accountid=14925

Abstract: Structured interviews with women on welfare (n = 40) reveal the costs of taking a beating. Thirty-five percent of respondents reported having been physically abused by their current or most recent intimate partner. Compared with their peers, physically abused women earned less, worked fewer weeks, & more frequently worked part-time involuntarily. Women whose partners sabotaged their work effort experienced more hardships associated with poverty than did other respondents. The causal connections between the dual traps of poverty & abuse are complex. Irrespective of the direction of causality, battering & poverty diminish the lives of a significant proportion of women on welfare. 62 References. Adapted from the source document. The Battering State: Towards a Political Economy of Domestic Violence

Author: Adelman, Madelaine11 School Justice Studies, Arizona State U, Tempe mad@asu.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60503593?accountid=14925

Abstract: A political economy of domestic violence situates domestic violence within a cultural-historical context to reveal the intersection between domestic violence & (1) the organization of the polity, (2) the arrangement of the economy, & (3) the dominant familial ideology expressed normatively through state policies. The combination of these components makes visible the articulation between domestic violence & an often invisible set of conditions in US society -- structural inequality as shaped by 'family values' & the logic of state-economy relations. The analysis of the political economy of battering as it intersects with poverty & globalization highlights the contours of "the battering state.". 74 References. Adapted from the source document. Adolescent Girls' Alcohol Use as a Risk Factor for Relationship Violence

Author: Buzy, Wendy Marsh1; McDonald, Renee; Jouriles, Ernest N; Swank, Paul R; Rosenfield, David; Shimek, Jennifer S; Corbitt-Shindler, Deborah1 Dept Psychology, U Houston, TX

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60518944?accountid=14925

Abstract: This research examined the relation between female adolescents' general alcohol use & their experience of relationship violence. This relation was examined both cross-sectionally & longitudinally, controlling for the proximal (ie, situational) effects of alcohol use. One hundred & six female high school students reported on their experiences of physical violence & sexual coercion by boyfriends, general patterns of alcohol use, victimization experiences while drinking, & hypothesized covariates including demographic & relationship variables & illicit drug use. Variables were assessed at 2 time points 4 months apart. Results indicated that general alcohol use was related to victimization both cross-sectionally & longitudinally, but different findings emerged for different forms of victimization (physical-only victimization vs both physical & sexual victimization). 4 Tables, 2 Figures, 37 References. Adapted from the source document.

Intimate Partner Violence as an Obstacle to Employment among Mothers Affected by Welfare Reform

Author: Riger, Stephanie1; Staggs, Susan L; Schewe, Paul1 U Illinois, Chicago sriger@uic.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60504208?accountid=14925

Abstract: Welfare policies in the US now make benefits contingent on employment outside the home. Yet violence from intimate partners & aspects of the mothering role may impede low-income women's ability to sustain employment. This article presents results of a longitudinal study conducted over a three-year period of 965 IL mothers who had received public assistance. Results suggest that recent (but not past) intimate partner violence is associated with women working fewer months. Associations over time between obstacles to employment & women's ability to maintain work highlight the need for longitudinal studies of employment among low-income women. 3 Tables, 50 References. Adapted from the source document.

Risk of Domestic Violence after Natural Disaster: Teaching Research and Statistics through the Use of a Participatory Action Research Model

Author: Reese, Donna J11 School Social Work, U Arkansas, Fayetteville reese@uark.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61536416?accountid=14925

Abstract: This paper describes the use of a participatory action research model to teach undergraduate social work research & statistics. Strategies of the model include (1) integration with social work education, (2) policy analysis, (3) literature review, (4) collaboration with practitioners, (5) collaboration with the target population through qualitative research, (6) quantitative study, (7) ongoing social action efforts, & (8) evaluation. An example is used of a project addressing a community problem with increased domestic violence during recovery from natural disaster. 38 References. Adapted from the source document. Religion, State Power, and Domestic Violence in Muslim Societies: A Framework for Comparative Analysis

Author: Hajjar, Lisa11 Law & Society Program, U California, Santa Barbara

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60103713?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article focuses on the issue of domestic violence in Muslim societies in the Middle East, Africa, & Asia. The analytical framework is comparative, emphasizing four factors & the interplay among them: shari'a (Islamic law), state power, intrafamily violence, & struggles over women's rights. The comparative approach historicizes the problem of domestic violence & impunity to consider the impact of transnational legal discourses (Islamism & human rights) on "local" struggles over rights & law. The use of shari'a creates some commonalities in gender & family relations in Muslim societies, notably the sanctioning & maintenance of male authority over female relatives. However, the most important issue for understanding domestic violence & impunity is the relationship between religion & state power. This relationship takes three forms: communalization, in which religious law is separate from the national legal regime; nationalization, in which the state incorporates religious law into the national legal regime; & theocratization, in which the national legal regime is based on religious law. 139 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://GS6DU7GB6H.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Religion%2C+State+Power%2C+and+Domestic+Violence+in+Muslim+Societies%3A+A+Framework+for+Comparative+Analysis&amp;author=Hajjar%2C+Lisa&amp;issn=08976546&amp;title=Law+%26+Social+Inquiry&amp;volume=29&amp;issue=1&amp;date=2004-01-01&amp;spage=1&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Screening Risks for Intimate Partner Violence and Primary Care Settings: Implications for Future Abuse

Author: Ross, Julie1; Walther, Virginia; Epstein, Irwin1 Dept Community & Preventive Medicine, Mount Sinai School Medicine, New York, NY julier7725@msn.com

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61556701?accountid=14925

Abstract: Most health care intervention models for intimate partner violence (IPV) are crisis driven & targeted to survivors of injury following episodes of physical violence. Knowledge about anticipatory & preventive approaches with women who are at risk for abuse is scarce, limiting professionals' ability to respond fully & effectively to this problem. This paper describes a retrospective, practice-based research study of social work interventions in two hospital-based primary care practices. A total of 431 female patients completed a self-administered questionnaire developed for the early detection of IPV risk factors during routine health care visits. The study showed surprisingly high rates of multiple risk markers in an urban primary care population whose medical presentations ordinarily would not raise provider suspicion about abuse. Findings confirmed the willingness of primary care populations to freely partake in routine screening & support the value of early identification & intervention with populations at risk. Practice implications are discussed. 3 Tables, 1 Figure, 41 References. Adapted from the source document. THE PREVALENCE AND INCIDENCE OF DOMESTIC VIOLENCE IN OLDER WOMEN IN PRIMARY CARE PRACTICES: WHY IT IS IMPORTANT TO SCREEN

Author: Regan, S; Gothelf, E; Zink, T; Rinto, B; Et al

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61341362?accountid=14925

Abstract: An abstract of a study by Regan et al examining the prevalence and incidence of domestic violence (DV) in older women in primary care clinics is presented. DV is a problem for women over 55, with lower rates of physical abuse and similar rates of emotional abuse to those of younger women. Health care and social service providers are encouraged to screen all women, especially those with conditions more frequently associated with DV.

INTIMATE PARTNER VIOLENCE OF MID-LIFE AND OLDER WOMEN IN RURAL AREAS

Author: Teaster, P; Dugar, T

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61344983?accountid=14925

Abstract: An abstract of a study that examines intimate partner violence of mid-life and older women in rural areas from the perspective of those who provide service, as well as from the perspective of Appalachian women over 50 year by Teaster and Dugar is presented. The findings include a lack of resources available to mid-life and older women; a lack of training for professional staff on unique issues of the age group; and a lack of community support options for the women.

Prevalence and Chronicity of Dating Violence Among a Sample of African-American University Students

Author: Bougere, Alan A1; Rowley, Lucille M; Lee, Glenell M1 Tulane University

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61743178?accountid=14925

Abstract: This study investigated African-American college students in the Southern United States as a part of the International Dating Violence Consortium. The study used the Personal and Relationship Profile (PRP) and the Conflict Tactics Scale (CTS2) to study factors important in defining dating violence among African-American college students. The sample was comprised of college students from three historically black universities and one historically white university from the southern United States recruited from social work classes in the spring and summer 2002 semesters. Of those, 228 students identified themselves as African-American. The findings indicate that this sample of African-American college students in the southern United States looks similar to Euro-American samples when it comes to experiencing different forms of dating violence. Adapted from the source document.

Abject Economics: The Effects of Battering and Violence on Women's Work and Employability

Author: Moe, Angela M1; Bell, Myrtle P1 Western Michigan U, Kalamazoo

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60476976?accountid=14925

Abstract: Research on the effects of battering on women's lives has focused on poverty, homelessness, & welfare receipt, often centering on women who are uneducated or undereducated. The authors analyze how battering impacts the work & employability of women from various employment levels & backgrounds. Data were obtained through qualitative interviews with 19 residents of a domestic violence shelter, some of whom had obtained substantial education & built solid & lucrative careers prior to being abused. The women described instances in which battering had obstructed their ability to find work, maintain employment, & use their wages to establish greater economic independence & safety. 76 References. Acculturation, Drinking, and Intimate Partner Violence among Hispanic Couples in the United States: A Longitudinal Study

Author: Caetano, Raul; Ramisetty-Mikler, Suhasini; McGrath, Christine

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/810385509?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article examines the 5-year association between acculturation, drinking, and maleto-female partner violence and female-to-male partner violence among Hispanic couples in the United States. A national representative sample of Hispanic couples 18 years of age or older was interviewed in 1995 and 2000. Both members of the couple were independently interviewed. Differences in prevalence rates of male-to-female partner violence and female-to-male partner violence, incidence, and recurrence across acculturation groups are not significant. Drinking is associated with acculturation among women. Couples with mixed acculturation level (high-medium) are less at risk for maleto-female partner violence. An increase of five standard drinks per week in men's drinking decreases the risk of female-to-male partner violence by 10%. Acculturation level at Time 1 is not associated with male-to-female partner violence and female-to-male partner violence status 5 years later.

Comparing Women and Men Arrested for Domestic Violence: A Preliminary Report

Author: Busch, Amy L1; Rosenberg, Mindy S1 Clinical Science Program, Dept Psychology, U California, Berkeley abusch@socrates.berkeley.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61518573?accountid=14925

Abstract: This study uses criminal justice data to compare women & men arrested for domestic violence on their levels of violence, reported victimization, general criminality, & substance abuse. Participants were 45 women & 45 men convicted of domestic violence between 1996 & 1998. Results indicate that women were less likely than men to have a history of domestic violence offenses & nonviolent crimes. They were also more likely to report that they had been injured or victimized by their partner at the time of their arrest. However, in other ways, women & men were similar: they were equally likely to have used severe violence & inflicted severe injuries on their victims; to have previously committed violence against non-intimates; & to have been using drugs or alcohol at the time of their arrest. The implications for treatment for women arrested for domestic violence are discussed. 2 Tables, 27 References. Adapted from the source document.

A Path Model of Risk Factors for Intimate Partner Violence among Couples in the United States

Author: Schafer, John1; Caetano, Raul; Cunradi, Carol B1 Coll Nursing, U Cincinnati, OH john.schafer@uc.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60483985?accountid=14925

Abstract: The present study was designed to identify the impact of drinking problems, impulsivity, & a history of childhood physical abuse on both male-to-female (MFIPV) & female-to-male intimate partner violence (FMIPV). The data were collected in 1995 from a representative national sample of couples living in the contiguous 48 states. Using a multistage probability sampling design, face-to-face interviews were conducted in respondents' homes, privately with each member of 1,635 couples. A complex path model building on earlier work was tested for African American, Hispanic, & White couples separately. Multiple-group path analysis demonstrated that impulsivity, alcohol problems, & childhood physical abuse were differentially associated with reports of MFIPV & FMIPV as a function of ethnicity. This study suggests that a history of being physically harmed by parental figures during childhood, impulsivity, & drinking problems are all risk factors for intimate partner violence in the general household population in the United States. 2 Tables, 3 Figures, 51 References. [Copyright 2004 Sage Publications, Inc.]

Adolescent Dating Violence: Do Adolescents Follow in Their Friends', or Their Parents', Footsteps?

Author: Arriaga, Ximena B1; Foshee, Vangie A1 Dept Psychological Sciences, Purdue U, West Lafayette, IN arriaga@purdue.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60488652?accountid=14925

Abstract: Past research suggests that adolescents whose parents are violent toward one another should be more likely to experience dating violence. Having friends in violent relationships also may increase the odds of dating violence. The authors examined which antecedent, friend dating violence or interparental violence, if either, is more strongly predictive of own dating violence perpetration & victimization. Five hundred & twenty-six adolescents (eighth & ninth graders) completed self-report questionnaires on two occasions over a 6-month period. Consistent with hypotheses, friend dating violence & interparental violence each exhibited unique cross-sectional associations with own perpetration & victimization. However, only friend violence consistently predicted later dating violence. The authors explored influence versus selection processes to explain the association between friend & own dating violence. 2 Tables, 1 Figure, 40 References. [Copyright 2004 Sage Publications, Inc.]

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=Adolescent+Dating+Violence%3A+Do+Adolescents+Follow+in+Their+Friends%27%2C+or+Their+Parents%27%2C+Footsteps%3F&amp;title=Journal+of+Interpersonal+Violence&amp;issn=08862605&amp;date=2004-02-01&amp;volume=19&amp;issue=2&amp;spage=162&amp;author=Arriaga%2C+Ximena+B%3BFoshee%2C+Vangie+A

The Role of Alcohol Use in Intimate Partner Violence and Nonintimate Partner Violence

Author: Thompson, Martie P1; Kingree, J B1 Dept Public Health Sciences, Clemson U, SC

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60491577?accountid=14925

Abstract: Alcohol use is a risk factor for violent victimization in general, & intimate partner violence in particular. However, there has been limited empirical attention on whether alcohol use is more often associated with IPV or non-IPV offenses. Further, few studies on the association between alcohol use & victimization have assessed for incident-specific alcohol use, or examined both victim & perpetrator alcohol use. We used data from the National Violence against Women Survey to determine if incident-specific alcohol use is more prevalent in IPV or non-IPV physical assaults, & to determine if the association varied by gender. Results indicated that among women, perpetrator alcohol use was twice as likely in IPV incidents than in non-IPV incidents, but among men, perpetrator alcohol use was four times less likely in IPV incidents than in non-IPV incidents. Our results highlight the important role of gender in understanding the association between alcohol use & victimization risk. 2 Tables, 39 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=The+Role+of+Alcohol+Use+in+Intimate+Partner+Violence+and+Nonintimate+Partner+Violence&amp;title=Violence+and+Victims&amp;issn=08866708&amp;date=2004-02-01&amp;volume=19&amp;issue=1&amp;spage=63&amp;author=Thompson%2C+Martie+P%3BKingree%2C+J+B

Supporting Community Solutions to Family Violence

Author: Taylor, Judy1; Cheers, Brian; Weetra, Colin; Gentle, Ian1 Spencer Gulf Rural Health School judy.taylor@unisa.edu.au

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61535558?accountid=14925

Abstract: Competing discourses have always figured prominently in policy debates about violence against women & children. Legislation & services to address it have largely been developed from theory & empirical knowledge of domestic violence occurring in intimate relationships. Knowledge from an Indigenous perspective about family violence, embedded in a context of colonization & involving a broader range of relationships, has been less influential. One of the consequences of this is that when Indigenous communities identify solutions to address family violence holistically, these solutions are difficult to implement. Research with a remote South Australian Aboriginal community, looking for ways to reduce the level of family violence, resulted in new understandings of it. Linking issues of economic deprivation, dispossession & the breaking down of culture, the community devised a holistic community development approach to reduce family violence. This paper identifies the waves of understanding about family violence & argues that community-initiated solutions must be supported by policy & funding options based on an Indigenous understanding of family violence. 24 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=Supporting+Community+Solutions+to+Family+Violence&amp;title=Australian+Social+Work&amp;issn=0312407X&amp;date=2004-03-01&amp;volume=57&amp;issue=1&amp;spage=71&amp;author=Taylor%2C+Judy%3BCheers%2C+Brian%3BWeetra%2C+Colin%3BGentle%2C+Ian

Intimate Partner Violence among Female Caregivers of Children Reported for Child Maltreatment

Author: Hazen, Andrea L1; Connelly, Cynthia D; Kelleher, Kelly; Landsverk, John; Barth, Richard1 Child & Adolescent Services Research Center, Children's Hospital & Health Center, San Diego, CA

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61548169?accountid=14925

Abstract: Objective: The purpose of this study was to determine the prevalence & correlates of intimate partner violence among female caregivers of children reported to child protective services. Method: Data were derived from the National Survey of Child & Adolescent Well-Being, a national probability study of children investigated for child abuse & neglect in the United States. Caregivers were interviewed about demographic characteristics, mental health, substance use, & physical violence by a partner. Results: The lifetime & past year prevalence of intimate partner violence was 44.8 & 29.0%, respectively. Caregiver major depression & history of prior reports of child maltreatment were strongly associated with violence against women. Conclusions: The findings highlight the need for effective screening & identification of intimate partner violence in families in which child maltreatment has occurred. 4 Tables, 1 Figure, 49 References. [Copyright 2004 Elsevier Ltd.]

Risky Lifestyles and Dating Violence: A Theoretical Test of Violent Victimization

Author: Gover, Angela R11 Center Studies Criminology & Law, U Florida, Gainesville agover@crim.ufl.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60549732?accountid=14925

Abstract: Research consistently indicates that there are numerous risk factors associated with dating violence. Few studies, however, developed theoretical explanations for the prevalence of dating victimization. In this study victimization theories were tested that suggested risk-taking behaviors (i.e., drug abuse, alcohol abuse, driving under the influence, & sexual promiscuity) mediated the effects of social ties & emotional states on the likelihood of violent victimization in adolescent dating relationships. This model was tested using a representative sample of public high school students in South Carolina. The results confirm theoretical predictions & indicate the effects of social ties on dating victimization occur indirectly, through their antecedent influence on risk taking. These findings, therefore, lend support for a lifestyles theory explanation of violent victimization in adolescent dating relationships. The implications of this research for theory & social policy are discussed. 2 Tables, 1 Figure, 1 Appendix, 62 References. [Copyright 2004 Elsevier Ltd.]

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=Risky+Lifestyles+and+Dating+Violence%3A+A+Theoretical+Test+of+Violent+Victimization&amp;title=Journal+of+Criminal+Justice&amp;issn=00472352&amp;date=2004-03-01&amp;volume=32&amp;issue=2&amp;spage=171&amp;author=Gover%2C+Angela+R

Finding Common Ground in the Study of Child Maltreatment, Youth Violence, and Adult Domestic Violence

Author: Daro, Deborah1; Edleson, Jeffrey L; Pinderhughes, Howard1 c/o Edleson -- School Social Work, U Minnesota, St. Paul

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60484239?accountid=14925

Abstract: For several decades, we have witnessed a surge in public policies aimed at ending child maltreatment, youth violence, & adult domestic violence. Commensurate with this increased interest has been a growing body of research on each issue's etiology, affected population, & the public policy & prevention impacts. Even a cursory review of the literature suggests a number of commonalities across these forms of violence. As such, it seems timely & prudent to craft a research framework that facilitates the identification & dissemination of practice & policy innovations that can address all three concerns. This article articulates an overarching framework to guide researchers in better identifying common avenues of study. After summarizing the commonalities found across the three areas, the authors identify crosscutting issues that have particular relevance for advancing our understanding of violence & its effects on personal & social interactions. 54 References. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Inc., copyright 2004.]

Advancing Prevention Research on Child Abuse, Youth Violence, and Domestic Violence: Emerging Strategies and Issues

Author: Guterman, Neil B11 School Social Work, Columbia U

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61500933?accountid=14925

Abstract: Prevention research on the related problems of child abuse, youth violence, & domestic violence has grown at an accelerating pace in recent years. In this context, a set of shared methodological issues has emerged as investigators seek to advance the interpersonal violence prevention knowledge base. This article considers some of the persistent methodological issues in these areas & points out emerging research strategies that are forging advances in garnering valid, rigorous, & useful knowledge to prevent interpersonal violence. Research issues & emerging strategies in three key domains of prevention research are considered, including complexities in validly conceptualizing & measuring varying forms of violence as specific targets for preventive intervention, research issues & strategies designed to reliably predict & identify future violence risk to be targeted by preventive intervention, & research issues & emerging strategies in the application of empirical methods to forge specific advances in preventive intervention strategies themselves. 80 References. The Nexus of Domestic Violence and Poverty. Resilience in Women's Anxiety

Author: Williams, Stacey L1; Mickelson, Kristin D1 Kent State University

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/811126710?accountid=14925

Abstract: This study extends past research by examining the mediating role of cognitive and social resilience in the domestic violence-anxiety relation. Mediation is tested on a sample of 100 impoverished women from the Women's Health Outcomes in Urban and Rural Environments (Women's HOUR) Study. Regression analysis and structural equation modeling provide consistent support for the proposed model: poor women's violence experience impairs support and self-esteem, which in turn influences their anxiety levels. Results demonstrate the dynamic role impaired resilience may play in the violence process, highlighting implications for research, practice, and policy at the nexus of violence and poverty.

Severe Domestic Violence and Child Maltreatment: Considering Child Physical Abuse, Neglect, and Failure to Protect

Author: Hartley, Carolyn C11 School Social Work, U Iowa, Iowa City carolyn-hartley@uiowa.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61497677?accountid=14925

Abstract: This study examined differences between families in which less or more severe domestic violence & child maltreatment were present compared to families in which only child maltreatment was known to be present. Both child physical abuse & neglect were examined using a child maltreatment reports involving parental perpetrators. The study found a higher percentage of neglect, specifically lack of supervision, & less child physical abuse in the more severe domestic violence compared to the child maltreatment only & less severe domestic violence groups. More mothers were found responsible for lack of supervision & fewer fathers were responsible for child physical abuse in the severe domestic violence group. Child protection workers also reported a higher level of concern for cases involving domestic violence. However, a closer examination of the qualitative characteristics of the domestic violence cases revealed that quantitative findings do not accurately present the whole story. Both mothers & fathers engaged in serious acts of physical abuse against their children. In addition, many mothers were held responsible to 'failure to protect' their children from the father's domestic violence. Implications for child welfare practice & policy & future research directions are addressed. 4 Tables, 38 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=Severe+Domestic+Violence+and+Child+Maltreatment%3A+Considering+Child+Physical+Abuse%2C+Neglect%2C+and+Failure+to+Protect&amp;title=Children+and+Youth+Services+Review&amp;issn=01907409&amp;date=2004-04-01&amp;volume=26&amp;issue=4&amp;spage=373&amp;author=Hartley%2C+Carolyn+C

Necessary Measures and Logistics to Maximize the Safety of Victims of Domestic Violence Attending Parent Education Programs

Author: Lutz, Victoria L1; Gady, Cara E1 Pace Women's Justice Center, Pace U School Law, White Plains, NY

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61553559?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article suggests measures that should be taken to ensure, as much as possible, the safety of victims of domestic violence. Because victims & perpetrators of domestic violence will inevitably be attendees at parent education programs, such programs must be sensitive to the safety concerns of these victims. Only when victims of domestic violence are safe will their children be safe & able to benefit from what their parents learn at these education programs. This article concludes that, by weaving common-sense logistical, administrative, & curricular recommendations into the fabric of every parent education program, the safety of victims & children will be prioritized as it must be, while all attendees still receive the maximum benefit from these valuable programs. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Inc., copyright 2004 Association of Family and Conciliation Courts.]

Managing Domestic Violence Cases in Family Court Social Services in Israel

Author: Laufer, Hanna11 School Social Work, Faculty Social Welfare & Health Studies, Mt. Carmel, Haifa, Israel

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61522347?accountid=14925

Abstract: The family courts in Israel provide social services. One of the more difficult issues that this service deals with is domestic violence. The present research evaluated management of referred cases to the Family Court Social Services, according to two criteria: reaching agreement & receiving treatment. Of 272 cases, 53% reached successful outcomes. Additional findings indicate that when the referral to the unit is made earlier, & if the marriage duration has been short, the probability of successful referral increases. The study also found that monitoring of cases referred to other agencies needs to be improved. 7 Tables, 37 References. Adapted from the source document.

Comprehensive Evaluation: A Holistic Approach to Evaluating Domestic Violence Offender Programmes

Author: Bowen, Erica1; Gilchrist, Elizabeth1 Postdoctoral Research Psychologist, Avon Longitudinal Study of Parents and Children, Departent of Community-Based Medicine, University of Bristol, 24 Tyndall Avenue, Bristol, BS8 1TQ, England

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/810471810?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article argues that the focus on behavioural outcomes in evaluations of domestic violence offender programmes has been too narrow, and that a more holistic approach incorporating investigation of the psychological characteristics of offenders and treatment characteristics may be required to determine what works, for whom, and under what circumstances. To this end, one interpretation of a comprehensive evaluation framework is outlined. The article hypothesises the insights to be gained by assessing the contribution of several potential mediating variables of the treatment process in the evaluation of these programmes. These variables are the heterogeneity of treatment targets in the target population; motivation to change; programme integrity variables; and therapeutic factors. The article concludes that only by implementing theoretically informed, multifaceted evaluations will we move closer to understanding the process of successful rehabilitation.

A Cooperative Approach to Accountability: Manitoba's Family Violence Prevention Program

Author: Brown, Laura K1; Troutt, Elizabeth1 Dept Economics, U Manitoba, Winnipeg Lbrown@cc.umanitoba.ca

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61511503?accountid=14925

Abstract: Can government's need for nonprofit accountability be accomplished without diminishing nonprofit organizations' ability to pursue their goals of responsiveness & flexibility? The conventional view argues that governments' accountability objectives conflict with nonprofits' objectives, implying that there must be some tradeoff. This article adopts the emerging alternative view in which the two parties' objectives are jointly pursued through a cooperative process. The analysis of a provincial funding program in Manitoba, Canada, presented here, provides evidence that such an approach is not only possible but also efficient. The program analyzed rests on a sustained cooperative process in which government officials & nonprofit managers jointly define goals & establish constraints. 23 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=A+Cooperative+Approach+to+Accountability%3A+Manitoba%27s+Family+Violence+Prevention+Program&amp;title=International+Journal+of+Public+Administration&amp;issn=01900692&amp;date=2004-04-01&amp;volume=27&amp;issue=5&amp;spage=309&amp;author=Brown%2C+Laura+K%3BTroutt%2C+Elizabeth

A Comparison of Men and Women Arrested for Domestic Violence: Who Presents the Greater Threat?

Author: Henning, Kris1; Feder, Lynette1 Portland State U, OR khenning@pdx.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61432821?accountid=14925

Abstract: Recent increases in the number of women arrested for domestic violence raise important questions about implementation of proarrest policies, equivalency of intimate partner aggression across genders, & management of female domestic violence offenders. This study compares demographic characteristics, criminal history variables, & the past domestic violence history of men (n = 5,578) & women (n = 1,126) arrested for domestic assault against a heterosexual intimate partner. Using victim reported information & data collected by local criminal justice agencies, we found that female arrestees were significantly less likely than males to have histories that warrant concern regarding the potential for future violence. Implications of these findings are discussed. 6 Tables, 50 References. Adapted from the source document.

Domestic Violence in Hispanics in the Southeastern United States: A Survey and Needs Analysis

Author: Murdaugh, Carolyn1; Hunt, Salena; Sowell, Richard; Santana, Irma1 Coll Nursing, U South Carolina, Columbia carolyn.murdaugh@sc.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61555450?accountid=14925

Abstract: Violence is a significant health issue for Latinas in the US, with 54.9% reporting violent victimization. However, little is known about battering among Hispanic women in the southeast. The southeastern corridor, a rural area in search of cheap labor, is a magnet for undocumented agricultural workers. The purpose of this study was to describe domestic violence in Hispanic/Latino women in the southeastern US, including type & frequency of violence experienced, barriers to obtaining treatment, & services needed by women who have been abused. Hispanic women completed questionnaires developed from information from focus groups. Results indicate that domestic violence is a major health problem for women in the southeast & document previous findings as well as offer new insights about Hispanic women who have experienced domestic violence. Implications are discussed in terms of the need for community partnerships & interventions, as well as routine screening & crisis intervention. 3 Tables, 36 References. Comparing Different Types of Child Abuse and Spouse Abuse Offenders

Author: Pittman, Joe F1; Lee, Chih-Yuan S1 Dept Human Development & Family Studies, Auburn U, AL joe.pittman@auburn.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61557900?accountid=14925

Abstract: This study examined differences in offenders' background characteristics, personal & interpersonal problems, & family climate between three types of child abuse offenders (neglecters, physical abusers, & psychological abusers) & two forms of spouse abuse offenders (physical abusers & psychological abusers) in two large samples (child abuse N = 2,910; spouse abuse; N = 7,035) of cases officially identified over a 8-year period (1988-1995) by the US Air Force Family Advocacy Program. Comparisons addressed demographic factors, personal & interpersonal problems, & aspects of family climate. Among child abusers, results supported the conclusion that types of child abuse varied with offender demographics & family climate factors. For spouse abusers, however, types of abuse were not as distinctly different in terms of the comparison variables. In general, therefore, for child abuse -- but not for spouse abuse -- findings challenge the view of abuse as a unitary phenomenon. 6 Tables, 33 References. Adapted from the source document.

The Role of Social Support in the Psychological Well-Being of African American Girls Who Experience Dating Violence Victimization

Author: Salazar, Laura F1; Wingood, Gina M; DiClemente, Ralph J; Lang, Delia L; Harrington, Kathy1 Rollins School Public Health, Atlanta, GA

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61455372?accountid=14925

Abstract: The goals of this study were to test the relationship between dating violence victimization (ie, verbal, emotional, & physical abuse) & psychological well-being (ie, depressive symptomatology, self-esteem, & body image) among 522 African American girls, & to determine whether social support acted as a buffer of negative effects (moderator) or as an intervening factor (mediator) in the relationship between dating violence victimization & psychological well-being. Results from structural equation modeling indicated that dating violence victimization was associated with negative psychological outcomes. Although social support did not moderate this relationship, it served as a mediator of the relationship between dating violence victimization & psychological well-being. Dating violence programs for African American girls should consider how to incorporate family, church, & other networks in the community to foster support, & allow adolescent girls to discuss their abusive experiences in a nonblaming environment. If programs are able to buoy girls who experience dating violence, then they may be able to ameliorate the associated negative psychological sequelae. 3 Tables, 1 Figure, 62 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=The+Role+of+Social+Support+in+the+Psychological+Well-Being+of+African+American+Girls+Who+Experience+Dating+Violence+Victimization&amp;title=Violence+and+Victims&amp;issn=08866708&amp;date=2004-04-01&amp;volume=19&amp;issue=2&amp;spage=171&amp;author=Salazar%2C+Laura+F%3BWingood%2C+Gina+M%3BDiClemente%2C+Ralph+J%3BLang%2C+Delia+L%3BHarrington%2C+Kathy

The Influence of Gender and Ethnicity on Judgments of Culpability in a Domestic Violence Scenario

Author: Ferguson, Christopher J1; Negy, Charles1 c/o Negy -- Dept Psychology, U Central Florida, Orlando

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60544337?accountid=14925

Abstract: Using an experimental analog design, in this study we examined 503 European American, African American, & Latino undergraduate students' responses to a domestic violence scenario in which the ethnicity & gender of the perpetrator were manipulated. Results indicated that participants perceived perpetration of domestic assault significantly more criminal when committed by a man than when committed by a woman. That finding was robust across European Americans, African Americans, & Latinos & was expressed by both genders. Also, European American participants expressed significantly more criticism toward African American perpetrators of assault than they did toward European American & Latino perpetrators of the exact offense, suggestive of racial bias consistent with stereotypes about African Americans being excessively aggressive. Finally, Latino participants expressed significantly more sympathy toward women who assault their husbands than toward assaulting husbands. Implications of the findings are discussed. 6 Figures, 42 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=The+Influence+of+Gender+and+Ethnicity+on+Judgments+of+Culpability+in+a+Domestic+Violence+Scenario&amp;title=Violence+and+Victims&amp;issn=08866708&amp;date=2004-04-01&amp;volume=19&amp;issue=2&amp;spage=203&amp;author=Ferguson%2C+Christopher+J%3BNegy%2C+Charles

Rethinking Domestic Violence in Theory and Practice

Author: Chancer, Lynn S11 Dept Sociology & Anthropology, Fordham U, Bronx, NY chancer@fordham.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60484607?accountid=14925

Abstract: Drawing on the author's previous work, this article suggests that conceptual advantages result from envisioning domestic violence on a larger continuum of "normalized" to "extreme" sadomasochistic interactions (including gendered interactions that can also range from "ordinary" to "deviant" in how they are perceived). Thereafter, it may be harder to ignore how redressing social inequities involving gender as well as racial & class imbalances can amount, at least in terms of prevention, to anti-domestic-violence measures of one important kind. More concretely, proceeding from the assumption that domestic violence remains disturbingly common (even though its exact scope is difficult to ascertain), this article contrasts how this social problem would best be approached in theory & how it often continues to be dealt with in practice. In making this comparison, a cursory review of recent policy developments in this area, from mandatory arrest policies & laws (& the criticisms these have engendered) to more recent interest in restorative justice & collaborative empowerment, is presented & incorporated into the paper's larger argument. 32 References. Adapted from the source document.

Dissatisfaction with Relationship Power and Dating Violence Perpetration by Men and Women

Author: Kaura, Shelby A1; Allen, Craig M1 Dept Human Development & Family Studies, Iowa State U, Ames

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60484666?accountid=14925

Abstract: This study focuses on the relationship between an individual's dissatisfaction with the level of power they have in their dating relationships, parental violence they experienced during their childhoods, & their dating violence perpetration. A sample of 352 male & 296 female undergraduate college students completed a dating violence survey, including selected subscales from the Revised Conflict Tactics Scales (CTS2) & the Relationship Power Scale. Findings show that relationship power dissatisfaction is associated with the use of violence in dating relationships for both men & women. However, parental violence emerged as an even stronger predictor of dating violence perpetration. Findings also indicate that male perpetration of dating violence is related to mother's violence, whereas female perpetration of dating violence is related to father's violence. The results indicate the importance of including gender in the study of dating violence perpetration. 3 Tables, 35 References. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Inc., copyright 2004.]

Conjoint Interventions for Adult Victims and Children of Domestic Violence: A Program Evaluation

Author: Sullivan, Michael1; Egan, Marcia; Gooch, Michael1 U Tennessee

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61507858?accountid=14925

Abstract: This study documented the evaluation of a 9-week group intervention program designed to address the needs of both abused mothers & their children who have witnessed violence. The intervention was designed to increase parenting skills, address the needs of both parents & children regarding increasing coping abilities & safety planning skills, & decrease the effects of postviolence stress. Parents & children were divided into groups based on clinical cutoff scores for analysis. Overall, the group intervention was effective in reducing blame & trauma symptoms. Parents generally perceived the intervention more helpful for their children than for themselves, but scores indicated that parents' feelings of isolation, stress levels, & health problems decreased significantly at posttest. Practice implications suggest this group intervention is most effective with children who enter the program with behavior scores above the clinical cutoff scores. 3 Tables, 34 References. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Inc., copyright 2004.]

Understanding the Impact of Intimate Partner Violence Mandatory Reporting Law

Author: Bledsoe, Linda K1; Yankeelov, Pamela A; Barbee, Anita P; Antle, Becky F1 Kent School Social Work, U Louisville

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61557429?accountid=14925

Abstract: Intimate partner violence is a pervasive, complex problem affecting the lives & health of a large percentage of women & children. This report provides data from an evaluation designed to address questions & concerns expressed about possible unintended effects of the mandatory reporting law on victims. Case reviews were completed on 631 adult protective service cases resulting from reports of intimate partner violence. Results indicate that following a report of suspected intimate partner violence made under Kentucky mandatory reporting law, all victims who could be located received at a minimum safety planning & information about community resources. Regardless of official case status, nearly half of all cases received services. 3 Tables, 1 Appendix, 24 References. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Inc., copyright 2004.]

Relationship Violence and Women's Reactions to Male- and Female-Controlled HIV Prevention Methods

Author: Saul, Janet1; Moore, Janet; Murphy, Sheila T; Miller, Lynn C1 Division Violence Prevention, National Center Injury Prevention & Control, Centers Disease Control & Prevention, Atlanta, GA jsaul@cdc.gov

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61306157?accountid=14925

Abstract: This study examined the association of relationship violence & preference for three HIV prevention methods among 104 African American & Hispanic women who were at some risk for heterosexual transmission of HIV & other sexually transmitted diseases (STDs). Women completed a brief questionnaire on sexual behaviors & history of relationship violence. All women then watched a video describing three HIV/STD prevention methods (male condoms, female condoms, & vaginal spermicide) that included a discussion of method effectiveness, how to use each method, & their benefits & limitations. Participants then completed a questionnaire assessing their reactions to each of the three HIV prevention methods discussed in the video. Women in violent relationships indicated less likelihood of using male condoms & greater likelihood of using female-controlled methods, particularly vaginal spermicide, than women in nonviolent relationships. In addition, a higher percentage of women in violent compared to nonviolent relationships expected their partners to prefer the vaginal spermicide & a lower percentage expected partners to prefer male condoms. These data suggest that the current focus on finding alternative HIV prevention methods for women in violent relationships is warranted & that a vaginal microbicidal product may be the preferred alternative for this group of women & their male partners. 3 Tables, 29 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=Relationship+Violence+and+Women%27s+Reactions+to+Male-+and+Female-Controlled+HIV+Prevention+Methods&amp;title=AIDS+and+Behavior&amp;issn=10907165&amp;date=2004-06-01&amp;volume=8&amp;issue=2&amp;spage=207&amp;author=Saul%2C+Janet%3BMoore%2C+Janet%3BMurphy%2C+Sheila+T%3BMiller%2C+Lynn+C

From Respite to Transition: Women's Use of Domestic Violence Shelters in Rural New Mexico

Author: Krishnan, Satya P1; Hilbert, Judith C; McNeil, Keith; Newman, Isadore1 Dept Health Science, New Mexico State U, Las Cruces

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61518336?accountid=14925

Abstract: Women who flee violence in their intimate relationships often seek sanctuary at domestic violence shelters. Some return to their abusers, whereas others transition to separate living. This study used descriptive & cluster analyses to examine the differential needs of women who indicated they intended to return to their abusive partners vs those who did not. On the basis of the differences between these two groups, a preliminary model of prediction variables was developed to better understand the needs of those who do not specify their future plans after leaving the shelter. Findings revealed differences in the domains of mental health & help-seeking behaviors. Discussion focuses on the meaning & purpose of the shelter stay (ie, for respite or transition) & the role of shleters & their ideology in the decisions made by shelter staff & clients. 3 Tables, 3 Figures, 36 References. Domestic Violence in Puerto Rican Gay Male Couples: Perceived Prevalence, Intergenerational Violence, Addictive Behaviors, and Conflict Resolution Skills

Author: Toro-Alfonso, Jose1; Rodriguez-Madera, Sheilla1 CUSEP/UPR, San Juan

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61484481?accountid=14925

Abstract: Domestic violence (DV) is a pattern of behaviors in the context of an intimate relationship that can be manifested in emotional, physical, or sexual abuse. DV currently represents a social & a public health issue. This study is an effort to foster a better understanding of DV among same-sex couples. In it, the authors included the participation of 199 Puerto Rican gay males to identify prevalence of DV, violence in their family of origin, participants' addictive behaviors & exposure to violence at childhood, & their conflict resolution skills. Participants were relatively young, highly educated Puerto Rican gay men who reported a high level of domestic violence in their relationships. This violence was identified as emotional violence by 48% of the participants. This sample reported high levels of violence among their families of origin & low conflict resolution skills. 4 Tables, 3 Figures, 51 References. Abstract: This article explores the authors' practice observations of female domestic violence survivors' journey from first agency contact to active participation in a support group process. Trauma Symptoms and Life Skill Needs of Domestic Violence Victims

Author: Gorde, Mrugaya W1; Helfrich, Christine A; Finlayson, Marcia L1 Psycho Social Rehabilitation Program, Lutheran Social Services Illinois, Chicago

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61559100?accountid=14925

Abstract: This study identified the trauma symptoms & life skill needs of 84 domestic violence victims from three domestic violence programs. Women completed two self-report tools: Trauma Symptom Inventory (TSI) & Occupational Self Assessment (OSA). Staff members participated in focus groups regarding their perceptions of the women's needs. Women scored within the clinical range on the Defensive Avoidance (39.8%), Intrusive Experiences (30.1%), & Tension Reduction Behavior (24.1%) clinical scales of the TSI. On the OSA, the groups' priorities differed although all demonstrated a desire to function more independently. Their mental health functioning & prioritization of needs differed based on their level of involvement with the service delivery system. Staff members believe women lack skills in the areas of money management, seeking & obtaining employment, locating permanent housing, independently completing self-care & home management activities, managing stress, & parenting. The findings indicate that both mental health & life skills needs must be addressed. 2 Tables, 40 References. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Inc., copyright 2004.]

Battered and on Welfare: The Experiences of Women with the Family Violence Option

Author: Postmus, Judy L11 School Social Welfare, U Kansas, Lawrence postmus@ku.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60499754?accountid=14925

Abstract: Noting the incidence of battered women on welfare, lawmakers passed the Family Violence Option (FVO), which allows states to offer waivers from welfare program requirements. Assumptions were made that many women would seek relief under the FVO. However, reports indicate that less than 5% of welfare recipients are receiving waivers. This paper presents the findings from a qualitative study that sought to explore the experiences of 29 battered women with the welfare system & the FVO in NY. Their experiences suggest that changes in FVO screening process are necessary to fully implement the program in the way legislators intended. 20 References. Adapted from the source document.

Domestic Violence: Predictors of Victim Support for Official Action

Author: Kingsnorth, Rodney F1; Macintosh, Randall C1 California State U, Sacramento kingsnorth@csus.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60529698?accountid=14925

Abstract: In this paper, using a sample of 5,272 domestic violence cases processed through the Sacramento County District Attorney's Office between 1 July 1999 & 31 Dec 2000, we analyze the predictors of victim support for official action. We test the impact of victim & offender characteristics, situational variables, & official behavior on whether it was the victim or some third party who called for assistance, whether the victim desired the arrest, & whether the victim was willing to prosecute. The multiple significant effects found include race/ethnicity, gender, cohabitation, co-parenthood, attack severity, victim injury, prior incidents, reporting of prior incidents, presence of a protective order, provision of victim services, victim substance use, & suspect injury. Especially important from a policy perspective is that prosecutor charging practice (ie, filing as a felony rather than a misdemeanor or violation of probation) was negatively associated with levels of victim support for prosecution. 2 Tables, 1 Figure, 62 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=Domestic+Violence%3A+Predictors+of+Victim+Support+for+Official+Action&amp;title=Justice+Quarterly&amp;issn=07418825&amp;date=2004-06-01&amp;volume=21&amp;issue=2&amp;spage=301&amp;author=Kingsnorth%2C+Rodney+F%3BMacintosh%2C+Randall+C

Characteristics of Female Perpetrators of Domestic Violence in Group Therapy

Author: Schroffel, Alan

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61305913?accountid=14925

Abstract: This report is based on a set of clinical observations that identified unique characteristics of 12 female perpetrators of domestic violence in court ordered 52-week group treatment. The observations are primarily based on the experiences of two therapists who treated a group of 12 female participants, concurrent with two male groups, each of which consisted of 10 participants. A number of comparative clinical observations are made in relation to the treatment of these male & female perpetrators. The group leaders identified three characteristics specific to female perpetrators of domestic violence: compulsive & premature disclosure by more than half the participants in the woman's group, vs minimal or deferred disclosure in the men's groups; perception of self as perpetrator & ambivalent perception of self as victim in the women's group, vs perception of self as either victim or perpetrator in the men's groups; & devaluation of self in the women's group, vs devaluation of the partner in the men's groups. The group leaders also observed that the perceptions & attitudes of the younger, poorer, less well-educated participants often conflicted with those of their more affluent counterparts. Therapists who treat mandated perpetrators of domestic violence in groups must contend with direct expressions of hostility. They must also help clients recognize & understand reasons for their dysfunctional partnerships & begin to think about how they can develop healthier relationships. The clinical observations from this group strongly suggest that psychological & socioeconomic factors interact to significantly influence treatment process & outcomes. 22 References. Adapted from the source document.

Making Sociological Sense out of Trends in Intimate Partner Violence: The Social Structure of Violence against Women

Author: Michalski, Joseph H11 King's University Coll, U Western Ontario

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60495646?accountid=14925

Abstract: Despite evidence of a cultural shift in orientations toward violence, the problem of violence against women persists across a range of different societies. The preoccupation with the psychology of violence & the focus on cultural orientations obscure the more salient features of social life that promote violence: the structure of interpersonal relationships. The exploration of sociodemographic correlates & the search for "risk factors" of intimate partner violence have overshadowed the inclusion of a distinct set of social structural characteristics that are conducive to domestic violence. The current article draws on comparative research & Donald Black's theoretical approach to argue that key factors include (a) the degree of social isolation, (b) interdependence of support networks, (c) inequality, (d) relational distance, (e) centralization of authority, & (f) exposure to violent networks. The weak explanatory power of previous research can be improved by developing measures to evaluate an integrated structural model of violence against women. 88 References. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Inc., copyright 2004.]

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=Making+Sociological+Sense+out+of+Trends+in+Intimate+Partner+Violence%3A+The+Social+Structure+of+Violence+against+Women&amp;title=Violence+Against+Women&amp;issn=10778012&amp;date=2004-06-01&amp;volume=10&amp;issue=6&amp;spage=652&amp;author=Michalski%2C+Joseph+H

Acculturation, Gender Stereotypes, and Attitudes about Dating Violence among Latino Youth

Author: Ulloa, Emilio C1; Jaycox, Lisa H; Marshall, Grant N; Collins, Rebecca L1 San Diego State U, CA eulloa@sciences.sdsu.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60529336?accountid=14925

Abstract: This study examined the relationship between personal characteristics (gender, acculturation, belief in gender stereotypes, recent dating experiences), & attitudes & knowledge about dating violence in urban Latino youth (N 678). All participants completed self-administered surveys at school. Relative to girls, boys held more problematic (proviolence) attitudes about dating violence & reported less knowledge about dating violence & its consequences. Teens who were more traditional (less acculturated), those who endorsed gender stereotypes, & those who reported recent fearful dating experiences tended to report less knowledge about abuse & lower endorsement of nonviolent attitudes. Multivariate analyses revealed that all four personal variables predicted dating violence knowledge. By contrast, attitudes were predicted by endorsement of gender stereotypes only, or gender stereotypes & gender. Implications for dating violence interventions & future directions for research are explored. 3 Tables, 50 References. Adapted from the source document.

The Use of Temporary Restraining Orders (TROs) As a Strategy to Address Intimate Partner Violence

Author: Kanuha, Valli Kalei1; Ross, Martha L1 School Social Work, U Hawaii, Honolulu kanuha@hawaii.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61543138?accountid=14925

Abstract: One of the most prominent interventions developed to protect victims in domestic abuse cases is the temporary restraining order or "TRO," the purpose of which is to restrain an alleged defendant from further harassment or abuse against a plaintiff. This report analyzes 397 restraining order petitions, & subsequent arrest & conviction data for TRO defendants from 1996 to 1998. Findings confirm that TRO petitioners report many types of abuse & while half of TRO defendants do not re-offend, a small percent of defendants are responsible for multiple police reports, arrests, & criminal violations. Outcome data are discussed for program & policy implications. 7 Tables, 30 References. Adapted from the source document.

Domestic Violence Victims as Homicide Offenders: A Study of Gender Ideology in the Japanese Criminal Justice System

Author: Takamatsu, Kana11 Graduate School of Frontier Science, University of Tokyo, Japan

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/810426111?accountid=14925

Abstract: This study attempts to identify the biases and assumptions reflected in intra-household gender roles, to relate these roles to homicide cases in Japan where the wife kills the husband, and finally, to examine how women criminal offenders are ideologically treated. From case studies, it is clear that women criminal offenders are considered through biases and assumptions within the criminal justice system. If the character of the offender mirrors this prevalent image, she tends to not be punished strictly by the criminal justice system. However, if the character of the offender goes against the image of women, she tends to be punished strictly. Their personality as women is taken into account to a greater extent than the actual crime. In addition, the backgrounds and reasons for female homicide cases-in this case domestic violence-were not considered serious crimes, particularly until the Law on the Prevention of Spouse Violence and Protection of Victims was passed. Achieving gender equality in a society is crucial to not only reduce the number of victims of domestic violence, but to also give a fair judgment (not necessarily a light punishment) to women criminal offenders.

Is Domestic Violence a Gender Issue? Views from a British City

Author: Nicolson, Paula1; Wilson, Richard1 School Health & Related Research, U Sheffield, UK p.nicolson@sheffield.ac.uk

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60527646?accountid=14925

Abstract: Domestic violence involves the abuse, whether emotional, physical, sexual &/or economic, of one or more family (or ex-family) members by another family member within the home or wider domestic context. It is typically associated with increasing entrapment, injury, medical complaints, psycho-social problems & (sometimes) unsuccessful help-seeking. Research evidence demonstrates that domestic abuse is prolific, prolonged, impacts physically, emotionally & economically upon women & children in particular, over the long-term including well after they are safe from the perpetrator. Furthermore, such abuse is widespread & happens all over the world. The findings reported here are from an observational population survey conducted in one British city to assess awareness of domestic violence. While there appears to be a broadly held & well-informed definition of what actions constitute domestic violence & why it occurs, there are striking differences in perspective concerning public awareness of the frequency of domestic violence & knowledge of its importance as a safety issue indicating that it is still not seen generally as a serious social problem. 5 Tables, 70 References. [Copyright 2004 John Wiley and Sons, Ltd.]

Perceptions of Help Resources for Victims of Intimate Partner Violence

Author: Krugman, Scott D1; Witting, Michael D; Furuno, Jon P; Hirshon, Jon Mark; Limcangco, Rhonda; Perisse, Andre R S; Rasch, Elizabeth K1 Dept Pediatrics, Franklin Square Hospital Center

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61528425?accountid=14925

Abstract: Intimate partner violence (IPV) constitutes a major public health problem in the United States. This cross-sectional survey of 108 emergency department (ED) care providers & 146 ED visitors at three metropolitan EDs compared the beliefs of ED health care providers with those of community members about the relative benefits of the helpfulness of resources for IPV victims using hypothetical case scenarios. Although providers generally indicated that help resources were helpful in all scenarios, visitors were more discriminating, showing less support for resources in the lower-risk scenario. Regarding differences between groups, visitors selected police & attorneys more frequently than providers as a helpful resource, whereas providers selected shelters & counselors more frequently than visitors. Adjustment for previous experience with IPV did not change these results. Understanding the differences between health care providers' & community members' perceptions of resources for victims of IPV may improve the effectiveness of referral to IPV resources. 4 Tables, 12 References. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Inc., copyright 2004.]

Abstract: The authors report the results of an evaluation of services provided by 54 Illinois domestic violence agencies. The First Israeli National Survey on Domestic Violence

Author: Eisikovits, Zvi1; Winstok, Zeev; Fishman, Gideon1 Faculty Social Welfare & Health Studies, U Haifa, Israel

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60494870?accountid=14925

Abstract: The aim of the survey reported in this article was to assess the frequency & severity of violence against women in Israel & to identify some major risk factors associated with that violence. During 2000 & 2001, a structured self-report questionnaire was administered to a stratified probability sample drawn from the general population in Israel including 2,544 households, of which 2,092 included only women respondents & 452 included both men & women (904 respondents in total). When compared to those of other Western countries, the rates of psychological aggression in Israel are slightly higher, although the rates of physical aggression are lower. 5 Tables, 26 References. The Extent of Family Violence in Europe. A Comparison of National Surveys

Author: Kury, Helmut1; Obergfell-Fuchs, Joachim; Woessner, Gunda1 Max-Planck-Institute for Foreign and International Criminal Law

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/811125970?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article reviews current victim studies on family violence in Europe and attempts to interpret the variation in the prevalence rates. Methodological shortcomings contribute in part to different rates across countries. The prevalence rates are also linked to preventive and causative factors. Three factors appear to explain the differing prevalence rates in European countries: deprived economic living conditions, traditional attitudes toward women and children, and a general tolerance of violent behavior in a given society. In addition, personal coping may influence whether a family member will act violently against another family member.

Definitional and Methodological Issues Related to Transnational Research on Intimate Partner Violence

Author: Saltzman, Linda E11 Centers for Disease Control and Prevention

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/811125307?accountid=14925

Abstract: To address intimate partner violence (IPV) transnationally, we must examine standardization of both terminology and measurement. This article comments on public health surveillance and efforts toward standardizing terminology on Violence Against Women and IPV. Starting with information about IPV from two U.S. surveys, it identifies issues including cueing and screening questions, survey context, repeat victimization, bounding, and question order to be considered in developing transnational research. Some steps for standardizing IPV measures include replicating initial findings in additional countries, agreeing on breadth of focus, determining strategies for choosing standard measures, and allowing for periodic updates to standardized definitions and measures.
