Interviewer Training in the WHO Multi-Country Study on Women's Health and Domestic Violence

Author: Jansen, Henrica A. F. M.1; Watts, Charlotte; Ellsberg, Mary; Heise, Lori; Garcia-Moreno, Claudia1 Dept Gender & Women's Health, World Health Organization

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61532021?accountid=14925

Abstract: The importance of a sound research strategy for measuring & understanding violence against women cross-culturally is well recognized. However, the value of specialized interviewer training to attain these data is not always fully appreciated. This article describes interviewer selection & training in the World Health Organization (WHO) Multi-Country Study on Women's Health & Domestic Violence & highlights their importance. Such training ensures high-quality data & cross-country comparability, protects the safety of respondents & interviewers, & increases the impact of the study. Moreover, women are not only willing to share experiences with trained & empathetic interviewers but also find the interview a positive experience. 2 Tables, 1 Figure, 11 References. 'Culture' as a Barrier to Service Provision and Delivery: Domestic Violence Services for Minoritized Women

Author: Burman, Erica1; Smailes, Sophie L; Chantler, Khatidja1 Women's Studies Research Centre/Discourse Unit, Dept Psychology & Speech Pathology, Manchester Metropolitan U, UK e.burman@mmu.ac.uk

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61498473?accountid=14925

Abstract: This paper addresses how domestic violence services to women of African, African-Caribbean, South Asian, Jewish & Irish backgrounds are structured by assumptions about 'culture' which produce barriers to the delivery of domestic violence services. Phoenix's (1987, 'Theories of Gender & Black Families', pp. 50-61 in G. Weiner & M. Arnot (eds) Gender Under Scrutiny. London: Hutchinson) discussion of the representation of black women is applied more generally to analyse how discourses of gender & racialization function within accounts of domestic violence service provision. Discourses of both cultural specificity & generality/commonality are shown to intersect to effectively exclude minority ethnic women from such services. Domestic violence emerges as something that can be overlooked or even excused for 'cultural reasons', as a homogenized absence; or alternatively as a pathologized presence, producing heightened visibility of minoritized women both within & outside their communities -- since domestic violence brings them & their communities under particular scrutiny. Such configurations also inform discourses of service provision to minoritized women. Finally key implications are identified for service design, delivery & development, including the need for both culturally specific & mainstream provision around domestic violence, & the need to challenge notions of 'cultural privacy' & 'race anxiety' in work with minoritized communities. 24 References. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Ltd., copyright 2004.]

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=%27Culture%27+as+a+Barrier+to+Service+Provision+and+Delivery%3A+Domestic+Violence+Services+for+Minoritized+Women&amp;title=Critical+Social+Policy&amp;issn=02610183&amp;date=2004-08-01&amp;volume=24&amp;issue=3&amp;spage=332&amp;author=Burman%2C+Erica%3BSmailes%2C+Sophie+L%3BChantler%2C+Khatidja

Changes in Intimate Partner Violence during Pregnancy

Author: Martin, Sandra L1; Harris-Britt, April; Li, Yun; Moracco, Kathryn E; Kupper, Lawrence L; Campbell, Jacquelyn C1 Dept Maternal & Child Health, U North Carolina, Chapel Hill sandra_martin@unc.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60588963?accountid=14925

Abstract: Women's experiences of partner violence, both before & during pregnancy, are described using a convenience sample of women recruited from prenatal clinics. Included were an "index group" of women who told their clinicians that they had been physically abused during pregnancy, & a "comparison group" of women who told their clinicians that they had not been physically abused during pregnancy (even though later more detailed assessment found that some of these comparison women had experienced such violence). The women averaged 27 years of age, with 83% being high school graduates, 26% being married, & 66% having had previous children. The Conflict Tactics Scales 2 assessed rates of partner violence victimization of the women & their male partners, including psychological aggression, physical assault, & sexual coercion. Injuries also were assessed. Results showed that comparison men were physically assaulted at significantly higher rates than were their female partners, both before & during pregnancy (even though these victimization rates were much lower than those seen among the index couples). Index women experienced higher rates of psychological aggression, physical assault, & sexual coercion than did their male partners, & these women were significantly more likely than their male partners to be injured. Pregnancy onset was associated with significant increases in the rates of psychological aggression among both the index & comparison couples. In addition, the index women experienced a significantly increased rate of sexual violence victimization during pregnancy. However, pregnancy was not associated with significant increases in the rates of physical assault or violence-related injuries among the index or comparison couples. 2 Tables, 56 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=Changes+in+Intimate+Partner+Violence+during+Pregnancy&amp;title=Journal+of+Family+Violence&amp;issn=08857482&amp;date=2004-08-01&amp;volume=19&amp;issue=4&amp;spage=201&amp;author=Martin%2C+Sandra+L%3BHarris-Britt%2C+April%3BLi%2C+Yun%3BMoracco%2C+Kathryn+E%3BKupper%2C+Lawrence+L%3BCampbell%2C+Jacquelyn+C

Pregnancy as a Stimulus for Domestic Violence

Author: Burch, Rebecca L1; Gallup, Gordon G, Jr1 c/o Gallup -- Dept Psychology, State U New York, Albany

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60538747?accountid=14925

Abstract: Data collected from a domestic violence prevention & treatment program were analyzed to determine the relationship between female reproductive status & violent incidents. Both the frequency & severity of male initiated violence against women were twice as high when they were pregnant. These results are discussed in the context of an evolutionary perspective on domestic violence. 42 References. Adapted from the source document.

Alcohol and Violence Related Cognitive Risk Factors Associated with the Perpetration of Intimate Partner Violence

Author: Field, Craig A1; Caetano, Raul; Nelson, Scott1 School Public Health, U Texas, Houston

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60552248?accountid=14925

Abstract: The primary aim of this study is to determine the association between alcohol, violence-related cognitive risk factors, & impulsivity with the perpetration of partner violence among current drinkers. A probability sample (n = 1468) of White, Black, & Hispanic couples 18 years of age or older in the US household population was interviewed in 1995 with a response rate of 85%. The risk factors of interest included the alcohol & violence related cognitions of approval of marital aggression, alcohol as an excuse for misbehavior, & aggressive expectations following alcohol consumption as well as impulsivity. In all, 15% (216/1468) of the respondents reported perpetration of domestic violence. In addition, 24% (7/29) of those who approved of marital violence, 11% (126/1163) of those who reported alcohol use as an excuse for misbehavior, 10% (128/1257) of those who reported aggressive expectations following alcohol consumption, & 14% (99/716) of those who reported impulsivity also reported perpetration of domestic violence. Bivariate analysis indicated that all of the cognitive risk factors were significantly more common in those who reported perpetration of intimate partner violence (IPV) (p < 0.05). However, multivariate analysis controlling for ethnicity, education, income, age, gender, & impulsivity indicated that those who reported strong or very strong expectations of aggressive behavior following alcohol consumption were 3.2 (95% CI = 1.3-7.9) times more likely to perpetrate IPV. Although all of the alcohol & violence related cognitive risk factors were associated with the perpetration of domestic violence, expectations of aggressive behavior following alcohol consumption appeared to be the strongest predictor of the perpetration of IPV among current drinkers. Therefore, alcohol expectancy may be an important factor to assess when attempting to identify & treat perpetrators of domestic violence who are also current drinkers. 3 Tables, 23 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=Alcohol+and+Violence+Related+Cognitive+Risk+Factors+Associated+with+the+Perpetration+of+Intimate+Partner+Violence&amp;title=Journal+of+Family+Violence&amp;issn=08857482&amp;date=2004-08-01&amp;volume=19&amp;issue=4&amp;spage=249&amp;author=Field%2C+Craig+A%3BCaetano%2C+Raul%3BNelson%2C+Scott

Partner Abuse, Learned Helplessness, and Trauma Symptoms

Author: Palker-Corell, Ann1; Marcus, David K1 c/o Marcus -- Dept Psychology, Sam Houston State U, Huntsville, TX

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60580449?accountid=14925

Abstract: We examined (a) whether attributional style is related to trauma & depressive symptoms among battered women, & (b) whether women from a battered women's shelter report a more helpless (or hopeless) attributional style than do women from the community. Women from a woman's shelter (n = 49) & a comparison group of women from the community (n = 51) completed measures of attributional style, relationship conflict, & psychological distress. As expected, women from the shelter reported considerably higher levels of conflict & distress. Furthermore, attributional style contributed to the prediction of both depressive & trauma symptoms. However, there was no evidence that battered women's attributional style differed from that of other women. Overall, the results provided some support for the revised learned helplessness model, but they did not support Walker's (2000) contention that partner abuse results in learned helplessness. 3 Tables, 37 References. Representations and Counter-Representations of Domestic Violence on Clydeside between the Two World Wars

Author: Hughes, Annmarie11 Dept Economic & Social History, U Glasgow A.Hughes@arts.gla.ac.uk

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60523865?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article looks at representations in relation to the actual incidence of domestic violence between the wars on Clydeside. It argues that representations of domestic violence used by the law-enforcement & social work agencies were, & still largely are, class & gender based & do not fully depict the experience of violence. Violence was assumed to be aggression by men against women, was signified as working class & was associated with aspects of working-class male popular culture, especially gambling, alcohol consumption, & football mania. Absolving middle-class men from complicity in such behavior, these representations also collectivized men's violence & distracted attention from individual abusers: domestic violence was not seen as assault rather as an unfortunate cultural problem. This gave abusers a self-justification. However, male popular culture did not cause men to beat their wives, nor were women the only sex to be beaten. In fact, the dominant representations of domestic violence were inadequate not only because they laid the blame on male popular culture but because they excluded consideration of middle-class men & women, women whose partners did not take part in male popular culture & the fact that victims were male as well as female. 1 Table. Adapted from the source document.

The Correlation between Race and Domestic Violence Is Confounded with Community Context

Author: Benson, Michael L1; Wooldredge, John; Thistlethwaite, Amy B; Fox, Greer Litton1 Division Criminal Justice, U Cincinnati, OH michael.benson@uc.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60095448?accountid=14925

Abstract: Why do African Americans appear to have a higher likelihood of engaging in domestic violence than whites? To address this question, we draw on insights from social disorganization theory & recent research on urban poverty. We argue that the apparent correlation between race & domestic violence is confounded with the different ecological contexts typically occupied by African Americans & whites. Using data from wave 2 of the National Survey of Families & Households & the 1990 US census, we show the following conclusions: (1) rates of domestic violence for both African Americans & whites vary systematically by type of community; (2) the correlation between race & domestic violence is substantially reduced or disappears altogether when whites are compared to African Americans in similar ecological contexts; & (3) individual-level risk factors for domestic violence appear to operate similarly for both African Americans & whites. 5 Tables, 64 References. Adapted from the source document.

Canadian Domestic Violence Policy and Indian Immigrant Women

Author: Shirwadkar, Swati11 Dept Sociology, U Pune, India

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60580133?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article explores the problems of Indian immigrant women who face cultural constraints in accessing the benefits of Canadian policies for domestically abused women. Findings from an exploratory study of abused immigrant Indian women & community social workers in Ontario, Canada, are presented. They expose the pressures of cultural, social, & family ties that prevent these women from getting necessary help for domestic violence. The limitations of Canadian policies & programs for these women & the means to improve their access to these policies & programs are discussed. The conclusion suggests how binational research is needed to improve the situation of abused Indian immigrant women in Canada & in India. 61 References. Author: Cousineau, Marie-Marthe1; Rondeau, Gilles1 School Criminology, U Montreal

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60535090?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article summarizes issues & priorities for prevalence surveys & intervention studies raised by researchers & practitioners at an international symposium on transnational & cross-cultural research on family violence, convened near Montreal, Canada, in June 2003. The major conceptual questions were What is meant by transnational or cross-cultural research? & What should be included in the definition of family violence? The Social Networks of Women Experiencing Domestic Violence

Author: Levendosky, Alytia A1; Bogat, G Anne; Theran, Sally A; Trotter, Jennifer S; Von Eye, Alexander; Davidson, William S, II1 Michigan State U

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61555280?accountid=14925

Abstract: The research literature has demonstrated that battered women living in shelters experience impaired social support. This study examines this phenomenon among battered women living in the community. This study compared a group of pregnant battered women (n=145) & a group of pregnant nonbattered women (n=58) in terms of their structural [eg, total number of supporters, network members in violent relationships with their partners (an index of homophily or nonhomophily]) & functional [eg, emotional)] social support. Four reasons for impaired support in battered women were proposed & examined, including social isolation, failure to disclose abuse, homophily, & low SES. Only homophily was related to impaired support among battered women. In addition, the relationship between structural & functional support & mental health outcomes (eg, depression, self-esteem) was examined. Criticism, practical support, homophily, & disclosure were all significant predictors of mental health for battered women. Implications for community-based interventions are discussed in the context of current intervention strategies with battered women. 4 Tables, 2 Figures, 66 References. Adapted from the source document.

Filipino Men's Familial Roles and Domestic Violence: Implications and Strategies for Community-Based Intervention

Author: Lee, Romeo B11 Behavioral Sciences Dept, De La Salle U, Manila, Philippines leer@dlsu.edu.ph

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61559838?accountid=14925

Abstract: Men's gender roles have contributed to family violence, but the ramifications of these roles in the development of community-based programs for men have not been given much attention. A small-scale qualitative examination of the familial context of Filipino men's positions & roles, & their domestic violence experiences & attitudes was carried out using eight discussion groups, each group with seven to eight members. Verbatim tape-recorded transcripts were analyzed using accepted techniques for theoretical analysis to establish emergent themes. Discussants saw themselves as being at the helm of their families. Men were knowledgeable of & took responsibility for their gender roles exerting control over the focus & direction of all their family affairs, including the gender roles of their wives/partners. This control demonstrated facets of their hegemonic masculinity such as sexual objectification & dominance. Men in this society come from a traditional position of power, dominance, & privilege. They will be particularly sensitive to interventions aimed at reducing violence against women that will enquire into their private lives. In their view, such interventions were both a direct challenge to their family leadership & a basis for 'losing face'. Strategies for positive interventions include the need for male-sensitive & male-centered approaches that avoid demonizing or stereotyping men. 1 Box, 32 References. Adapted from the source document.

When Terror Strikes at Home: The Interface between Religion and Domestic Violence

Author: Nason-Clark, Nancy11 Dept Sociology, U New Brunswick, Fredericton nasoncla@unb.ca

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60522893?accountid=14925

Abstract: Several aspects of the relationship between religion & domestic violence are examined. The extent of domestic violence experienced by women in "religious families" & the difficulties of providing assistance to such women are discussed. It is asserted that the Judeo-Christian notions of forgiveness & reconciliation may actually contribute to women's experience of domestic violence because such concepts increase the vulnerability of abused women. The extent to which faith-based batterer intervention programs can reduce domestic violence is acknowledged; however, it is stressed that such approaches must utilize both religious & secular resources to effectively curb abusive behavior. Several recommendations for future research are offered, eg, identifying aspects of religious traditions that discourage efforts to raise a community's awareness of domestic violence & determining how issues of race & class intersect with domestic violence in religious families. 35 References. J. W. Parker

A Positive Domestic Violence Screen Predicts Future Domestic Violence

Author: Houry, Debra1; Feldhaus, Kim; Peery, Benjamin; Abbott, Jean; Lowenstien, Steven R; al-Bataa-de-Montero, Sameerah; Levine, Saul1 Center Injury Control, Emory U

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60531323?accountid=14925

Abstract: The objective of this study was to determine if a brief screen for domestic violence (DV) predicts future violence. We conducted a cohort study of adult women who presented to an inner-city emergency department during an 8-week study period. Participants were screened for DV using the Partner Violence Screen (PVS). At 4 months, follow-up telephone interviews were conducted: rates of verbal & physical violence were measured using the modified Conflict Tactics Scale. Relative risks of violent events (physical & verbal) were calculated. Of the 215 women who enrolled, 36 (16%) had an initial screen positive for DV. Ninety-six women participated in the follow-up phase; of these women, 9% had screened positive for DV. At 4 months, women with DV were 11.3 times more likely to experience physical violence & 7.3 times more likely to experience verbal aggression. The study's screen identified women at high risk for subsequent physical violence & verbal aggression. 4 Tables, 1 Figure, 15 References. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Inc., copyright 2004.]

Is Domestic Violence Relevant? An Exploratory Analysis of Couples Referred for Mediation in Family Court

Author: Tishler, Carl L1; Bartholomae, Suzanne; Katz, Bonnie L; Landry-Meyer, Laura1 Ohio State U, Columbus tishler.1@osu.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61496513?accountid=14925

Abstract: Mediation is an essential component of custody evaluation & reconciliation services in domestic courts. Data from 306 couples with & without a reported history of domestic violence (DV) who were ordered to attend an assessment for mediation were analyzed to determine differences in the mediation process. More than one third reported a history of DV. Chi-square analysis showed that differences in the mediation process exist between couples reporting DV & couples reporting no DV. A greater proportion of couples with reported DV (a) actually attended the court-mandated assessment session, (b) were deemed unsuitable to participate in the mediation process, (c) were in default of child-support payments, & (d) reported drug & alcohol abuse. No significant differences were found between the two groups in the measured mediation outcomes. Implications for the use of mediation with couples who reported DV & recommendations for future research are discussed. 2 Tables, 48 References. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Inc., copyright 2004.]

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=Is+Domestic+Violence+Relevant%3F+An+Exploratory+Analysis+of+Couples+Referred+for+Mediation+in+Family+Court&amp;title=Journal+of+Interpersonal+Violence&amp;issn=08862605&amp;date=2004-09-01&amp;volume=19&amp;issue=9&amp;spage=1042&amp;author=Tishler%2C+Carl+L%3BBartholomae%2C+Suzanne%3BKatz%2C+Bonnie+L%3BLandry-Meyer%2C+Laura

Deaths in the Family: Domestic Violence, Witchcraft Accusations and Political Militancy in Transkei, South Africa, 1904-1965

Author: Redding, Sean11 Dept History, Amherst Coll, MA sredding@amherst.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60573979?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article discusses the intimate causes of violence within the family in the Transkei by analyzing specific court cases that illustrate the significance of common beliefs in supernatural powers in framing domestic disputes. The belief in women's potential for violence, both directly through physical means, but also indirectly through the use of supernatural powers, was the foundation for many familial disputes. People used witchcraft allegations & violence to isolate & eradicate causes of social disruption & to punish instigators. Although the argument mostly concerns the intimacy of familial or domestic violence, it indicates also how witchcraft accusations in the late 1950s & early 1960s crossed over into the realm of politics in an analogous attempt to isolate & remedy the supernatural causes of the broader social & economic problems created by state policies. People turned to both witchcraft accusations & violence against state officials in the political unrest that emerged during those years. Adapted from the source document.

Welfare Reform, Domestic Violence, and Employment. What Do We Know and What Do We Need to Know?

Author: Riger, Stephanie1; Staggs, Susan L1 University of Illinois at Chicago

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/810994715?accountid=14925

Abstract: Welfare reforms enacted in 1996 require that recipients work to receive benefits. Advocates for battered women feared that abusive men would escalate violence in response to independence brought to women by employment. Yet research on employment, domestic violence, and welfare reform has yielded mixed findings. The authors review those findings and propose that a context-sensitive social-psychological perspective identifying relevant dimensions of culture and measuring partner's employment may be more useful than a focus only on characteristics of women. Findings suggest that inclusion of generous work supportsin welfare legislation may help reducepoverty and domestic violence.

"Family Violence": The Language of Legitimacy

Author: Hammons, Stacy A11 Dept Sociology & Anthropology, Fresno Pacific U, CA shammons@fresno.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60499948?accountid=14925

Abstract: Examinations of social movements have typically overlooked the organizational context of social-movement agencies as well as the role of agency employees in shaping the larger movement. This study explored meso-& micro-level forces that affect the battered women's movement, as found in one particular women's shelter. The findings suggest that although the larger social movement may encourage the examination of battering from a systemic perspective, shelter workers adopt a more narrow, psychological interpretation. Shelter workers adopt this perspective because of the socialization that occurs within the shelter context itself, which employees then replicate to be seen as competent. 36 References. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Inc., copyright 2004.]

Domestic Violence in the Orthodox Jewish Home: A Value-Sensitive Approach to Recovery

Author: Grodner, Elana; Sweifach, Jay

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61490485?accountid=14925

Abstract: Domestic violence, a once-hidden problem in the Orthodox Jewish community, is now acknowledged to occur in Orthodox Jewish families. Because this problem was not affirmed earlier, effective therapeutic techniques for working with battered Orthodox women have only recently begun to emerge. This article highlights one therapeutic technique -- the value-sensitive approach -- which uses ethnocultural-religious factors as part of the therapeutic process & has generated positive results in working with battered Orthodox women. 14 References. Dual Arrest Decisions in Domestic Violence Cases: The Influence of Departmental Policies

Author: Finn, Mary A1; Blackwell, Brenda Sims; Stalans, Loretta J; Studdard, Sheila; Dugan, Laura1 Dept Criminal Justice, Georgia State U

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60525442?accountid=14925

Abstract: Dual arrests in family violence cases have increased following passage of proarrest laws. This study examined the relationship between officers' perceptions of their departmental policies & arrest outcomes. Each officer was given 1 of 6 hypothetical scripts that varied as to whether the wife only was injured or the wife & husband were injured. They were then asked what action they would take. Results indicate that officers who believed that their department would support arrest of both parties are more likely to arrest both parties. Officers who perceived that their department encouraged arrest of the primary aggressor only are more likely to arrest the husband only. When both parties were injured, officers were likely to arrest both. Experienced officers were more likely than novice officers to use dual arrest. Implications for the importance of primary aggressor language are discussed. 4 Tables, 59 References. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Inc., copyright 2004.]

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=Dual+Arrest+Decisions+in+Domestic+Violence+Cases%3A+The+Influence+of+Departmental+Policies&amp;title=Crime+%26+Delinquency&amp;issn=00111287&amp;date=2004-10-01&amp;volume=50&amp;issue=4&amp;spage=565&amp;author=Finn%2C+Mary+A%3BBlackwell%2C+Brenda+Sims%3BStalans%2C+Loretta+J%3BStuddard%2C+Sheila%3BDugan%2C+Laura

Accountbility for Change: Solution-Focused Treatment with Domestic Violence Offenders

Author: Lee, Mo Yee1; Uken, Adriana; Sebold, John1 Coll Social Work, Ohio State U, Columbus lee.355@osu.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61551245?accountid=14925

Abstract: In this article, the authors discuss & evaluate a solution-focused treatment program for domestic violence offenders. Building on a strengths perspective, a solution-focused approach holds a person accountable for solutions instead of focusing on problems. The outcome study was a 1-group pre & posttest design with a 6-month follow-up to evaluate the effectiveness of a solution-focused group treatment program for 90 domestic violence offenders who were ordered by the court to receive treatment. Findings of the outcome study indicated a recidivism rate of 16.7% of program participants as based on official records over a 6-year period. There was a significant improvement in participants' relational skills in intimate relationships as evaluated by their spouses or partners & a significant increase in their self-esteem based on self-reports. Implications for treatment & research with domestic violence offenders are discussed. 6 Tables, 1 Figure, 64 References. Adapted from the source document.

Components of an Aboriginal-Based Family Violence Intervention Program

Author: Brown, Jason1; Languedoc, Sue1 Dept Family Social Sciences, U Manitoba, Winnipeg jason_brown@umanitoba.ca

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61522084?accountid=14925

Abstract: The purpose of the study was to describe the essential elements of an Aboriginal-based family violence intervention program. Twenty-one Aboriginal family violence program administrators & service providers were asked "What program components are essential in an Aboriginal family violence prevention program?" Respondents identified the need for sound administrative structure & function, qualified & healthy staff, & consistent program funding. Coordinated service delivery with other community agencies was described. Program components should be based on traditional teachings, should create awareness of personal & family dynamics & change, & should educate about family violence. There were needs for components for partners seperately, partners & their children, partners together, & the whole family. These components coincided with Aboriginal traditional teachings. Implications for program development were described. 1 Table, 1 Figure, 25 References. Adapted from the source document.

A Postcolonial Women's Law? Domestic Violence and the Ontario Liquor Board's "Indian List," 1950-1990

Author: Valverde, Mariana

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/59978708?accountid=14925

Abstract: The 1920's Prohibition era discourse linked drinking with domestic violence. As a supposed legacy of Prohibition, the Ontario Liquor Board kept a list from the 1930s-1990s of troublesome alcoholics, mainly referred to the list by abused women, that put a police watch on the men listed & prohibited their liquor purchases at bars & stores. This list was dubbed the "Indian list", demonstrating the original, postcolonial intent to separate First Americans from whites, & the continuation of prior race-specific liquor prohibitions. White & Indian cases on the interdiction list are described; 17 out of 154 concern women & some involve seniors in old age homes. This emphasis on alcohol in this legal tool shows how early feminism attempted to govern domestic abuse with the removal of alcohol, not by criminalizing domestic violence. M. Pflum

Coping among Adult Female Victims of Domestic Violence

Author: Waldrop, Angela E1; Resick, Patricia A1 Dept Psychiatry, Medical U South Carolina, Charleston waldrop@musc.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60544695?accountid=14925

Abstract: This paper examines the current literature regarding coping among battered women. It considers a number of contextual factors that are related to women's choices in coping with partner abuse, including factors related to the relationship (eg, frequency & severity of abuse, length of relationship) & women's resources (eg, social support, financial resources). Relationships between different forms of coping & psychological outcomes are also examined. As the research in domestic violence coping is somewhat lacking in coherence, theories & models from the broader coping literature are used to organize the findings from the domestic violence field. Methodological concerns, such as research methods, measurement issues, & sampling, are raised throughout the paper. Suggestions for future research are made. 48 References. Adapted from the source document.

Monsters and victims: Male felons' accounts of intimate partner violence

Author: Wood, Julia T11 University of North Carolina, Chapel Hill

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/811184007?accountid=14925

Abstract: Previous research on men who commit intimate partner violence has addressed two questions: (1) how can researchers and clinicians classify men's accounts of intimate partner violence?, and (2) do men who engage in intimate partner violence subscribe to particular codes of manhood? The present study linked these two questions by asking how men account for their own intimate partner violence and how their accounts draw upon understandings of manhood. Grounded theory analysis of interviews with 22 incarcerated men identified three categories of themes in participants' accounts of intimate partner violence: justifications ('she disrespected me as a man; ''a man has a right to control his woman; ''she provoked me; ''she took it'); dissociations ('I am not the abusive type; ''my violence was limited, and abusers don't limit their abuse'); and remorse ('I regret I abused her'). Divergence among these representations of violent men's reasons for violence and previous typologies reflects this study's focus on insiders' views of behavior. Also, the themes that emerged from participants were informed by conflicting, although not wholly independent, codes of manhood that circulate in U. S. cultural life. Attention to violent men's sense-making strategies and contradictory narratives of manhood suggests opportunities for intervention and rehabilitation.

Domestic Violence against Women, and Their Economic Dependence: A Count Data Analysis

Author: Basu, Bharati1; Famoye, Felix1 Dept Economics, Central Michigan U, Mount Pleasant

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60571117?accountid=14925

Abstract: In examining the relationship between violence against women & women's economic dependence, existing literature treats the incidents of violence either as a binary or as a continuous variable. However, the incidents of violence is a count variable &, quite often, data on the number of violent incidents is categorized. This paper estimates the relation between violence against women & economic dependence by using a categorized negative binomial regression model. The model is suitable for categorized count data & thus provides a more accurate estimation of the relation than what is provided in the literature. Data analysis in this paper shows that less economic dependence of women is associated with less violence. 4 Tables, 31 References. Adapted from the source document.

Child Maltreatment in Families Experiencing Domestic Violence

Author: Lee, Li-Ching1; Kotch, Jonathan B; Cox, Christine E1 Dept Epidemiology, Johns Hopkins Bloomberg School Public Health, Baltimore, MD llee2@jhsph.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60513546?accountid=14925

Abstract: This study examined the association between physical domestic violence (PDV) & reported child maltreatment in a cohort of children at risk for maltreatment. Participants were 219 6- to 7-year-old children & their caregivers. PDV was measured by combining caregivers' self-reports & children's reports, while child maltreatment was based on state Division of Social Services Central Registry records. Among 219 child-caregiver pairs studied, 42 (19.2%) had at least one maltreatment report in the 2 years following the interviews. PDV consistently predicted child maltreatment, with adjusted odds ratios ranging from 2.96 to 3.46. In addition, we investigated interactions between PDV & other predictors of child maltreatment. Among Aid to Families with Dependent Children (AFDC) participants, PDV was highly associated with child maltreatment. However, this pattern was not observed among subjects who did not have AFDC. There is an increased incidence of child maltreatment reports in families experiencing PDV. AFDC participation intensified the probability of child maltreatment in the presence of PDV. Findings also suggest that in households experiencing PDV, social supports may protect children from maltreatment. 3 Tables, 2 Figures, 91 References. Adapted from the source document.

Impacts of Agency Coordination on Nonprofit Domestic Violence and Sexual Assualt Programs in Communities with STOP Formula Grant Funding

Author: Zweig, Janine M; Burt, Martha R

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61553851?accountid=14925

Abstract: The goals of the current study are (a) to understand the community & state context in which STOP(Services/Training/Officers/Prosecutors)-funded victim service (VS) programs operate, (b) to assess the degree to which receipt of STOP funding for VS programs & the degree of state-level STOP agency support for collaboration among community agencies have led to improved program services & community interaction, & (c) to assess the degree to which improved interaction between community agencies leads to improvements for VS programs. The results show that community interaction between VS programs & other community agencies can improve VS program services as reported by service providers. In addition, the higher the pre-STOP levels of activity around violence against women issues in communities, the more agencies can enhance their service system with STOP funding. Also, STOP funding has facilitated greater levels of change for communities whose pre-STOP attention to violence against women was lower. 2 Tables, 1 Figure, 10 References. Rethinking Law and Violence: The Domestic Violence (Prevention) Bill in India, 2002

Author: Rajan, Rajeswari Sunder11 English Faculty, Wolfson Coll, U Oxford

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60522981?accountid=14925

Abstract: This essay focuses on the controversy generated by recent proposed legislation on domestic violence in India. An alternative draft bill on domestic violence prepared by the feminist legal nongovernmental organization (NGO), the Lawyers' Collective, & supported by women's groups nationally, includes a demand that victims of domestic violence (usually wives) be permitted by law to continue to occupy the domestic home, a demand that the Government bill has refused to include. This demand is theoretically informed by a politics of space. Bodies & space are linked, to the extent that each is an abstraction without the concept of the other to ground it. The feminist legal proposal challenges property-as-absolute-(male) ownership by conceptualizing the household as, instead, shared domestic space. The proposal does not dissimulate common sense -- it is conscious of being radical, in part at least because it demystifies the 'domestic' as an ideological construct & offers it instead realistically & minimally as simply an alternative to destitution. The recognition that there are no support structures for dependant women outside the family (such as, eg, state-sponsored welfare institutions), so that destitution can be both sudden & real for women of any class & circumstances, has led to the conceptualization of a law that formulates a right to shared space as one that makes no claim to shared ownership -- while at the same time questioning the other's absolute property right. Despite the limited nature of the claim it makes, this proposal has been viewed as threatening by Indian law-makers. 1 Appendix. Adapted from the source document.

Dating Violence Attributions: Do They Differ for In-Group and Out-Group Members Who Have a History of Dating Violence?

Author: Harrison, Lisa A1; Abrishami, Golee1 Dept Psychology, California State U, Sacramento

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60546775?accountid=14925

Abstract: In this research we examined the influence of in-group bias & dating violence history on dating violence attributions. Participants were 113 college students (97 women & 16 men; age M=21.9). They read a vignette that depicted dating violence & then completed a questionnaire concerning the assault. The couple was described as either part of the participants' in-group or the participants' out-group. The dating violence was described as either a first-time event for the couple or a repeated act of violence. Participants formed more lenient attributions for the in-group assailant than for the out-group assailant, but only if he was a first-time assailant rather than a repeat assailant. In addition, participants attributed less blame to the in-group victim than to the out-group victim, but only if she was a repeat victim of dating violence. These findings are examined in relation to in-group bias. 3 Tables, 28 References. Adapted from the source document.

Critiquing the Case for Marriage Promotion: How the Promarriage Movement Misrepresents Domestic Violence Research

Author: Catlett, Beth Skilken1; Artis, Julie E1 Dept Sociology, DePaul U

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61537972?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article argues that governmental policies to promote marriage fail to recognize the reality of domestic violence in many families' lives. By minimizing & misrepresenting the role of domestic violence, these policies ultimately are self-defeating. After describing the recent history of welfare reform & marriage promotion policies, we briefly review the arguments advanced in favor of such policies. We then offer a critique of marriage promotion through the prism of social science research on domestic violence & explain how advocates largely ignore or misrepresent this research. Finally, we discuss the need to ground policy formation in the voices of those women whose lives such policies ultimately will affect. 44 References. When Advocacy for Domestic Violence Victims Backfires: Types and Sources of Victim Disempowerment

Author: McDermott, M Joan1; Garofalo, James1 Center Study Crime/Delinquency/Corrections, Southern Illinois U, Carbondale

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61514198?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article argues that although victim advocacy in domestic violence has brought significant improvement in victim services, there are instances in which advocacy backfires: The very interventions designed to help & empower victims contain the seeds of disempowerment. After discussing types & sources of victim disempowerment from their own research & that of others, the authors draw attention to some macro issues related to domestic violence victim advocacy. 21 References. Social Support and Spirituality as Culturally Relevant Factors in Coping among African American Women Survivors of Partner Abuse

Author: Fowler, Dawnovise N1; Hill, Hope M1 School Social Work, U Texas, Austin

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61433962?accountid=14925

Abstract: This study examined partner abuse, mental health, & coping in a sample of African American women survivors of partner abuse (N = 126). The mediating effects of social support & spirituality, as culturally relevant factors in coping, on the relationships between partner abuse & both depression & posttraumatic stress disorder (PTSD) symptoms were investigated. Findings from hierarchical regression analysis indicated that PTSD symptoms remain significantly related to partner abuse after controlling for the effects of social support & spirituality. Findings point to practice & research implications regarding African American women survivors' mental health & ways of coping. 2 Tables, 57 References. Gender Differences in Adolescent and Young Adult Predictors of Later Intimate Partner Violence

Author: Chen, Ping-Hsin1; White, Helene Raskin1 U Medicine & Dentistry, New Jersey Medical School

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60539540?accountid=14925

Abstract: The authors examined distal predictors of perpetration & victimization of intimate partner violence (IPV) among 725 young adult men & women using prospective data. Earlier problem drinking & negative affect were common predictors of perpetration for both genders. Lower education predicted female perpetration; parental fighting predicted male perpetration. No common predictors of victimization were found for men & women. Lower education, problem drinking, & childhood parental beating predicted female victimization. None of the models explained a large amount of the variance in IPV. More longitudinal research is needed on gender differences in the explanations for & consequences of IPV. 3 Tables, 46 References. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Inc., copyright 2004.]

Insults, Injury, and Injustice: Rethinking State Intervention in Domestic Violence Cases

Author: Stark, Evan11 Rutgers U, Newark

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60548079?accountid=14925

Abstract: Part of a review symposium on Linda G. Mills' (2003) framework for reforming the US criminal justice system's participation in the anti-domestic violence movement. An overview of Mills' critique of the national criminal justice system's involvement in domestic violence cases & suggestion that community-based support programs be used to rectify domestic violence between couples is presented. Various problems with Mills' methodology are highlighted including the exclusion of convincing research or case studies that detail problems with the existing approach from her work. Mills' assertion that she is not a conservative feminist is strongly questioned. Additional difficulties with Mills' alternative framework & empirical contentions are identified; for instance, her assertion that the mandatory arrest of male abusers actually augments African American women's experience of domestic violence is questioned. A possible explanation for the failure of mandatory arrest to reduce the domestic violence faced by African American women is then offered. A comparison of Mills' & S. Das Gupta's (2003) respective critiques of the US criminal justice system's involvement in domestic violence cases is also provided to illustrate how the federal government can remain a participant in the anti-domestic violence movement. J. W. Parker

Race, Poverty, and the Crime-Centered Response to Domestic Violence: A Comment on Linda Mills's Insult to Injury: Rethinking Our Responses to Intimate Abuse

Author: Coker, Donna11 School Law, U Miami

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60504589?accountid=14925

Abstract: Part of a review symposium on Linda G. Mills' (2003) framework for reforming the US criminal justice system's participation in the anti-domestic violence movement. Existing literature that has examined the problems created by a criminal justice approach to reducing domestic violence in the US is reviewed, illustrating that mandatory punishment for perpetrators of domestic violence has not significantly reduced recidivism rates but has augmented state control over battered women. Mills' explanation for the criminal justice system's considerable involvement in domestic violence cases, assertion that battered women who stay in abusive relationships are socially pathologized, & claim that battered women's enactment of emotional or non-violent aggression is equivalent to abusers' physical violence are subsequently explored. A comparison of Mills' programmatic reform, which advocates the use of community-based support program called Intimate Abuse Circles, with an alternative framework that prioritizes restorative justice is conducted, illuminating the shortcomings of Mills' approach & recognizing the advantages of the restorative justice model. 101 References. J. W. Parker

Rethinking Criminal Justice Responses to Intimate Partner Violence

Author: Raphael, Jody11 Coll Law, DePaul U

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61556505?accountid=14925

Abstract: Part of a review symposium on Linda G. Mills' (2003) framework for reforming the US criminal justice system's participation in the anti-domestic violence movement. Several aspects of Mills' work are critiqued or questioned including her contention that couples with histories of domestic violence are equally responsible for such violent behavior, her use of this position to challenge mandatory arrest policies, & her tendency to overlook how domestic violence contributes to women's inability to realize educational or occupational growth. Although Mills' community-based support program approach to reducing domestic violence, called Intimate Abuse Circles, is deemed beneficial for some women, it is asserted that this perspective would not be welcomed by all battered women. Mills' criticism of the criminal justice system's involvement in domestic violence cases is partially accepted; however, it is maintained that some cases will require the system's participation to ensure battered women's safety & that legal resources can be strong agents of cultural change. Alternative approaches for preventing domestic violence in the US are thus offered & recommendations for improving upon Mills' work are suggested. 23 References. J. W. Parker

Domestic Violence and the Politics of Trauma

Author: Humphreys, Cathy1; Joseph, Stephen1 School Health & Social Studies, U Warwick, Coventry, UK

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61551735?accountid=14925

Abstract: Posttraumatic Stress Disorder (PTSD) has had a chequered history in relation to gender-based violence & specifically domestic violence. There is evidence that a significant proportion of women who are abused suffer from PTSD. However, there remains a controversy about whether this is a useful & progressive concept on which to base intervention. Mainstream mental health services in the UK have shown little 'take up', while women's services supporting survivors also remain ambivalent, although for different reasons. Clinical psychology has been where the concept has been embraced. This paper highlights aspects of Judith Herman's (Herman, Judith (1992). Trauma and recovery. New York: Basic Books.) original intervention framework which have remained undeveloped, specifically the attention to social support & the need for an active social movement. These ideas have been marginalized in the development of professional & individualised approaches to survivors. It is suggested that reinvigorating these ideas would enhance the work with survivors & provide greater resonance with the underpinnings of work with women's services. 72 References. [Copyright 2004 Elsevier Ltd.]

Deconstructing the Relationship between Intimate Partner Violence and Sexual HIV Risk among Drug-Involved Men and Their Female Partners

Author: El-Bassel, Nabila1; Gilbert, Louisa; Golder, Seana; Wu, Elwin; Chang, Mingway; Fontdevila, Jorge; Sanders, Glorice1 Social Intervention Group, School Social Work, Columbia U, New York, NY

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61495642?accountid=14925

Abstract: This study, based on data from a random sample of 322 men on methadone, examines whether traditional male gender role beliefs, male substance use, & couple drug-involvement lead to male psychological dominance, which in turn leads to perpetration of intimate partner violence (IPV) & sexual HIV risk behavior. Structural equation modeling indicated that male psychological dominance is directly associated with perpetrating both physical IPV & sexual HIV risk; however, physical IPV did not lead to sexual HIV risk as predicted originally. Stronger endorsement of traditional male gender role beliefs was associated with male psychological dominance. Couple drug-involvement was also directly associated with male psychological dominance as well as sexual HIV risk. Male substance use led to couple drug-involvement, but not to physical or sexual HIV risk as hypothesized. Study findings highlight the significance of couple drug-involvement & male psychological dominance as pathways leading to physical IPV & sexual HIV risk behavior. Implications for HIV prevention efforts targeting drug-involved men & their sexual partners are discussed. 3 Tables, 2 Figures, 60 References. Adapted from the source document.

Voicing the Silent Fear: South Asian Women's Experiences of Domestic Violence

Author: Gill, Aisha11 U Surrey Roehampton

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60527693?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article examines the subjective experiences of South Asian women in the United Kingdom who have suffered domestic violence, & identifies some of the risk factors for domestic violence within this community. The study, based on in-depth interviews with 18 Asian women, describes & analyses several aspects of domestic violence in relation to South Asian women. The guiding research questions are: how do Asian women interpret their experiences of domestic violence, & to whom do they report it? This article presents data that suggest that abusive acts against Asian women arise out of a multiplicity of cultural circumstances influenced by power relations. Abusive acts are not therefore limited to a single characteristic, such as physical abuse, or to a particular relationship. Recurrent themes emerge from the women's accounts, revealing their definitions of domestic violence & showing how some continue to play down the levels of violence they experience. The article voices the concerns of & hardships experienced by victims & survivors of domestic violence, in their own words. Finally, the article offers an analysis of the ways in which notions of honor & shame are used both as tools to constrain women's self-determination & independence, & as catalysts for domestic violence when these notions of family & community are challenged by women. Adapted from the source document.

Correlates of the Co-Occurrence of Wife Abuse and Child Abuse among a Representative Sample

Author: Tajima, Emiko A11 School Social Work, U Washington, Seattle etajima@u.washington.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61433422?accountid=14925

Abstract: The co-occurrence of domestic violence & child abuse is well documented (Appel, A. E., & Holden, G. W. (1998). J. Fam. Psychol. 12: 578-599; Edleson, J. L. (1999). Violence Against Women 5: 134-154). However, little is known about the correlates of co-occurring wife & child abuse. Analyzing data from the 1985 National Family Violence Survey (subsample N = 2733), this study identified risk factors associated with the co-occurrence of wife & child abuse. One-way ANOVA & chi-square analyses were conducted to compare characteristics of parents, children, & households among subgroups of families reporting some form of abuse. Key differences emerged between the three types of homes compared (i.e., those with child abuse alone, wife abuse alone, or wife & child abuse), indicating possibly distinct etiologies & processes. In particular, the co-occurrence of wife & child abuse was marked by less education, worse health, increased reports of depression, & increased husband drug use. Findings may be useful to practitioners & researchers interested in risk factors for different forms of family violence. 4 Tables, 2 Figures, 35 References. Adapted from the source document.

Intimate Partner Violence and the Justice System: An Examination of the Interface

Author: Jordan, Carol E11 Center Research Violence Against Women, U Kentucky

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61548783?accountid=14925

Abstract: Women entering the court system face a challenging experience, in part, because a courtroom can be an intimidating & difficult place for any person, & in part because women victimized by crimes in which the offender is known to them face distinctive difficulties when they seek the court's remedies. The interface is also made more challenging for women as the literature offers disparate findings as to the efficacy of criminal justice responses & civil remedies. This article briefly explores the unique characteristics of intimate partner violence cases that influence the interface of these victims with the court system. A review is provided of research on the criminal justice interventions in cases of intimate partner violence; & research on the efficacy of civil protective orders. 22 References. Helping Women Understand Their Risk in Situations of Intimate Partner Violence

Author: Campbell, Jacquelyn C11 School Nursing, Johns Hopkins U

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61555516?accountid=14925

Abstract: Only approximately one-half of the 456 women who were killed or almost killed by a husband, boyfriend, or ex-husband or ex-boyfriend in a recent national study of homicide of women accurately perceived their risk of being killed by their abusive partner. Women are unlikely to overestimate their risk; however, many will underestimate the severity of the situation. From the same study, it was found that relatively few of the victims of actual or attempted intimate partner femicide were seen by domestic violence advocates during the year before they were killed; they were far more likely to be seen in the health care system. Implications are drawn as to innovative ways that women who are abused can be identified & with skilled assessment of the danger in their relationship helped make more informed plans for their safety. 33 References. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Inc., copyright 2004.]

Separation as a Risk Factor for Victims of Intimate Partner Violence: Beyond Lethality and Injury. A Response to Campbell

Author: Logan, T K1; Walker, Robert1 Dept Behavioral Science, U Kentucky

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61434060?accountid=14925

Abstract: Leaving an abusive partner can lead to death or serious injury, but separation involves other risk factors as well: mental & physical health problems; concerns over child safety & custody; & barriers to help-seeking, including poverty, psychological stress, & social isolation. These risk factors may affect a woman's ability to adjust to the separation or remain separated from her violent ex-partner. Violent ex-partners sometimes threaten to harm the children & may use child visitation to further victimize the abused ex-partner. The need for further research on risk factors associated with separation from a violent partner is discussed. 66 References. A. Lee

Abstract: African American women are at elevated risk for nonfatal & lethal intimate partner violence (IPV). Future research directions include using more diverse Black samples, considering how living at the intersection of multiple forms of oppression shapes Black women's experience with violence, exploring the influence of historical events & oppressive images on victimization, & focusing on survivors' resilience & activism. 1 Table, 18 References. Intimate Partner Violence and Use of Welfare Services among California Women

Author: Kimerling, Rachel1; Baumrind, Nikki1 National Center Posttraumatic Stress Disorder, Palo Alto VA Health Care System, CA

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61454525?accountid=14925

Abstract: The current study is a population-based investigation of the association between past-year exposure to intimate partner violence (IPV) & current welfare use, while also accounting for the effects of other violence experienced in adulthood & symptoms of posttraumatic stress disorder (PTSD). These data indicate that acute exposure to intimate partner violence is significantly over-represented among women currently on welfare. However, it appears to be a woman's cumulative exposure to interpersonal violence & associated symptoms of PTSD that are uniquely associated with welfare participation. These data highlight the prevalence of violence against women & its consequences for this population. Results suggest that the prevention & detection of violence is an important welfare issue, & highlight the need for more research in this area. 3 Tables, 31 References. Adapted from the source document.

Across the public/private boundary: contextualising domestic violence in South Africa

Author: Bassadien, Shahana Rasool1; Hochfeld, Tessa1 Department of Comparative Social Policy at Oxford University. E-mail: shahana@telkomsa.net

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61651809?accountid=14925

Abstract: In this article we plan to challenge two aspects of the public/private dichotomy based on its relevance in the South African context. Firstly, we question the assumption that women's experiences of violence are private and thus a secret, and secondly we assert that the notion of the 'public' includes social discourses on domestic violence that have not been adequately acknowledged and addressed. These discourses are powerful in determining women's decisions to act (or not) after experiencing domestic violence, and hence are potentially harmful to women. We conclude that the way the theoretical notion of the public vs private is understood fundamentally shapes intellectual and service responses. The result is that service providers, when working with survivors of abuse, are still operating from the base of inappropriate imported theories that neglect to explain domestic violence in convincing, local ways, which means that opportunities for assistance to women and men are lost. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://GS6DU7GB6H.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Across+the+public%2Fprivate+boundary%3A+contextualising+domestic+violence+in+South+Africa&amp;author=Bassadien%2C+Shahana+Rasool%3BHochfeld%2C+Tessa&amp;issn=10130950&amp;title=Agenda&amp;volume=&amp;issue=66&amp;date=2005-01-01&amp;spage=4&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Restorative justice and domestic violence: some exploratory thoughts

Author: Hargovan, Hema11 University of KwaZulu-Natal. E-mail: Hargovanh@ukzn.ac.za

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61601945?accountid=14925

Abstract: Restorative justice as a framework for working with victims and offenders in the aftermath of crime has been adopted in a variety of processes and contexts over the years. This focus critically examines the restorative justice approach in criminal justice with special reference to its applicability to domestic violence cases. Despite the implementation of the Domestic Violence Act of 1998, violence against women in South Africa continues unabated. The second part of the focus outlines specific provisions in the Act and proposes possibilities for integration of restorative principles. The focus concludes that a prerequisite for successful integration of restorative options is the development of regulatory frameworks and minimum standards in restorative justice practice. Intimate Partner Violence and Monogamy among Women in Methadone Treatment

Author: Hearn, Kimberly D; O'Sullivan, Lucia F; El-Bassel, Nabila; Gilbert, Louisa

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61337797?accountid=14925

Abstract: It is now becoming clear how important it is to understand women's HIV risk in the context of their sexual relationships with male partners, particularly among more vulnerable populations of women such as drug-involved women and women with physically abusive partners. Drawing from in-depth interviews with a sample of 38 ethnically diverse women, this study explores the meanings of monogamy and concurrent sexual partnerships in the relationships of women in methadone treatment with a history of physical abuse. Moreover, the ways in which having a history of intimate partner violence influences women's desire and ability to insist on monogamy is addressed. The women's narratives indicated that the majority valued monogamy and reported practicing it; however, many women were indifferent to this ideal or were unable to challenge non-monogamous partners for fear of severe reprisals. In addition, men's suspicions about violations of monogamy on the part of the women often resulted in extreme violence.

Exploring Alternative Pathways Out of Poverty: Making Connections between Domestic Violence and Employment Practices

Author: Costello, Mayet1; Chung, Donna; Carson, Ed1 U South Australia

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/59978334?accountid=14925

Abstract: In this article we report on the research findings from our exploratory study examining connections between Australian responses to two important social issues: domestic violence & employment. Although international literature particularly from North America, comprehensively addresses the connections between domestic violence & employment, there is a paucity of Australian research on this topic. Intrigued by this as both practitioners & researchers, we sought to discover whether the problems, solutions & conclusions addressed internationally were relevant to an Australian context. The result was a unique contribution to Australian knowledge & practice where we demonstrate the applicability of some aspects of North American approaches to Australia & document innovative local practices in the field. In this article we argue for a more comprehensive approach in responses to the connections between domestic violence & employment as an alternative pathway out of poverty for women affected by domestic violence. 22 References. 'Why Is a Woman Still Not Safe When She's in Her Home?': Seven Years of NIBRS Data on Victims and Offenders of Intimate Partner Violence

Author: Vazquez, Salvador1; Stohr, Mary K; Skow, Karen; Purkiss, Marcus1 c/o Stohr -- Dept Criminal Justice, Boise State U, ID

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60028490?accountid=14925

Abstract: The descriptors of intimate partner violence at the state level have rarely been empirically explored. In this examination of seven years of one state's National Incident Based Reporting System (NIBRS) data, we delineate the descriptors of victims, offenders, their relationships & the extent of injury sustained by the victims. We discovered that our findings mirror those of the best national victimization studies in three important respects: the victims of IPV tend to be female, young & disproportionately minority; the offenders tend to be male & slightly older than the victim; & injuries & offenses against female victims were more serious than those experienced by males. 8 Tables, 3 Figures, 58 References. Adapted from the source document.

War against the Family: Domestic Violence and Human Rights in Russia - A View from the Bashkortostan Republic

Author: Zakirova, Venera

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/811958252?accountid=14925

Abstract: Although the former Soviet totalitarian structures are being dismantled, new forms of political, economic and social organizations are not yet in place. In Communist Russia, the family as an ideological institution shaped official family policy. The contemporary transformation of Russian society has inevitably touched family life and women's roles within it. This article presents an analysis of domestic violence in Russia, and makes recommendations for social action, legislation, social condemnation and the women's movement.

Intergenerational Transmission of Domestic Violence: Evidence for Colombian Families

Author: Salas Bahamon, Luz Magdalena11 Subdireccion General Dept Nacional Planeacion lsalas@dnp.gov.co

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60016002?accountid=14925

Abstract: With information from the CEDE 2003 survey, conducted to 2295 women in Bogota, Barranquilla & Barrancabermeja, reproduction mechanisms of domestic violence were studied, particularly, severe physical mistreatment against women, from one generation to the next. Using probit models, factors that determine if a woman can be physically mistreated by her partner, given that he grew up in a violent & non-violent home, & factors that determine if a woman gets divorced from her violent partner, given that she grew up in a violent & non-violent home, were estimated. The results show how domestic violence is transmitted from generation to generation by learning (life experiences at home); nevertheless, there are mechanisms that break out the cycle of violence & reduce the proportion of families that reproduce aggressive behaviors in the next generation. Following Pollak's methodology (2002), divorce is the mechanism that reduces the most the level of domestic violence in some cities of Colombia, going from 33.4% of violent homes to 15.7%, in the long term. Assuming assortative mating, the level of domestic violence decreases to 14.8%. Simulation exercises, such as the increase in women education level, greater job opportunities & more access to health services, reduces the number of violent homes in the long term. Other variables related to family decision making were also analyzed. Graphs, 2 Diagrams, Appendixes, References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://GS6DU7GB6H.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Intergenerational+Transmission+of+Domestic+Violence%3A+Evidence+for+Colombian+Families&amp;author=Salas+Bahamon%2C+Luz+Magdalena&amp;issn=01203584&amp;title=Desarrollo+y+Sociedad&amp;volume=&amp;issue=56&amp;date=2005-01-01&amp;spage=285&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

The Mental Health and Service Needs of Young Children Exposed to Domestic Violence: Supportive Data

Author: Lundy, Marta1; Grossman, Susan F1 School Social Work, Loyola U, Chicago, IL mlundy@luc.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61296644?accountid=14925

Abstract: As the visibility of domestic violence (DV) continues to increase, so too does our awareness of the needs of the children in violent families. This study documents the various needs & problems of over 40,000 children as reported by their battered caretakers. What follows is a description of the findings from data collected between 1990 & 1995 from approximately 50 DV agencies in a Midwestern state. The findings indicate that large proportions of children had emotional & social problems whereas about one quarter had physical health & educational difficulties. Common problems included being very protective of family members, discipline issues, mood swings, & difficulty leaving parents. The implications of these findings & ideas for future planning are identified. Tables, Appendixes, References. Adapted from the source document.

HIV and Domestic Violence: Intersections in the Lives of Married Women in India

Author: Desai, Sapna

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61350802?accountid=14925

Abstract: Gender inequality is driving two distinct yet interlinked epidemics among women in India: HIV & AIDS & domestic violence. As domestic violence is increasingly recognized & HIV infection expands, policy & programs do not reflect the interlinked risks & consequences in married women's lives. This article seeks to establish the nexus between HIV & AIDS & domestic violence & identify potential areas for a state-led response. In a health & human rights approach, it assesses women's vulnerability to each epidemic at the individual, societal, & program levels to analyze direct & underlying factors that determine women's risk. Three areas are identified as opportunities for an integrated response: strengthen HIV & domestic violence strategies & address their overlap; mainstream gender; & improve data & research. References. Adapted from the source document.

Intimate Partner Violence Among Women in a Migrant Community in Southwest Nigeria

Author: Owoaje, Eme T1; Olaolorun, Funmilola M1 Department of Community Medicine, College of Medicine, University of Ibadan, PMB5116, Ibadan, Oyo State, Nigeria emeowoaje@yahoo.com

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61425850?accountid=14925

Abstract: This descriptive cross sectional study was conducted to determine attitudes to and the prevalence of intimate partner violence (IPV) among women of reproductive age in a migrant community in southwest Nigeria. All consenting females aged 15-49 years in the area sampled were interviewed. A structured questionnaire administered in the local language was used to obtain information on attitudes toward IPV in seven hypothetical situations as well as their exposure to IPV. Eighty-seven percent of women had ever experienced IPV, while 20% experienced IPV in the preceding 12 months. Regarding attitudes to IPV, 79.5% of women believed that wife beating was justified in at least one of seven situations. Respondents who were not living with a male partner were more likely than their counterparts who were married/cohabiting to accept IPV (p = 0.03). There is a need for a cultural reorientation through appropriate health education interventions. Adapted from the source document.

Men's Arrack Drinking And Domestic Violence Against Women In A Bangladeshi Village

Author: Karim, K M Rabiul11 Department of Social Work, University of Rajsbahi, Rajshahi 6205, Bangladesh rkarimsw@yahoo.com

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61365329?accountid=14925

Abstract: Arrack, indigenously made and known as tari, is a widely-used alcoholic beverage among poor drinkers in rural Bangladesh. This study examines the influences of men's arrack drinking habits on violence against women at the family level in Parbattipur, a typical village of northwest Bangladesh. Fifty purposively-selected married male arrack drinkers as well as their wives were interviewed singly by using a semi-structured questionnaire. Results show that arrack drinkers suffer from behavioral abnormalities. It creates family problems and women are often victimized by their husbands' drunken behaviors. Although violence against women is a common phenomenon in rural Bangladesh, it is argued that men's habitual arrack drinking behaviors, through the interplay of a patriarchal family structure, are directly linked with incidences of violence against women within marriage. Adapted from the source document.

Domestic Violence and Repeat Victimization

Author: McLean, Maxwell11 West Yorkshire Police, UK

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/59983692?accountid=14925

Abstract: This paper seeks to establish domestic violence as the most prevalent repeated, criminal offence of our time. The author makes use of his experience in introducing the United Kingdom's first ever Repeat Victimiaztion Methodology to the crime of domestic violence, implemented in Killingbeck Division, Leeds. He seeks to show that early structured intervention does reduce repeated attendances & that significant achievements can be made with little or no extra resources. The paper demonstrates, by reference to a murder case study, the tragic consequences of agencies' failure to share information. A number of policy recommendations are made for consideration by governments, many of which are now being implemented in the United Kingdom. 7 Figures, 5 References. Adapted from the source document.

The Disassociation between Domestic Violence Service Provision and Multi-Agency Initiatives on Domestic Violence

Author: Welsh, Kirsty11 Centre Criminological Research, Dept Law, U Sheffield, UK

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/59997827?accountid=14925

Abstract: In recent years in England & Wales, the multi-agency approach has come to be increasingly promoted in government circles as a response in domestic violence. In this article, I reflect on doctoral research to question the assumption that the multi-agency approach should be celebrated as a leading response to the problem. Rather, I argue that such approaches increase neither women's protection nor their safety. Focusing on findings from two research areas in Northern England, I argue that there was a separation or disassociation between multi-agency domestic violence initiatives & service provision in these areas. I illustrate the argument using three examples from the research. Other issues are important in founding the argument that such approaches are not increasing women's protection or safety. These issues centre on the interaction between service provision & prevention. I argue that, because appropriate service provision is essential in preventing domestic violence, an approach that is not grounded in service provision but is disassociated from it can do little to prevent domestic violence & protect women. References. Adapted from the source document.

A Coordinated Response to Intimate Partner Violence: Lessons from an Exploratory Study

Author: Slaght, Evelyn1; Hamilton, Nina1 School Social Work, Illinois State U, Normal

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61367652?accountid=14925

Abstract: A qualitative study of two Family Violence Coordinating Councils in Illinois confirmed the importance of collaboration between the law enforcement & treatment communities in responding to the needs of families enmeshed in domestic violence. A key finding of the study is that a joint philosophy of intervention is crucial to effective coordination. As key actors change, training is needed to reemphasize the special needs of batterers & victims, so that emphasis on prosecution does not preclude treatment. The judiciary, the State's Attorney's Office, probation, treatment programs, the medical community, the child welfare agency, & the clergy need to be invested in the coordinating process. Multimodal treatment services are needed, & must be supported by law enforcement. Data documenting the impact of intervention on intimate partner violence should be routinely collected to assess the effectiveness of a coordinated response in reducing recidivism. 20 References.
