The Parallels between Undue Influence, Domestic Violence, Stalking, and Sexual Assault

Author: Brandl, Bonnie1; Heisler, Candace J; Stiegel, Lori A1 c/o Stiegel -- ABA Commision Law & Aging, Washington, DC

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61631765?accountid=14925

Abstract: The dynamics of undue influence (UI) have many similarities with domestic violence, stalking, & grooming behavior used by some sexual predators. This article will help practitioners -- particularly law enforcement investigators & prosecutors -- better recognize UI as a pattern of behaviors, not an isolated incident. Understanding the dynamics of UI will enhance professionals' appreciation of the responses of victims & the manipulative nature of exploiters. Strategies that have been used effectively with domestic violence, stalking, & some sexual abuse cases may provide remedies for victims of UI & criminal justice options for holding perpetrators accountable. Enhanced awareness of these dynamics should lead to improved investigations, more effective strategies when interviewing & working with victims, & more successful prosecutions of perpetrators who use UI to financially exploit an older person. Internal Barriers to Help Seeking for Middle-Aged and Older Women Who Experience Intimate Partner Violence

Author: Beaulaurier, Richard L1; Seff, Laura R; Newman, Frederick L; Dunlop, Burton1 School Social Work, Coll Health & Urban Affairs, Florida International U, Miami

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61387237?accountid=14925

Abstract: There has been relatively little qualitatively or quantitatively published research to guide the field in identifying & addressing barriers to seeking help for older women who experience domestic violence. Women over the age of 45 have seldom been participants in research on domestic violence. This paper describes results from a qualitative study that focused on reactions to & internalization of abusive behaviors of an intimate partner. Twenty-one focus groups were conducted with 134 middle-aged & older women. Analysis of the data identified six factors that describe reactions to abusive behavior that become barriers to help seeking, including self-blame, powerlessness, hopelessness, the need to protect family, & the need to keep such abuse secret from others. These findings are discussed with implications for community outreach & future research. Tables, References. Adapted from the source document. Precinct Domestic Violence Teams: Whose Goals should Determine Program Activities?

Author: Weisz, Arlene N1; Black, Beverly M; Nahan, Neva1 School Social Work, Wayne State U, Detroit, MI

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61380421?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article describes an urban community's attempts to increase domestic violence survivors' participation in the criminal justice system by combining social work advocacy, specialized police officers, & prosecutors into precinct domestic violence teams. An analysis of the outcomes of 1,057 domestic violence reports found that the presence of the teams did not result in increased survivor participation in prosecution. A survey of 265 of the survivors indicated that prosecution was rarely a goal of the survivors, whose participation was needed to meet the goals of the criminal justice system. This study illustrates the importance of learning the views of people that interventions are designed to help. Tables, References. Adapted from the source document. Author: Rountree, Michele A11 School Social Work, U Texas, Austin

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61387956?accountid=14925

Abstract: The purpose of this exploratory study is to identify the current practices & barriers related to the use of HIV/AIDS risk assessment measures & the provision of HIV/AIDS prevention services in seven domestic violence shelters in Arizona. Interviewees reported several structural impediments to the integration of risk assessment measures & educational/prevention services related to HIV/AIDS including fiscal constraints, enhanced training of staff, hiring of new staff, discrimination by staff & a need for an interdisciplinary approach. Domestic Violence: Focus Groups from the Survivors' Perspective

Author: Dziegielewski, Sophia F1; Campbell, Katharine; Turnage, Barbara F1 School Social Work, U Cincinnati, OH

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61397598?accountid=14925

Abstract: As our communities & families are faced with the growing problem of violence, more information is needed as to what women in abusive relationships see as challenges that prevent or delay leaving. To address the issue in the brief therapeutic environment, 14 women in counseling for abuse agreed to participate in three task groups & were asked to identify & rank-order the challenges they faced that could prevent them from leaving. Group one included women who had a desire to leave but were not sure they could. Group two was women working on a case plan but who still had concerns; &, the third group was women who had left the relationship & were expecting not to return. Overall, when analyzing & comparing these groups, all women (regardless of the stage of leaving) expressed similar challenges & concerns. Major themes presented in all groups included: Limited resources (eg, money, shelter, daycare & medical expenses), fears (eg, fear of being killed, retaliation, being alone, making the wrong choices, the unknown), commitment to the relationship, concern for children, love for the abuser & other emotional issues. These challenges are discussed & specific applications to facilitate practice are made. Tables, References. Adapted from the source document. Exploring the Relevance of Attachment Theory as a Dependent Variable in the Treatment of Women Mandated into Treatment for Domestic Violence Offenses

Author: Carney, Michelle Mohr1; Buttell, Frederick P1 School Social Work, U Georgia, Athens mmcarney@uga.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61371304?accountid=14925

Abstract: Objective: The purpose of the study was to: (a) investigate the pre-treatment levels of interpersonal dependency & violence among women entering a 16-week, court-mandated, batterer intervention program (BIP) & determine if there were any associations between interpersonal dependency & violence; (b) investigate differences in demographic variables & psychological variables between treatment completers & drop-outs; & (c) evaluate the treatment effect of a standard BIP in altering levels of interpersonal dependency among treatment completers. Method: The study employed a secondary analysis of 75 women, 39 treatment completers & 36 drop-outs. Results: Analysis indicated that women who assault their intimate partners & are court-ordered into treatment are excessively dependent on their partners prior to beginning treatment, that level of interpersonal dependency is directly related to a multidimensional conceptualization of domestic violence (i.e., psychological aggression, physical assault, sexual coercion & injury), that interpersonal dependency is an important variable in predicting treatment completion & that the BIP increased the level of interpersonal dependency among treatment completers. Conclusion: Implications of the findings for professionals providing intervention services to women in court-mandated batterer intervention programs were explored & discussed. Tables, References. Personality Profiles of Women and Men Arrested for Domestic Violence: An Analysis of Similarities and Differences

Author: Simmons, Catherine A1; Lehmann, Peter; Cobb, Norman; Fowler, Carol R1 School Social Work, U Texas, Arlington

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61374152?accountid=14925

Abstract: Women arrested for intimate partner violence raise challenges for those working in domestic violence programs. Theoretically, there is no agreement about whether women are aggressive for the same reasons as men or merely victims fighting back in an abusive relationship. Practically, there is very little research to guide treatment of this population. In the current exploratory study, Millon Clinical Multiaxial InventoryIII (MCMI-III) personality profiles of 78 females referred by the courts to a domestic violence diversion program were compared to those of a matched sample of 78 males referred by the courts to the same program. Compared with male offenders, women were more likely to demonstrate elevated histrionic, narcissistic, & compulsive personality traits, & less likely to demonstrate dependant personality traits. Additionally, women in this study were more likely to display MCMI-III profiles indicating the presence of personality disorders. Implications of these findings for the treatment setting are discussed. Tables, Graphs, References. Adapted from the source document. Investigating Intersections between Gender and Intimate Partner Violence Recidivism

Author: Renauer, Brian1; Henning, Kris1 Portland State U, OR renauer@pdx.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61380734?accountid=14925

Abstract: Mandatory & preferred arrest policies for domestic violence (DV) have led to an increase in DV arrests & prosecutions of male & female suspects. The strain of this increase on criminal justice resources suggests a need for prioritizing DV cases & determining suspects most at risk for recidivism & victimization. The present study sought to address a largely ignored question in the field of DV: who is more likely to recidivate, male or female DV offenders involved with the criminal justice system? Two forms of recidivism were coded using subsequent police reports for DV: recidivism as a suspect & recidivism as a victim. The results indicate that there are significant differences in the ways in which male & female DV offenders recidivate. Males were more likely to recidivate as a suspect & females were more likely to be listed as a future DV victim in police reports. Male & female offenders also significantly differed in the frequency of their recidivism. Gender, independent of other factors, was reliably associated with both forms of recidivism & a similar pattern of recidivism was found in two different cities. There was, however, a small group of female offenders in both cities who appear to be primary aggressors. Another grouping of female offenders appears to be involved in relationships characterized by mutual aggression & bi-directional violence. Together these two groups account for roughly a quarter of the women in our sample. The implications of the findings for arrest practices, treatment & interventions, & using gender as a risk factor are discussed. Validity & reliability problems with using police reports as a measure of DV recidivism are also reviewed. Tables, References. Adapted from the source document. COPIES ARE AVAILABLE FROM: HAWORTH DOCUMENT DELIVERY CENTER, The Haworth Press, Inc., 10 Alice Street, Binghamton, NY 13904-1580

The Meaning of Dating Violence in the Lives of Middle School Adolescents: A Report of a Focus Group Study

Author: Fredland, Nina M1; Ricardo, Izabel B; Campbell, Jacquelyn C; Sharps, Phyllis W; Kub, Joan K; Yonas, Michael1 School Nursing, Johns Hopkins U

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61352553?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article reports qualitative findings of seven focus groups that illuminate the phenomenon of dating & dating violence from the perspective of the young adolescent. This study was part of a larger intervention project, "An Arts-Based Initiative for the Prevention of Violence Against Women & Girls," a collaboration of the Centers for Disease Control & Prevention & Johns Hopkins University, School of Nursing. Concepts of interest included: dating norms, ecological factors, elements of healthy & unhealthy relationships, & prevention strategies. Four themes emerged: respect versus disrespect, influence of friends, cost & benefit of sexual activity & violence as an acceptable response. The implications of this data highlight the need for school personnel to increase their awareness & understanding of the challenges middle school youth face. Dating violence prevention programs should be designed accordingly. References. Adapted from the source document. Exploring the Effects of Head Injuries among Battered Women: A Qualitative Study of Chronic and Severe Woman Battering

Author: Roberts, Albert R1; Kim, Jung H1 Dept Administration Justice, Livingston Coll, Rutgers U, Piscataway, NJ prof.albertroberts@comcast.net

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61382054?accountid=14925

Abstract: This qualitative study examined the link between chronic woman battering & head injuries in a sample of 52 battered women. The voices of the women in terms of the qualitative data revealed that severe & repeated women battering seems to be associated with head injuries, flashbacks, insomnia, major depression & nightmares of the batterer killing the victim. The research findings support the need for neurological workups among battered women with severe head injuries to rule out temporal lobe epilepsy, & other traumatic brain injuries. It is imperative that battered women be assessed & screened for neurological injuries as well as mental disorders so that appropriate medical & mental health services can be provided. Social workers & psychiatric nurses in hospital emergency rooms & other medical settings are in a pivotal role to detect & facilitate intervention for battered women with neurological injuries & mental disorders. Tables, References. Adapted from the source document. COPIES ARE AVAILABLE FROM: HAWORTH DOCUMENT DELIVERY CENTER, The Haworth Press, Inc., 10 Alice Street, Binghamton, NY 13904-1580

How to Make the Law Work? Budgetary Implications of Domestic Violence Laws in Latin America, Central America, and the Caribbean

Author: Luciano, Dinys1; Esim, Simel; Duvvury, Nata1 Pan American Health Organization, Gender & Ethnicity Unit, Washington, DC

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/59979959?accountid=14925

Abstract: This study focuses on budgetary implications of domestic violence (DV) laws in the Latin American, Central American, & Caribbean (LAC) region. It is based on a survey of literature & data on DV legislation, literature reviews on the social & economic costs of DV, & an analysis of policies on DV in the region based on a survey of nonprofits working on DV in the region. The study shows DV is not being mainstreamed into ministerial budget line items as would be expected following the passage of law. There is clearly a difference between what is ratified in laws & the implementation of activities that follows those decisions. Funding for DV programs is insufficient to reach the entire target population & address the magnitude of the problem. Even after laws were passed, major sources of funding for DV services have continued to be discretionary funds from the ministries' budgets & international donor funds. References. Adapted from the source document. COPIES ARE AVAILABLE FROM: HAWORTH DOCUMENT DELIVERY CENTER, The Haworth Press, Inc., 10 Alice Street, Binghamton, NY 13904-1580

The Importance of Expectation Fulfilment on Domestic Violence Victims' Satisfaction with the Police in the UK

Author: Robinson, Amanda L1; Stroshine, Meghan S1 School Social Sciences, Cardiff U, UK

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60009582?accountid=14925

Abstract: Purpose: This paper seeks to investigate what victims of domestic violence expect police to do for them, & how these expectations subsequently influence their levels of satisfaction. Design/methodology/approach: Structured interviews with 222 victims of domestic violence were conducted by staff from an integrated community-based service delivery agency in Cardiff, Wales. Multivariate analyses were performed to reveal the factors that contribute to domestic violence victims' satisfaction with the police. Findings: Consistent with the expectancy disconfirmation model, results indicate that the most important determinant of satisfaction is the extent to which victims' expectations about police behaviour & demeanour are fulfilled. Originality/value: The results of this study & implications for police policy are discussed. Tables, References. Adapted from the source document.

The Journey to Resiliency: An Integrative Framework for Treatment for Victims and Survivors of Family Violence

Author: Cairns-Descoteaux, Bonita11 Roberts Wesleyan Coll, Rochester, NY Descoteauxb@roberts.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61367941?accountid=14925

Abstract: The model presented in this article provides a framework for working with victims of family violence that differs from other integrative frameworks in the professional literature by including a spiritual dimension. There are four parts to the model that are discussed in this paper: development of a healthy self-concept, connection to a loving God, healing of memories, & consolidating personal growth. The article begins with a process to assess clients, based on their journey towards resiliency. Using this assessment as a basis for intervention, the social worker can begin working with the client, using the four phases in this model. References. Adapted from the source document.

The Role of Alcohol Use and Depression in Intimate Partner Violence among Black and Hispanic Patients in an Urban Emergency Department

Author: Lipsky, Sherry1; Caetano, Raul; Field, Craig A; Bazargan, Shahrzad1 Dallas Regional Campus, U Texas Houston School Public Health

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61510496?accountid=14925

Abstract: Objectives: The primary objective of this study was to assess the role of alcohol use & depression in intimate partner violence (IPV) victimization & perpetration among Blacks & Hispanics in an underserved urban emergency department population. Methods: This cross-sectional study surveyed male & female patients presenting to an urban emergency department. The outcome measures were physical or sexual IPV victimization & perpetration in the previous 12 months. The independent predictors included demographic variables, alcohol & drug use, & depressive symptoms. Logistic regression analyses calculated the adjusted odds ratio (AOR) & 95% confidence interval (CI) for predictors of IPV victimization & perpetration in separate models. Results: The prevalence of IPV victimization among Blacks & Hispanics were similar (14% & 10%, respectively) but blacks were nearly twice as likely to report IPV perpetration (17% vs. 9%, respectively).Predictors of IPV perpetration were Black race, married or living with a partner, heavy drinking, illicit drug use, & current depression. Depression, but not substance use, also predicted IPV victimization, in addition to Black race, married or living with a partner, & younger age. Conclusions: Screening for substance abuse & depression in an inner city emergency department population may help to identify individuals at high risk of IPV, particularly IPV perpetration. 3 Tables, 53 References. Adapted from the source document.

Domestic Violence at the Intersections of Race, Class, and Gender: Challenges and Contributions to Understanding Violence against Marginalized Women in Diverse Communities

Author: Sokoloff, Natalie J1; Dupont, Ida1 John Jay Coll Criminal Justice, City U New York

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60006441?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article provides a comprehensive review of the emerging domestic violence literature using a race, class, gender, sexual orientation intersectional analysis & structural framework fostered by women of color & their allies to understand the experiences & contexts of domestic violence for marginalized women in U.S. society. The first half of the article lays out a series of challenges that an intersectional analysis grounded in a structural framework provides for understanding the role of culture in domestic violence. The second half of the article points to major contributions of such an approach to feminist methods & practices in working with battered women on the margins of society. References. Author: Shetty, Sudha1; Edleson, Jeffrey L1 Access Justice Instit, School Law, Seattle U

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61368248?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article discusses the Hague Convention on the Civil Aspects of International Child Abduction & its impact on battered mothers & their children seeking safety in the United States. We discuss relevant articles of the convention, the extent to which adult domestic violence is present in cases of international parental abduction, & cases in which battered mothers have contested the forced return of their children to an abusive partner. The Composite Abuse Scale: Further Development and Assessment of Reliability and Validity of a Multidimensional Partner Abuse Measure in Clinical Settings

Author: Hegarty, Kelsey; Bush, Robert; Sheehan, Mary

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61329889?accountid=14925

Abstract: Absence of a well-validated comprehensive partner abuse questionnaire has been a major methodological issue in domestic violence research. A new multidimensional measure of partner abuse, the Composite Abuse Scale (CAS), has four dimensions: Severe Combined Abuse, Emotional Abuse, Physical Abuse, and Harassment. A general practice patient sample (N = 1,836) has been used in the development and testing of CAS. Factor analyses in this current study confirmed the four dimensions from a preliminary nurses sample study and resulted in a final scale of 30 items consisting of acts of physical, emotional, and sexual abuse. These four factors exhibited good internal reliability (Cronbach's alpha > 0.85) and the corrected item-total correlations were high (> 0.5). Evidence of criterion and construct validity is presented. [PUBLICATION ABSTRACT].

Women Police Stations as a Dispute Processing System: The Tamil Nadu Experience in Dealing with Dowry-Related Domestic Violence Cases

Author: Natarajan, Mangai11 John Jay Coll Criminal Justice, City U, New York, NY mnataraj@jjay.cuny.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/59998991?accountid=14925

Abstract: In 1992, the Tamil Nadu State government in India began to introduce all-women police stations whose primary role is to deal with crimes against women, including family violence & dowry disputes. Dowry giving is a customary practice in India, which often results in disputes between young wives & their new families. As a result, the young women may be subjected to physical & mental ill treatment, sometimes resulting in death. Using detailed data from 474 case records & interviews with 60 dowry victims, the present study examines how women police stations serve as a dispute processing system, & describes the services extended by the stations to the victims of dowry disputes. Many cases were successfully resolved & violence was frequently reduced. The study holds implications for the extension of training in dispute resolution for women police in India. Specialized Domestic Violence Courts: Improvement for Women Victims?

Author: Coulter, Martha L1; Alexander, Abigail; Harrison, Victoria1 Department of Community and Family Health, College of Public Health, University of South Florida, and Director The Jennifer and James Harrell Center for the Study of Family Violence, 13201 Bruce B. Downs, MDC 56, Tampa, FL 33612 E-mail: mcoulter@hsc.usf.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61652558?accountid=14925

Abstract: The multimethod study assesses the perceptions of specialized domestic violence courts' processes with victims' experiences as the central focus. Perceptions of the traditional courts and specialized domestic violence courts are compared among victims, courtroom police, attorneys, judges and victim advocates. Domestic violence education among attorneys, judges, and victim advocates is also compared. Despite the intended improvements with the specialized court model, victims report similar problems in both court models. Safety and victims support among respondents is mixed. Professionals from the specialized court receive no more domestic violence education than those from the general court. Victims' and courtroom police recommendations are presented. Exploring Agreement on Appropriate Responses to Domestic Violence and Sexual Trauma Across Victim Advocates, Mental Health Service Providers, and Substance Abuse Treatment Providers

Author: Herz, Denise C1; Stroshine, Meghan; Houser, Kristen1 School of Criminal Justice & Criminalistics, California State University, King Hall D1047, Los Angeles, CA 90032 E-mail: dherz@calstatela.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61624258?accountid=14925

Abstract: Research suggests that survivors of domestic violence and sexual assault are more at risk than the general population for substance abuse and/or mental health disorders (Kendler et al., 2000). Additionally, research suggests that survivors of both crimes are at risk to be "multiply diagnosed"-have a history of victimization, a mental health diagnosis, and abuse of alcohol and drugs (Steele & Rechberger, 2002). While advocates have called for a change in the treatment of trauma survivors to treat their multiple needs, the availability of collaborative care between victim advocates and behavioral health professionals remains questionable. Using survey data collected from victim advocates, mental health service providers, and substance abuse treatment providers, this study assesses the extent to which providers of different backgrounds agree on how to effectively and appropriately serve domestic violence and sexual assault survivors-the first step in achieving the goal of holistic care. Changing Help-Seeking Rates for Intimate Partner Violence in Canada

Author: Du Mont, Janice1; Forte, Tonia; Cohen, Marsha M; Hyman, Ilene; Romans, Sarah1 Centre Research Womens Health, Sunnybrook & Womens Health Sciences Centre, U Toronto, ON

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61371713?accountid=14925

Abstract: The adverse physical & psychological sequelae of intimate partner violence (IPV) are well documented, as are government initiatives in Canada since the early 1990s to address the problem through public awareness campaigns & service enhancement programs. While these initiatives have been designed to encourage abused women to come forward, there has been little research examining changes over time in help-seeking rates among this group. To fill this void, we compared data from two large Canadian population-based, cross-sectional telephone surveys: the 1993 Violence Against Women Survey (1993-VAWS) & the 1999 General Social Survey (1999-GSS). Among women who reported physical &/or sexual violence by a current or previous partner, we examined differences in rates of disclosure of abuse, help-seeking by type of service, & barriers to service use. Abused women in the 1999-GSS were significantly more likely than those in the 1993-VAWS to have reported disclosing a violent incident(s) to a family member (66.4% vs. 43.9%), friend or neighbor (67.4% vs. 45.4%), doctor or nurse (31.9% vs. 23.0%), &/or minister, priest, or cleric (11.5% vs. 7.3%). The 1999-GSS cohort was also more likely to have presented to a shelter or transition house (11.0% vs. 7.8%), a crisis center(17.3% vs. 4.2%), a counselor or psychologist (39.1% vs. 14.7%), a women's center (11.2% vs. 3.4%), &/or a community or family center (15.4% vs. 4.7%). Among those women who did not seek help, fewer in the 1999-GSS reported that they did not know of any services (6.4% vs. 17.0%), or that services were not available (0.8% vs. 14.5%). Although we found a demonstrable increase in the numbers of abused women seeking help, overall, rates of service utilization were still low as late as 1999, highlighting the importance of continued government commitment to funding IPV initiatives. Tables, References. Adapted from the source document. COPIES ARE AVAILABLE FROM: HAWORTH DOCUMENT DELIVERY CENTER, The Haworth Press, Inc., 10 Alice Street, Binghamton, NY 13904-1580

Domestic Violence among Druze Women in Israel as Reflected by Health Status and Somatization Level

Author: Goldblatt, Hadass1; Granot, Michal1 Dept Nursing, U Haifa, Mount Carmel, Israel

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/741612552?accountid=14925

Abstract: Women with a history of physical or emotional abuse demonstrate a higher incidence of medical disorders but may be reluctant to disclose the abuse to health care providers. The present study explored whether measures of health status may serve as an indication of violence against women. Sixty-two Druze women (18-50 years old) completed health status (SF-36 survey), somatization (Brief Symptom Inventory -- BSI), & abuse (the Abuse Assessment Screen -- AAS) questionnaires during a visit to the family physician in the community primary care clinic. Higher somatization symptoms & diminished health status were associated with severity of violence. Results attest to the value of these measures as an additional indirect device for screening victims of domestic violence in general & among traditional societies in particular. Tables, References. Domestic Violence: A Tragedy behind the Doors

Author: Ergin, Nilufer1; Bayram, Nuran; Alper, Zuleyha; Selimoglu, Kerem; Bilgel, Nazan1 c/o Bilgel -- Faculty Medicine, Dept Family Medicine, Uludag U, Bursa, Turkey

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61371183?accountid=14925

Abstract: Objective: To explore the prevalence, type, frequency & causes of domestic marital violence among Turkish women in a socio-economically developed metropolitan setting. Methods: A cross-sectional study was performed in 2003. All married women, aged 18 years & over, who were visiting any of the 50 primary health care units for different purposes were asked to participate in the study. Of 1427 asked, 1010 gave consent & completed face-to-face interviews. Multiple correspondence analysis, & logistic regression were used for statistical analyses. Results: Domestic violence was statistically significantly associated with educational level. Illiterate women reported marital abuse 2.6 times more than university or more educated women. No statistically significant relationship was observed between domestic marital violence & women's age or occupation, marital duration or family income. The most frequently reported type of violence was physical, followed by psychological. Among women reporting domestic violence, those with the lowest educational level & income were experiencing all types of domestic violence frequently. Conclusion: Placing more importance on educating girls could be a key component of preventing domestic violence. Tables, Figures, References. Adapted from the source document. A Consumer-Behavior Perspective on Intimate Partner Violence

Author: Stephens, Debra Lynn1; Hill, Ronald Paul; Gentry, James W1 Dr. Robert B. Pamplin, Jr. School Business Administration, U Portland

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60489218?accountid=14925

Abstract: This research examines women's experiences of & responses to intimate partner violence using the perspective of the extended self. From in-depth interviews with a demographically diverse group of women in the United States, the primary theme to emerge was that chronic abuse is experienced as the male partner's ongoing campaign to incorporate the abused woman into his extended self, by appropriating or destroying the aspects of her that give her autonomy. The most important implication for agencies serving abused women is that many of their clients are faced with the daunting task of repairing or reconstructing their core & extended selves, a process that may necessitate the long-term commitment of agency resources without the imposition of restricted, institutionally imposed identities that would serve only to impede clients' reclamation of their autonomy. 62 References. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Inc., copyright 2005.]

Reports of Intimate Partner Violence Made Against Police Officers

Author: Erwin, Melissa J1; Gershon, Robyn R M; Tiburzi, Michael; Lin, Susan1 c/o Gershon -- Mailman School Public Health, Dept Soicomedical Sciences, Columbia U, New York, NY

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61453038?accountid=14925

Abstract: The purpose of this case control study was to compare police officers (n = 106) from a large urban police force, charged with committing intimate partner violence (IPV) with officers who were not charged (n = 105), in order to identify risk factors for IPV. We also compared the frequency, type, & final disposition of IPV reports filed against police officers before & after the enactment of the 1994 Violence against Women Act (VAWA). Officers accused of IPV were more likely to be members of a minority, on the force more than 7 years & assigned to a high crime district. No major differences on intake baseline MMPI scores were noted between cases & controls. A significant increase in reports of IPV was noted after the enactment of VAWA; the rate changed from 0.2/100 to 1.2/100 person years, although there were too few pre-1994 reports to compare further. Most of the final dispositions of the cases resulted in cases closed due to unsupported testimony from victims. 4 Tables, 27 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=Reports+of+Intimate+Partner+Violence+Made+Against+Police+Officers&amp;title=Journal+of+Family+Violence&amp;issn=08857482&amp;date=2005-02-01&amp;volume=20&amp;issue=1&amp;spage=13&amp;author=Erwin%2C+Melissa+J%3BGershon%2C+Robyn+R+M%3BTiburzi%2C+Michael%3BLin%2C+Susan

Hostage-Taking in the Context of Domestic Violence: Some Case Examples

Author: Van Hasselt, Vincent B1; Flood, John J; Romano, Stephen J; Vecchi, Gregory M; Fabrique, Nathalie de; Dalfonzo, Vincent A1 Nova Southeastern University, 3301 College Avenue, Fort Lauderdale, FL, 33314-7796, vanhasse@nova.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/811411962?accountid=14925

Abstract: Initial reports of domestic violence are generally made to law enforcement officers who must respond and intervene. A subset of these episodes involves cases in which the victim, and, in many instances her child(ren), have been taken hostage by her husband or partner. Moreover, there are indications that the number of such incidents is growing. The purpose of this project was twofold: (1) to provide one of the first reports on the prevalence and characteristics of these events, and (2) to more closely analyze domestic crisis (hostage) situations using actual case examples. All information was obtained from the Hostage Barricade Database System (HOBAS) of the FBI's Crisis Negotiation Unit. HOBAS is a postincident information collection tool which stores historical data from law enforcement agencies across the nation on hostage/barricade incidents. An examination of this database yielded different types of domestic hostage-taking acts and outcomes (e.g., tactical vs. negotiated resolutions, survival vs. death/injury of perpetrator and/or victim[s]). Implications of the findings, for future crisis negotiation efforts directed toward nonviolent resolution of these high-risk critical incidents, are discussed.

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=Hostage-Taking+in+the+Context+of+Domestic+Violence%3A+Some+Case+Examples&amp;title=Journal+of+Family+Violence&amp;issn=08857482&amp;date=2005-02-01&amp;volume=20&amp;issue=1&amp;spage=21&amp;author=Van+Hasselt%2C+Vincent+B%3BFlood%2C+John+J%3BRomano%2C+Stephen+J%3BVecchi%2C+Gregory+M%3BFabrique%2C+Nathalie+de%3BDalfonzo%2C+Vincent+A

Comparing Wife Abuse Perceptions Between Chinese Police Officers and Social Workers

Author: Tam, Suet Yan; Tang, Catherine So-kum11 The Chinese University of Hong Kong, Shatin, NT, Hong Kong, ctang@cuhk.edu.hk

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/811411970?accountid=14925

Abstract: Seventy-one Chinese social workers and 74 Chinese police officers residing in Hong Kong were surveyed on their gender-role attitudes, endorsement of wife abuse myths, and definitions of wife abuse. Results showed that compared to social workers, police officers held more conservative gender-role attitudes, endorsed more myths about wife abuse, and adopted more restrictive definitions of physical and psychological wife abuse. Hierarchical regression analyses showed that definitions of wife abuse were related to different predictors for these two groups of professionals. Among social workers, egalitarian gender-role attitudes were a significant predictor of broad definitions of physical wife abuse, but no significant predictor was found for psychological wife abuse. Among police officers, significant predictors of definitions of physical wife abuse included marital status, educational attainment, and endorsement of wife abuse myths; gender-role attitudes were the only significant predictor of definitions of psychological wife abuse.

The Effects of a Police/Victim Assistance Crisis Team Approach to Domestic Violence

Author: Corcoran, Jacqueline1; Allen, Shannon1 Federal Bureau of Prisons, Fort Worth, Texas, jcorcora@saturn.vsu.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/811411873?accountid=14925

Abstract: A police/victim assistance crisis intervention program was evaluated over a 6-month period for its effect on police-related outcomes. Police report data on crisis team cases (N = 96) were compared to a random selection of family violence cases (N = 80) that were not served by the crisis team. The crisis team cases generated more arrests; however, victim cooperation was lower than in noncrisis intervention cases. Implications for work with family violence and victim assistance are discussed, as well as needs for future research.

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=The+Effects+of+a+Police%2FVictim+Assistance+Crisis+Team+Approach+to+Domestic+Violence&amp;title=Journal+of+Family+Violence&amp;issn=08857482&amp;date=2005-02-01&amp;volume=20&amp;issue=1&amp;spage=39&amp;author=Corcoran%2C+Jacqueline%3BAllen%2C+Shannon

Title: Police Perspective: Discovering Hidden Truths in Domestic Violence Intervention

Domestic Violence between Same-Gender Partners: Recent Findings and Future Research

Author: McClennen, Joan C11 Southwest Missouri State U

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60537118?accountid=14925

Abstract: Empirical literature about same-gender domestic violence was relatively nonexistent until the past 20 years, & conducting research with this population about a sensitive topic remains a daunting endeavor. Existing studies reveal similarities between opposite- & same-gender domestic violence in prevalence, types of abuse, & various dynamics, as well as dispel myths & establish a theoretical basis on which to conduct future research. Differences are evident in areas such as help-seeking behaviors & correlates, thus demanding unique assessment & intervention strategies. This article presents further explanation of the latest research, recommendations for future studies, & effective as well as problematic methodological practices about same-gender domestic violence. 14 References. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Inc., copyright 2005.]

The Utility of Male Domestic Violence Offender Typologies: New Directions for Research, Policy, and Practice

Author: Cavanaugh, Mary M1; Gelles, Richard J1 U Pennsylvania

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60510583?accountid=14925

Abstract: The development of empirically based behavioral & psychological typologies provides clear evidence that offenders vary across types. A review & synthesis of the literature reveals three types of batterers common across current typology research -- a low, moderate, & high-risk offender. Examination of these types demonstrates that most male offenders do not escalate over time from low to high levels of risk. This observation refutes previous claims regarding a common consensus among researchers that battering escalates in frequency & intensity over time. There are particular characteristics specific to each type that establish thresholds distinct to each classification. Thus, it is unlikely that an offender will move from one type to another. Interventions targeted to address the needs of subsamples of offenders can lead to more effectively protecting those most at risk for future violence. Addressing the issue of woman battering through the prism of typologies has significant implications for research, policy, & practice. 2 Tables, 28 References. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Inc., copyright 2005.]

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=The+Utility+of+Male+Domestic+Violence+Offender+Typologies%3A+New+Directions+for+Research%2C+Policy%2C+and+Practice&amp;title=Journal+of+Interpersonal+Violence&amp;issn=08862605&amp;date=2005-02-01&amp;volume=20&amp;issue=2&amp;spage=155&amp;author=Cavanaugh%2C+Mary+M%3BGelles%2C+Richard+J

Culture and Domestic Violence: Transforming Knowledge Development

Author: Bent-Goodley, Tricia B11 School Social Work, Howard U

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61544449?accountid=14925

Abstract: Cultural competence continues to receive limited attention in domestic violence service provision from research to the evaluation of programs. Yet with changing demographics reflecting larger numbers of people of color & increasing needs for more effective responses, it is critical that we change the way we think about domestic violence. Using a feminist framework, this article examines how knowledge has been developed in domestic violence & its consequence related to service provision & perceptions. The article ends with recommendations as to how to better include culturally competent responses in domestic violence. 44 References. Trends in Understanding and Addressing Domestic Violence

Author: Mears, Daniel P1; Visher, Christy A1 Justice Policy Center, Urban Instit, Washington D.C dmears@urgan.org

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61513777?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article is a response to three questions posed by the editor about past & future research on interpersonal violence by focusing in this essay on domestic violence: (a) What is the most important thing we have learned about this social problem in the last 20 years, (b) what is the most important thing we need to learn about it in the next 10 years, & (c) what is the most promising methodological innovation in the last 20 years for the study or treatment of domestic violence? This assessment suggests that the field has witnessed considerable advances in domestic violence research & policy but that many as yet untapped opportunities exist to improve both knowledge & practice. 21 References. Exciting Discoveries on the Health Effects of Family Violence: Where We Are, Where We Need to Go

Author: Kendall-Tackett, Kathleen11 Family Research Lab, Crimes Against Research Center, U New Hampshire

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60523825?accountid=14925

Abstract: In the 20 years since the founding of the Journal of Interpersonal Violence, there have been exciting new discoveries on the long-term physical health effects of family violence. As exciting as these discoveries have been, we still know little about why the experience of family violence makes people sick. Some of the most promising areas of study on this topic will be in neuroscience, sleep studies, & cognitive variables such as hostility. Once we understand mechanisms, we can design interventions that can ameliorate these effects. 21 References. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Inc., copyright 2005.]

Socioeconomic Resources, Gender Traditionalism, and Wife Abuse in Urban Russian Couples

Author: Cubbins, Lisa A1; Vannoy, Dana1 Battelle Seattle Research Center, WA cubbinsl@battelle.org

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60506669?accountid=14925

Abstract: Until recently, Soviet data restrictions led to limited knowledge about wife abuse in Russia. This study adds to emerging research on Russian domestic violence by testing hypotheses derived from resource theory on the effects on wife abuse of husband's absolute resources vs spouses' relative resources. Analysis of data from the 1996 National Survey of Russian Marriages (N= 664) shows support only for the influence of husband's absolute socioeconomic resources (education, employment status, & occupational rank). As in US studies, intergenerational patterns of wife abuse & husband's alcohol use have notable effects on wife abuse. The findings suggest that although resource theory may partly explain wife abuse in urban Russia, spouses' relative resources & husband's gender traditionalism currently have little influence. 4 Tables, 54 References. Adapted from the source document.

A Paradigm for Developing a Comprehensive Treatment Protocol for Survivors of Domestic Violence

Author: Espinoza, Henry11 Pan American Health Organization, Washington, DC espinozh@paho.org

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61393888?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article proposes a paradigm for developing a comprehensive treatment protocol for survivors of domestic violence. Domestic violence includes varying forms of physical sexual, psychological, and economic abuse against women, and its solution requires more than the mere treatment of injuries in the health care system. Espinoza argues that the health care community, the police and justice system, as well as other sectors such as local government, churches, and the educational system must harmonize their prevention and treatment efforts to combat domestic violence. Despite enthusiasm for inter-agency collaboration, only limited cooperation has occurred. In order for services to be integrated, all of these different sectors must develop a common understanding of the problem of domestic violence, agreed upon goals, and share a common paradigm for joint work. Figures, References. D. Sabat

Perceptions of Dating Violence following a Sexual or Nonsexual Betrayal of Trust: Effects of Gender, Sexism, Acceptance of Rape Myths, and Vengeance Motivation

Author: Forbes, Gordon B1; Jobe, Rebecca L; White, Kay B; Bloesch, Emily; Adams-Curtis, Leah E1 Dept Behavioral Sciences, Millikin U, Decatur, IL gforbes@millikin.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60515604?accountid=14925

Abstract: College women s (N = 220) & men s (N = 208) perceptions of violence following a betrayal by a romantic partner were studied in an independent groups design that varied gender of the participant, type of betrayal (sexual or nonsexual), & gender of the betrayed individual. Participants read one of four brief vignettes in which the betrayed person slapped his/her partner & made his/her lip bleed. Justification of this action was rated on 10 items. Factor analysis of these items indicated the presence of three factors for men & four for women. Both men & women indicated that hitting the partner, getting even, & being angry were more justifiable following a sexual betrayal. The hitting of a male partner by a betrayed woman was perceived as more justified than the hitting of a female partner by a betrayed man. In absolute terms, hitting & getting even by both women & men were generally viewed as unjustifiable, whereas being angry was viewed as justifiable. For both women & men, scores on the Vengeance Scale (Stuckless & Goranson, 1992) & Rape Myth Acceptance Scale (Lonsway & Fitzgerald, 1995) were related to getting even. For men, the Hostile Sexism Scale (Glick & Fiske, 2001) was related to getting even or being angry & breaking up. Results indicated that perceptions of reactions to betrayal are related to type of betrayal & to the dispositional characteristics of the perceiver. 2 Tables, 41 References. Adapted from the source document.

Domestic Violence, Sexual Owernship, and HIV Risk in Women in the American Deep South

Author: Lichtenstein, Bronwen11 Dept Criminal Justice, Instit Rural Health Research, U Alabama, Tuscaloosa blichten@bama.ua.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60494620?accountid=14925

Abstract: Domestic violence & sexual abuse are important correlates of HIV risk in women. This paper examines the links between HIV risk & domestic violence in women in a region with the highest HIV/AIDS rates in the United States. The theoretical framework incorporates Butler's (1993) & (1990) concept of performative gender & Collins' (2000) "controlling images" of African American women as a context for domestic violence in the Deep South. Two focus groups were convened to develop a definition of domestic violence as HIV risk; 50 in-depth individual interviews of HIV-positive women were subsequently conducted for specific information on the topic. A final focus group was conducted for verification & feedback. The interview data revealed that controlling images of women as sexualized bodies were enacted through rape, sexual coercion, & name-calling in intimate relationships. The main finding was that the women lacked the ability to control sexual activities (including condom use) in abusive relationships with HIV-positive men. The women used various strategies to escape abusive partners & to obtain treatment for HIV/AIDS. The study concludes that the links between gender inequity, domestic violence, & HIV transmission should appear in prevention materials to encourage domestic violence screening in health settings, & to provide abused women with information on the not-so-obvious risks of being infected in abusive relationships. 1 Table, 37 References. [Copyright 2004 Elsevier Ltd.]

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=Domestic+Violence%2C+Sexual+Owernship%2C+and+HIV+Risk+in+Women+in+the+American+Deep+South&amp;title=Social+Science+%26+Medicine&amp;issn=02779536&amp;date=2005-02-01&amp;volume=60&amp;issue=4&amp;spage=701&amp;author=Lichtenstein%2C+Bronwen

Domestic Violence: Court Case Conviction and Recidivism

Author: Ventura, Lois A1; Davis, Gabrielle1 Dept Criminal Justice, Coll Health & Human Services, U Toledo

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61368352?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article examines the effect of convictions for domestic violence or a related charge on the recidivism of batterers. Findings indicate that convictions reduce the likelihood of domestic violence recidivism. The variance explained by the conviction variable is modest; however, conviction remains significant when the batterers' histories of domestic violence charges & other background variables are introduced. The article also explores the associations between batterers' background characteristics & court convictions, as well as the effect of sanctions on the recidivism of convicted batterers. Tables, References. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Inc., copyright 2005.]

Police-Reported Intimate Partner Violence during Pregnancy: Who Is at Risk?

Author: Lipsky, Sherry1; Holt, Victoria L; Easterling, Thomas R; Critchlow, Cathy W1 School Public Health, U Texas Houston, Dallas sherry.lipsky@utsouthwestern.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61345562?accountid=14925

Abstract: The objective of this retrospective cohort study was to determine risk factors for police-reported intimate partner violence (IPV) during pregnancy among Seattle residents with a registered live birth or fetal death in Washington State. Logistic regression was used to calculate adjusted odds ratios (aOR) & 95% confidence intervals for the association between demographic, behavioral, & obstetric history risk factors & any, physical, & nonphysical police-reported IPV. Significant risk factors for any police-reported IPV during pregnancy included unmarried status (aOR 2.36), public health program use (aOR 1.33), smoking or alcohol use during pregnancy (aORs 1.45 & 1.80, respectively), previous live birth (aOR 1.39), & previous spontaneous or induced abortion (aORs 1.39 & 1.34, respectively). Risk factors for physical IPV varied only slightly from those for any IPV, & fewer factors were associated with nonphysical IPV. Demographic, behavioral, & obstetric history risk factors are potential markers of IPV risk during pregnancy. 2 Tables, 1 Figure, 74 References. Adapted from the source document.

Beyond Gender: Class, Poverty and Domestic Violence

Author: Evans, Susan11 U Western Sydney evanss@wahs.nsw.gov.au

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61539835?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article argues there is a need for a more encompassing understanding of the relationships between domestic violence & all forms of marginalization in the Australian context. Specific attention will be given to the invisibility of class & poverty in contemporary discourse about domestic violence in the mainstream population. That there continues to be a higher prevalence of domestic violence, & more severe physical injury sustained as a result of domestic violence among population groups living with poverty, exposes the partiality of mainstream knowledges informing Australian domestic violence policy & practice. This article proposes prevention efforts must accommodate how class & the effects of poverty interlock with other aspects of social identity to shape the experience of domestic violence for people both victimized by, & perpetrating violence. 24 References. Adapted from the source document.

A Study in Social Change: The Domestic Violence Prevention Movement in Taiwan

Author: Chao, Elaine

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60513568?accountid=14925

Abstract: Taiwan has dramatically improved its response to domestic violence within the last fifteen years, becoming the first East Asian country to pass major legislation criminalizing domestic violence. Ethnographic research on the origins, development, & operations of the domestic violence prevention movement shows how individuals from diverse backgrounds acted collectively to achieve this social reversal. Activists have profited from feminist social networks, the growing economic & political power of women, domestic violence models from other countries, a unified vision of creating new legislation, & an atmosphere open to change. Their efforts were catalyzed by three widely publicized cases regarding violence against women & culminated in the passage of the Domestic Violence Prevention & Treatment Act in 1998. The domestic violence prevention movement in Taiwan provides a valuable case study of social change & a model for other East Asian countries interested in passing similar legislation. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=A+Study+in+Social+Change%3A+The+Domestic+Violence+Prevention+Movement+in+Taiwan&amp;title=Critical+Asian+Studies&amp;issn=14672715&amp;date=2005-03-01&amp;volume=37&amp;issue=1&amp;spage=29&amp;author=Chao%2C+Elaine

Barriers to Domestic Violence Help Seeking: Implications for Intervention

Author: Fugate, Michelle1; Landis, Leslie; Riordan, Kim; Naureckas, Sara; Engel, Barbara1 City Chicago Mayor's Office Domestic Violence

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60012803?accountid=14925

Abstract: Data were obtained from the Chicago Women's Health Risk Study, in which 491 abused women were interviewed in public health centers & a hospital. Responses of a subgroup of these women who did not seek help through the identified interventions are examined. Common themes emerge across the help-seeking interventions studied: individual thresholds for the seriousness of the violence, a perceived requirement to end the relationship, & certain specific barriers. The responses provide a glimpse into abused women's reasons for not seeking particular interventions. Implications for public health, mental health, domestic violence counseling agencies, & the police are discussed. Tables, References. Knowledge and Attitudes about Intimate Partner Violence among Immigrant Latinos in Rural North Carolina

Author: Moracco, Kathryn E1; Hilton, Alison; Hodges, Kathryn G; Frasier, Pamela Y1 Dept Health Behavior & Health Education, U North Carolina-Chapel Hill

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60010716?accountid=14925

Abstract: To create appropriate intimate partner violence (IPV) services for Latino immigrants, practitioners must be aware of their needs. We conducted interviews with 100 recent Latino immigrants in a rural North Carolina county. Overall, IPV was not perceived to be a problem; however, men & women differed in their perceptions. Men were more likely to agree with IPV myths, & both men & women felt that IPV had a detrimental impact on children. Many did not know about the local domestic violence agency, & knowledge about protective orders was limited. Outreach should emphasize the seriousness of IPV, adapt content for gender-specific audiences, & increase awareness about local resources. Tables, References. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Inc., copyright 2005.]

Individual, Family, and Community Risk Markers for Domestic Violence in Peru

Author: Flake, Dallan F11 U Michigan dflake@umich.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61368303?accountid=14925

Abstract: This study draws on an ecological framework in testing relationships between individual, family, & community characteristics & the likelihood of women experiencing domestic violence in Peru. The sample of 15,991 women was taken from the 2000 Peru Demographic & Health Survey. Logistic regression models revealed that at the individual level, low educational attainment, early union formation, & a violent family background increase a woman's likelihood of abuse. Family-level risk markers include cohabitation, large family size, partner alcohol consumption, employment, & a woman's having higher status than her husband. At the community level, living in a noncoastal area & having an urban residence increase the likelihood of abuse. Tables, References. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Inc., copyright 2005.]

An African-Centered Approach to Domestic Violence

Author: Bent-Goodley, Tricia B11 School Social Work, Howard U, Washington, D.C tbent-goodley@howard.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61294371?accountid=14925

Abstract: African-centered social work offers a set of principles upon which to develop a culturally competent response to domestic violence as experienced by African American families. Finding creative & relevant solutions for addressing domestic violence can keep family members safe, identify & address abuse, & keep children in the home when appropriate. Such efforts can promote greater awareness of healthy family relationships & the empowerment of individuals to shape their lives in a healthy & safe manner. In this article, in addition to examining principles of the African-centered paradigm, the author offers specific ways in which the paradigm can be applied to domestic violence. Tables, References. Victims of Chronic Dating Violence: How Women's Vulnerabilities Link to Their Decisions to Stay

Author: Few, April L1; Rosen, Karen H1 Dept Human Development, Blacksburg, VA alfew@vt.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61513366?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article describes relational & situational vulnerabilities that emerged from interviews with 28 women (7 Black & 21 White) who were victims of chronic abuse suffered at the hands of male dating partners. Based on a qualitative content analysis, we explore how these vulnerabilities relate to women's decisions to stay in their abusive relationships & consider the implications of racial differences that emerged. Prevention & intervention strategies that help women see themselves as active choice makers are discussed. 1 Table, 1 Figure, 43 References. Adapted from the source document.

Life Constraints and Psychological Well-Being of Domestic Violence Shelter Graduates

Author: Ham-Rowbottom, Kathleen A; Gordon, Erin E; Jarvis, Kelly L; Novaco, Raymond W11 University of California, Irvine, California, 92697-7085, rwnovaco@uci.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/811408281?accountid=14925

Abstract: Psychosocial adjustment and life constraints of 81 domestic violence shelter graduates were examined via field interviews in the community, assessing women's current life status, satisfaction with core life domains, and violence experience, pre- and post-shelter. Psychometric scales for depression and trauma symptoms were also administered. Participants had received extensive services in either an emergency or a transitional living shelter. Although fairly satisfied across life domains, many had serious post-shelter financial hardships. Most importantly, they reported remarkably little post-shelter violence exposure, either within or outside of romantic relationships. Despite now living independently, 43% and 75% reported clinical levels of depression and trauma symptoms, respectively. In hierarchical stepwise regressions, depression was related to women's childhood sexual abuse, dissatisfaction with housing and their own parenting, and experience of financial difficulties conjoined with public assistance. Trauma symptoms were associated with childhood sexual abuse and post-shelter financial difficulties. The impaired psychosocial functioning and life difficulties of these predominantly successful domestic violence survivors highlights the need for specialized shelter intervention and continuity of care in the community.

Domestic Violence Research: What Have We Learned and Where Do We Go from Here?

Author: Stover, Carla Smith11 National Center Children Exposed Violence, Yale School Medicine Child Study Center

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61536121?accountid=14925

Abstract: Domestic violence has been an intense area of study in recent decades. Early studies helped with the understanding of the nature of perpetration, the cycle of violence, & the effect of family violence on children. More recently, studies have focused on beginning to evaluate domestic violence interventions & their effects on recidivism. This article acknowledges the importance of what we have learned about the prevalence & impact of domestic violence & explores the need for more focused effort to pinpoint interventions that are effective with perpetrators & victims. Methodological issues relevant to past intervention studies are also discussed & future research directions are outlined. 28 References. Author: Field, Craig A1; Caetano, Raul1 Dallas Regional Campus, School Public Health, U Texas, Houston

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60509313?accountid=14925

Measuring Intimate Partner Violence (IPV): You May Only Get What You Ask for

Author: Waltermaurer, Eve11 Sociology Dept, State U New York, New Paltz

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60540261?accountid=14925

Abstract: With the goal of understanding the true extent of intimate partner violence (IPV), researchers have put tremendous effort over the past 20 years developing, revising, & assessing IPV screening instruments. The enhancements made in IPV instrumentation reflect our improved understanding of the nature of IPV. Unfortunately, as is often the case with progress, we are presently at the stage where IPV researchers have an arsenal of multiple IPV screens that are, in some cases, slightly different, whereas in others, the differences are sizeable. This article explores the evolution & variation of a sample of IPV screens. To further progress in IPV research, we must make conscious decisions concerning the best tool for our individual research. Simultaneously, we must enhance our understanding of how these IPV screening instruments overlap so that comparisons of IPV prevalence or incidence across time & population are possible. 27 References. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Inc., copyright 2005.]

Men's and Women's Use of Intimate Partner Violence in Clinical Samples: Toward a Gender-Sensitive Analysis

Author: Hamberger, L Kevin11 Dept Family & Community Medicine, Medical Coll Wisconsin, Racine Family Practice Center lkh@ncw.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61339216?accountid=14925

Abstract: Early research with nationally representative samples suggested that women reported initiating violence as often as men. Such research has been criticized as focusing only on participation rates, & not assessing gender differences in impact, context, & motivation for using partner violence. Furthermore, research with nationally representative samples has been largely a-theoretical & may lack relevance to those working with clinical samples. Research with clinical populations has begun to address gender similarity & differences in the commission & experience of partner violence. The present article reviews research on men's & women's partner violence using a model for examining such gender differences that incorporates key elements of partner violence, including initiation of the overall pattern of partner violence, proportional initiation rates of violent episodes, physical & mental health impacts of partner violence, behavioral & emotional responses to partner-initiated violence, motivations for using partner violence, & fearfulness of partner-initiated violence. The review concludes that, within & across clinical samples, women are disproportionately victimized by partner violence compared to men. Implications for research, clinical programs, & policy development are discussed. 69 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=Men%27s+and+Women%27s+Use+of+Intimate+Partner+Violence+in+Clinical+Samples%3A+Toward+a+Gender-Sensitive+Analysis&amp;title=Violence+and+Victims&amp;issn=08866708&amp;date=2005-04-01&amp;volume=20&amp;issue=2&amp;spage=131&amp;author=Hamberger%2C+L+Kevin

A Comparison of Male and Female Dually Arrested Domestic Violence Offenders

Author: Feder, Lynette1; Henning, Kris1 Portland State U, OR lfeder@pdx.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61328954?accountid=14925

Abstract: This research provides information on dually arrested female defendants in domestic violence cases. The data comes from all heterosexual domestic violence cases resulting in a dual arrest from one large mid-South city during a 28-month period. Information on matched pairs of dually arrested male & female defendants (N = 317) came from multiple sources, including official criminal justice records (arrest reports & local criminal histories) as well as victim/offender interviews with both the arrestee & his/her partner. Comparisons were made between males & females in terms of their demographics, criminal histories, prior history of domestic violence (as both victim & offender), & behavior during the instant offense. Analyses revealed that these couples had ample contact, whether as victim or offender, with the criminal justice system. While there were no significant differences in partners' reports of minor & severe physical abuse, criminal justice date indicated that male arrestees demonstrated a significantly higher overall level of severity of violence than their female counterparts. Additionally, male arrestees had a more serious history of intimate partner & extrafamilial violence as well as other indicators of an antisocial lifestyle in comparison to female arrestees. To the extent that females in our cohort demonstrated less overall criminality & engaged in less severe domestic aggression, the idea of equivalency of violence between male & female arrestees must remain questionable. 4 Tables, 61 References. Adapted from the source document.

Exerting Power or Striking Back: A Gendered Comparison of Motivations for Domestic Violence Perpetration

Author: Kernsmith, Poco11 School Social Work, Wayne State U, Detroit, MI poco@wayne.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60095809?accountid=14925

Abstract: This study compares the motivations for using violence & the context in which violence occurs among male & female domestic violence perpetrators. One hundred twenty-five participants in batterer intervention counseling in Los Angeles County, California, were surveyed. The sample was approximately half male & half female. Males & females were found to differ in their motivations for using violence in relationships. Females reported using violence in response to prior abuse, citing revenge & retaliation as a primary motivation. Because treatment approaches are commonly derived from the power & control model, which indicates that violence is used to gain power & control, batterer intervention counseling may not be appropriate for women, who appear more motivated by the desire to maintain personal liberties in a relationship where they have been victimized. Treatment must walk the fine line between addressing victimization issues & holding women accountable for decisions to use violence. 2 Tables, 1 Figure, 45 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=Exerting+Power+or+Striking+Back%3A+A+Gendered+Comparison+of+Motivations+for+Domestic+Violence+Perpetration&amp;title=Violence+and+Victims&amp;issn=08866708&amp;date=2005-04-01&amp;volume=20&amp;issue=2&amp;spage=173&amp;author=Kernsmith%2C+Poco

Domestic Violence among Male and Female Patients Seeking Emergency Medical Services

Author: Phelan, Mary Beth1; Hamberger, L Kevin; Guse, Clare E; Edwards, Shauna; Walczak, Suzanne; Zosel, Amy1 Dept Emergency Medicine, Medical Coll Wisconsin, Milwaukee mbphelan@mcw.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60108832?accountid=14925

Abstract: Gender differences among a cohort of injured patients seeking emergency medical services were examined with respect to their experiences as perpetrators &/or victims of domestic violence. Contextual issues, including violence initiation, emotional & behavioral responses to partner-initiated violence, & injury frequency & severity were analyzed. Women reported male partner-initiated violence more frequently than men reported female partner-initiated violence. Behavioral responses to partner initiated violence varied. Women were more likely to report using force back & to involve law enforcement. Women were more likely to be injured in a domestic assault over their lifetime, within the last year, & at the time of recruitment. Comparison of injury severity revealed that women reported higher rates of injuries than men in all possible severity categories. Women also reported experiencing more fear than men during partner-initiated violence, as well as being subjected to larger numbers of dominating & controlling behaviors, & greater intimidation secondary to their partner's size. Understanding contextual differences in partner violence for women & men has significant implications for policy development, identification, treatment, & referral of patients identified as living in violent relationships. 6 Tables, 47 References. Adapted from the source document.

Gender Symmetry in Dating Intimate Partner Violence: Does Similar Behavior Imply Similar Constructs?

Author: Cercone, Jennifer J1; Beach, Steven R H; Arias, Ileana1 Dept Psychology, U Georgia, Athens jcercone@uga.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60105563?accountid=14925

Abstract: The present study examined the extent to which there is gender symmetry in the topography & experience of dating intimate partner violence (IPV). Self-report data were collected from 450 undergraduate men & women at a large Southeastern university. Perpetration & victimization rates were examined, as were context, function, & experience of fear. Results support the view that dating IPV is generally symmetrical at a topographical level, although significantly more women than men reported perpetration of severe physical assault. However, gender asymmetries were found in the context, function, & experience of fear. These findings suggest that gender-sensitive approaches are crucial to the understanding of dating IPV. 2 Tables, 2 Figures, 37 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=Gender+Symmetry+in+Dating+Intimate+Partner+Violence%3A+Does+Similar+Behavior+Imply+Similar+Constructs%3F&amp;title=Violence+and+Victims&amp;issn=08866708&amp;date=2005-04-01&amp;volume=20&amp;issue=2&amp;spage=207&amp;author=Cercone%2C+Jennifer+J%3BBeach%2C+Steven+R+H%3BArias%2C+Ileana

Applying the Transtheoretical Model to Female and Male Perpetrators of Intimate Partner Violence: Gender Differences in Stages and Processes of Change

Author: Babcock, Julia C1; Canady, Brittany E; Senior, Ashley; Eckhardt, Christopher I1 Dept Psychology, U Houston, TX jbabcock@uh.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60097627?accountid=14925

Abstract: The Transtheoretical Model has been recently applied to men seeking services at battering intervention & prevention programs (Eekhardt, Babcock, & Homack, 2004; Murphy & Baxter, 1997). This study considers whether women arrested for intimate partner violence differ from male perpetrators in terms of stages of change & processes of change. No gender differences were found regarding stage of change. In general, all individuals presenting for treatment were in the early stages of change. The use of various processes of change was strongly related to stage of change, with individuals in the more advanced stages of change using more behavioral & experiential strategies to become nonviolent. However, except for the use of social liberation strategies, there were no gender differences in the use of the various processes. These findings suggest that the Transtheoretical Model may apply to female perpetrators equally as well as to male perpetrators. Intervention programs designed for male batterers using the Transtheoretical Model may also be helpful in the treatment of women arrested for domestic violence. 4 Tables, 1 Figure, 59 References. Adapted from the source document.

Author: Cramer, Elizabeth P11 School Social Work, Virginia Commonwealth U, Richmond

Abstract: Offers an overview of the concepts paradigm, theory, models, & techniques related to interpersonal violence (IPV). Changing Practices: The Specialised Domestic Violence Court Process

Author: Eley, Susan11 U Stirling, Scotland

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60506126?accountid=14925

Abstract: Specialized domestic violence courts, initially developed in the United States of America, have been recognized by other jurisdictions including Canada, Australia & the United Kingdom. This article presents a case study of K Court in Toronto, drawing upon documentary evidence, direct observations & interviews with key informants. It is argued that the specialized domestic violence court process includes changing practices of some of the key stakeholders. Learning lessons from abroad can offer jurisdictions insights that can steer implementation of appropriate practices in the field. 35 References. Adapted from the source document.

An Integrative Review of Intimate Partner Violence among Men Who Have Sex with Men: Correlates of Victimization and Development of a Conceptual Framework

Author: Roberts, Jeffrey A11 U Kentucky

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/59989229?accountid=14925

Abstract: Provides a systematic, integrative review of the literature regarding correlates of & interventions for intimate partner violence among men who have sex with men (MSM-IPV). Correlates include HIV/AIDS, substance use, relationship dynamics, power imbalances, homophobia, mental health, & help seeking & leave taking. A conceptual framework of MSM-IPV that synthesizes the extant MSM-IPV & appropriate heterosexual IPV literatures is presented. Gaps in the literature are apparent; future research directions are suggested, & the implications of MS-IPV as a public health issue are touched on. Figures, References. J. Zendejas

Domestic Violence and the Indian Diaspora in the United States

Author: Abraham, Margaret11 Dept Sociology, Hofstra U, Hempstead, NY margaret.abraham@hofstra.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/59996085?accountid=14925

Abstract: The profile of the current Asian Indian population in the United States is largely an outcome of immigration policies & practices. For most immigrants, including Asian Indians, gender, class & ethnic relations get reshaped as women & men adapt to life in a foreign country. This article discusses domestic violence among the Indian diaspora in the United States with insights from the staff of the community-based women's organisation, Sakhi for South Asian Women, in New York City. It provides a general overview of the Indian population in the United States & explains how immigration policies & regulations factor into immigrant women's experiences of marriage, migration, & marital & domestic violence in a community that has been perceived as a model minority in the United States. Notions of cultural identity, family values & gender, as well as the forms of marriage & violence, are briefly examined. Emphasis is placed on the pivotal role played by Sakhi for South Asian Women in transforming domestic violence from an individual, private matter into a public issue. Tables, References.
