Acculturation and Reported Intimate Partner Violence among Latinas in Los Angeles

Author: Garcia, Lorena1; Hurwitz, Eric L; Kraus, Jess F1 U California, Davis lgarcia@ucdavis.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60508738?accountid=14925

Abstract: This study sought to understand the relationship between acculturation & reporting intimate partner violence (IPV) among Latinas. A cross-sectional interviewer-administered survey was conducted at public health care clinics throughout Los Angeles County. Logistic regression was used to estimate the effect of acculturation on reporting IPV. An increasing trend of reporting IPV was observed among Latinas who were more acculturated (chi-square = 41.02, p = .0006). Highly acculturated Latinas were more likely to report IPV compared with least acculturated Latinas (prevalence odds ration = 2.18, 95% confidence level = 0.98, 4.89) & moderately acculturated Latinas were more likely to report IPV compared with least acculturated Latinas (prevalence odds ration = 1.29, 95% confidence level = 0.69, 2.43). Culturally competent IPV prevention programs may be the key to significantly reducing the number of women exposed to this serious public health problem. 4 Tables, 2 Figures, 69 References. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Inc., copyright 2005.]

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=Acculturation+and+Reported+Intimate+Partner+Violence+among+Latinas+in+Los+Angeles&amp;title=Journal+of+Interpersonal+Violence&amp;issn=08862605&amp;date=2005-05-01&amp;volume=20&amp;issue=5&amp;spage=569&amp;author=Garcia%2C+Lorena%3BHurwitz%2C+Eric+L%3BKraus%2C+Jess+F

Examining the Impact of the Family Violence Option on Women's Efforts to Leave Welfare

Author: Hetling, Andrea1; Born, Catherine E1 University of Maryland, Baltimore

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/813859691?accountid=14925

Abstract: Objective: The establishment of the Family Violence Option (FVO) in 1997 was met with some controversy, as critics believed waivers from time limit and work requirements would hinder women's ability to leave welfare and find employment. Method: Using administrative and interview data from Maryland, multivariate equations analyze if domestic violence disclosure, administrative documentation, or waiver use had a statistically significant affect on one year employment and welfare use outcomes of individuals. Results: Waiver holders did not differ fromnonvictims, but victims who are not documented received fewer months of welfare and earned less income. Conclusions: Findings do not indicate that FVOwaivers encourage women to stay on welfare longer. However, the poor outcomes of undocumented victims indicate that some individuals may be slipping through the cracks of a well-intentioned policy.

Community Level Effects of Gender Inequality on Intimate Partner Violence and Unintended Pregnancy in Colombia: Testing the Feminist Perspective

Author: Pallitto, Christina C1; O'Campo, Patricia1 Dept Population & Family Health Sciences, Johns Hopkins Bloomberg School Public Health, Baltimore, MD

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/59976179?accountid=14925

Abstract: Violence against women, especially by intimate partners, is a serious public health problem that is associated with physical, reproductive, & mental health consequences. The effect of intimate partner violence on women's ability to control their fertility & the mechanisms through which these phenomena are related merit further investigation. Building on findings from a previous analysis in which a statistically significant relationship between intimate partner violence & unintended pregnancy in Colombia was found, this analysis examines the effect of gender inequality on this association using data from the 2000 Colombian Demographic & Health Survey. Specifically, the objective of this analysis is to explore whether gender inequality (as measured by women's autonomy, women's status, male patriarchal control, & intimate partner violence) in municipalities partially explains the association between intimate partner violence & unintended pregnancy in Colombia. Results of logistic regression analysis with multi-level data show that living in a municipality with high rates of male patriarchal control significantly increased women's odds of having an unintended pregnancy by almost four times. Also, living in a municipality with high rates of intimate partner violence increased one's odds of unintended pregnancy by more than 2.5 times, & non-abused women living in municipalities with high rates of intimate partner violence were at a significantly increased risk of unintended pregnancy. In addition, abused women living in a municipality with high personal female decision-making autonomy had more than a fourfold increased risk of having an unintended pregnancy. These findings demonstrate the need for reproductive health programs to target areas at particularly high risk for unintended pregnancy by reducing intimate partner violence & gender inequality. 4 Tables, 66 References. [Copyright 2004 Elsevier Ltd.]

A Situational Analysis of Dating Violence among Mexican American Females Associated with Street Gangs

Author: Valdez, Avelardo1; Flores, Raquel1 Graduate School Social Work, U Houston, TX

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61533577?accountid=14925

Abstract: This study focuses on situational processes associated with dating partner violence among adolescent Mexican American females in an economically disadvantaged community. Using qualitative data generated from a specific question in a life history instrument, the questionnaire was administered to 150 high-risk Mexican American gang-affiliated female respondents (ages 14 -18). To generate the analysis, the data were coded using the constructs of precursor, instigation, & violent confrontation. Identified as the precursors of these confrontations were jealousy, control & disrespect. The findings demonstrate how situational processes in dating violence are framed by gender & social structural inequality. These micro-level processes are important in that they suggest mechanisms & properties that can contribute to a more nuanced understanding of violence as it relates to the situational context & individual behavior. 75 References. Adapted from the source document.

Sita's Trousseau: Restorative Justice, Domestic Violence, and South Asian Culture

Author: Goel, Rashmi11 Sturm Coll Law, U Denver

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61380070?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article focuses on the particular cultural factors that affect South Asian women who are abused & immigrant South Asian women who are abused, in particular, in the restorative justice process. By exploring cultural practices & the icon of Sita, the mythological heroine of the Ramayana, this article demonstrates how the South Asian ideals of womanhood & wifehood help to create a mind-set whereby South Asian women are reluctant to advocate for themselves & are reluctant to leave. Such a condition is contrary to the conditions & abilities assumed by the restorative justice movement for dispute resolution, inside or outside of domestic violence. It is concluded that restorative justice options are ill-suited to application among immigrant South Asian communities for domestic violence cases. References. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Inc., copyright 2005.]

'I didn't do it, but if I did I had a good reason': Minimization, Denial, and Attributions of Blame Among Male and Female Domestic Violence Offenders

Author: Henning, Kris1; Jones, Angela R; Holdford, Robert1 Portland State University, PO Box 751, Portland, Oregon, 97202, khenning@pdx.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/811408696?accountid=14925

Abstract: Women are increasingly being arrested and prosecuted for assaulting an intimate partner. Whereas extensive research has been conducted to identify the treatment needs of male domestic violence offenders, few studies have examined females convicted of the same charges. In the present study 1,267 men and 159 women convicted of intimate partner abuse were compared on scales assessing attributions of blame for their recent offense, minimization, denial, and socially desirable responding. Research with male offenders has identified these factors as important treatment targets, as they appear to influence an offender's risk for noncompliance and recidivism. The results of the study suggest that both male and female domestic violence offenders engage in socially desirable responding during court-ordered evaluations, that both attribute greater blame for the recent offense to their spouse/partner than they acknowledge for themselves, and that significant numbers of both genders deny the recent incident and/or minimize the severity of the offense. Areas for further research are highlighted along with a discussion of the implications of these findings for practitioners.

What's So Special about Specialized Courts? The State and Social Change in Salt Lake City's Domestic Violence Court

Author: Mirchandani, Rakha11 Dept Sociology, Bowling Green State U, OH rmircha@bgnet.bgsu.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60096406?accountid=14925

Abstract: The nationwide growth in specialized or problem-solving courts, including drug courts, community courts, mental health courts, & domestic violence courts, among others, raises questions about the role of the state with respect to social change. According to social control theories of the state, especially theories of technocratic or rationalized Justice, law is increasingly about efficiency, speed, & effectiveness. Specialized courts, however, take on a social problem approach to crime, seeking to address crime's "root causes" within the individual, the society, & the larger culture in ways more characteristic of social movements. Are specialized courts about social control or social change? This study examines state action in a specialized court in domestic violence in order to examine this question. I focus on a domestic violence court that arose in February 1997 & four years later employed full-time judges. prosecuting & defense attorneys, & numerous other staff to handle all misdemeanor domestic violence cases in Salt Lake County, Utah. I ask how legal, political, & community officials justify the court & its operation in order to examine some important issues about the role of the state & social change. Ultimately, I suggest that my findings about the complementary roles of social control & social change within domestic violence courts have implications not only for critical theories of technocratic justice & for the battered women's movement but also for democratic theories of the state. 103 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=What%27s+So+Special+about+Specialized+Courts%3F+The+State+and+Social+Change+in+Salt+Lake+City%27s+Domestic+Violence+Court&amp;title=Law+%26+Society+Review&amp;issn=00239216&amp;date=2005-06-01&amp;volume=39&amp;issue=2&amp;spage=379&amp;author=Mirchandani%2C+Rakha

Coercion in Intimate Partner Violence: Toward a New Conceptualization

Author: Dutton, Mary Ann1; Goodman, Lisa A1 Dept Psychiatry, Georgetown U Medical Center, Washington, DC

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60110090?accountid=14925

Abstract: For decades, battered women's advocates have placed coercive control squarely at the center of their analysis of intimate partner violence. Yet, little work has been done to conceptualize & measure the key construct of coercive control. In this article, we apply French & Raven's social power model to a conceptualization of coercive control in intimate partner violence relationships. Central elements of the model include: social ecology; setting the stage; coercion involving a demand & a credible threat for noncompliance; surveillance; delivery of threatened consequences; & the victim's behavioral & emotional response to coercion. These elements occur in spiraling & overlapping sequences to establish an overall situation of coercive control. The implications of this model for theory & practice are discussed. 1 Figure, 64 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=Coercion+in+Intimate+Partner+Violence%3A+Toward+a+New+Conceptualization&amp;title=Sex+Roles%3A+A+Journal+of+Research&amp;issn=03600025&amp;date=2005-06-01&amp;volume=52&amp;issue=11-12&amp;spage=743&amp;author=Dutton%2C+Mary+Ann%3BGoodman%2C+Lisa+A

Treating Perpetrators of Domestic Violence: Gender Differences in the Applicability of the Theory of Planned Behavior

Author: Kernsmith, Poco11 School Social Work, Wayne State U, Detroit, MI poco@wayne.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60099823?accountid=14925

Abstract: Female perpetrated intimate partner violence has commonly been treated, both legally & clinically, similarly to male perpetrated violence. However, there is little empirical research of the gender differences in treatment needs or the applicability of classic models of batterer intervention for women. This study examines the applicability of one theory that has commonly been used to guide treatment of male perpetrators, the theory of planned behavior. R. M. Tolman, J. L. Edleson, & M. Fendrich (1996) found the components of this theory to be moderately predictive of abusive behavior among males. In a survey of 114 male & female participants in batterer intervention counseling in Los Angeles County, the current study confirmed that the model predicted male behaviors but found the model did not apply to female violent behavior. 6 Tables, 52 References. Adapted from the source document.

An Adult Attachment Theoretical Perspective of Gender Symmetry in Intimate Partner Violence

Author: Gormley, Barbara11 School Education, U Miami, Coral Gables, FL b.gormley@miami.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60110116?accountid=14925

Abstract: Adult attachment theory & research specifically related to men's & women's intimate partner violence (IPV) are reviewed. In an effort to help explain gender similarities, two different IPV patterns predicted by individual differences in adult attachment orientations are proposed. Gender differences are addressed, including by critiquing the assessment of outcome severity in previous research. Applications to practice & recommendations for future research are discussed. 80 References. Adapted from the source document.

Understanding Gender and Intimate Partner Violence from an International Perspective

Author: Krahe, Barbara1; Bieneck, Steffen; Moller, Ingrid1 Dept Psychology, U Potsdam, Germany krahe@rz.uni-potsdam.de

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60110169?accountid=14925

Abstract: This paper reviews the international literature on intimate partner violence with a focus on gender differences in perpetration & victimization rates. A total of 35 studies from 21 countries are discussed that report prevalence or incidence rates of men's & women's involvement in physical &/or sexual aggression against an intimate partner. In addition, evidence on risk factors as well as consequences of intimate partner violence for men & women is presented. Conceptual & methodological differences between the studies & the lack of comparable databases within countries are discussed as limitations of the evidence, & perspectives for future research are outlined in the framework of cross-cultural psychology. 4 Tables, 71 References. Adapted from the source document.

Theorizing Gender in Intimate Partner Violence Research

Author: Anderson, Kristin L11 Dept Sociology, Western Washington U, Bellingham Kristin.Anderson@wwu.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60091856?accountid=14925

Abstract: Research findings of sex-symmetry in the perpetration of intimate partner assaults have sparked vigorous debate about the appropriate definition & measurement of intimate violence. A neglected but central issue in this debate is the conceptualization & measurement of gender. This article first examines the often unstated theoretical perspective on gender that underlies the research on sex-symmetry in intimate partner violence. This perspective treats gender as an individual characteristic of persons. Next, I describe challenges to the individualist model of gender from two emerging theoretical perspectives -- interactionist & structuralist gender theories. The article concludes with suggestions for research on intimate partner violence that are informed by these new gender theories. 65 References. Adapted from the source document.

The Interaction of Country of Origin, Acculturation, and Gender Role Ideology on Wife Abuse

Author: Harris, Richard J1; Firestone, Juanita M; Vega, William A1 The University of Texas, San Antonio, Richard.Harris@utsa.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/807903965?accountid=14925

Abstract: Objective: Using data from the Mexican American Prevalence and Services Survey (), this research tests whether the impact of acculturation and gender role ideology on wife abuse depends on country of origin. Methods: Two separate logistic regressions, one for U.S.-born Latinas and one for Mexican-born Latinas, are compared to test the impact of the interaction of place of origin with the other variables. Results: Our findings support earlier research indicating that power dynamics within a relationship impact the likelihood of a wife reporting she has experienced abuse. Significant differences in the influence of independent variables are found when comparing U.S.- and Mexican-born respondents. In particular, variables related to family power dynamics operate differently. Gender role beliefs, however, have an independent influence for both groups after controlling for sociodemographic factors and power dynamics. Women with more traditional orientations are less likely to report abuse. Conclusions: Because the impact of gender role ideology is significant and in the same direction for both those born in the United States and those born in Mexico, it is unlikely that the traditional familism and gender role orientations reported among the Mexican born afford them greater protection against abuse.

http://GS6DU7GB6H.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=The+Interaction+of+Country+of+Origin%2C+Acculturation%2C+and+Gender+Role+Ideology+on+Wife+Abuse&amp;author=Harris%2C+Richard+J%3BFirestone%2C+Juanita+M%3BVega%2C+William+A&amp;issn=00384941&amp;title=Social+Science+4&amp;volume=86&amp;issue=2&amp;date=2005-06-01&amp;spage=463&amp;id=doi:10.1111%2Fj.0038-4941.2005.00313.x&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

How Do Changes in Welfare Law Affect Domestic Violence? An Analysis of Connecticut Towns, 1990-2000

Author: Nou, Jennifer1; Timmins, Christopher1 Nuffield Coll, Oxford U

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61372174?accountid=14925

Abstract: The 1996 Personal Responsibility & Work Opportunity Reconciliation Act transformed welfare from an ongoing cash assistance program by restricting participation through time limits & emphasizing rapid entrance into the labor force. Changes in welfare dependency induced by these legal reforms had the potential to impact rates of domestic violence. Using decennial census, welfare caseload, & police report data, this paper investigates the introduction of time limits & work mandates across Connecticut towns. For a variety of reasons, Connecticut proves to be an ideal laboratory from which to obtain evidence. We find that rates of domestic violence fell in Connecticut with the passage of the new welfare law, especially in towns most subject to the law's provisions. Using Federal Bureau of Investigation Uniform Crime Report data, we also find evidence that such results hold independent of the reform's effects on other personal crimes & crimes in general. Tables, References. Adapted from the source document.

Training Police Officers on Domestic Violence and Racism

Author: Huisman, Kimberly1; Martinez, Jeri; Wilson, Cathleen1 U Maine

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61371998?accountid=14925

Abstract: Teaching about domestic violence & racism is a formidable task that requires the inclusion of material that challenges dominant ideologies about race & gender. A recent training for police officers about domestic violence & racism serves as a good example of how challenging the task can be. Drawing from this 1-day police training, from existing pedagogical literature, & from the authors' collective experiences teaching about these issues, the authors address challenges encountered & propose strategies to mitigate them. The authors propose a multilevel approach to implementing strategies to address these challenges, which includes ongoing involvement during three stages of the training. References. Female-Perpetrated Intimate Partner Violence and Romantic Attachment Style in a College Student Sample

Author: Orcutt, Holly K1; Garcia, Marilyn; Pickett, Scott M1 Dept Psychology, Northern Illinois U, DeKalb horcutt@niu.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61295048?accountid=14925

Abstract: The frequency, severity, & reciprocity of female-perpetrated intimate partner violence & its consequences (i.e., injuries) were investigated in a college sample of women (N = 457). Participants were classified into one of the following four groups on the basis of self reported physical assault perpetration & victimization against their relationship partners: nonviolent, perpetrator-only, victim-only, & bi-directionally violent. Results showed that females in the bi-directionally violent group had a reportedly higher occurrence (although not always statistically significant) of perpetration & victimization than those in the perpetrator-only & victim-only groups. Additionally, a similar degree of reciprocity was indicated by females in bi-directionally violent relationships in terms of violence severity & the occurrence of injury. Adult romantic attachment style was also examined among a subset of females (N = 328), & bi-directionally violent females were found to have the highest reported levels of attachment anxiety. Further, females high in attachment anxiety & low in attachment avoidance were more likely to report perpetrating violence than females high in both styles. Implications for prevention are discussed. Tables, Figures, References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=Female-Perpetrated+Intimate+Partner+Violence+and+Romantic+Attachment+Style+in+a+College+Student+Sample&amp;title=Violence+and+Victims&amp;issn=08866708&amp;date=2005-06-01&amp;volume=20&amp;issue=3&amp;spage=287&amp;author=Orcutt%2C+Holly+K%3BGarcia%2C+Marilyn%3BPickett%2C+Scott+M

Typology of Reactions to Intimate Partner Violence among Men and Women Arrested for Partner Violence

Author: Hamberger, L Kevin1; Guse, Clare1 Racine Family Practice Center, WI

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60049358?accountid=14925

Abstract: This study examined behavioral & emotional responses to partner-initiated violence reported by men & women court-ordered to domestic violence counseling. Respondents provided Likert-type ratings of behavioral & emotional responses to their partners' initiated violence. Cluster analysis to determine heterogeneity of emotional & behavioral responses resulted in a three-cluster solution. The profile for Cluster 1, predominantly male, showed no specific behavioral or emotional reaction pattern. Cluster 2 respondents, evenly split between males & females, but comprising a high proportion of all of the female participants, reported frequently doing what the partner wanted & attempting to escape. Emotional responses experienced by Cluster 2 respondents were anger, insult, & fear. Cluster 3, predominantly male, reported a frequent tendency to use force in response & escape when their partners initiated physical violence. Emotionally, Cluster 3 participants reported experiencing high levels of anger & insult, & relatively low levels of fear & low levels of amusement. Implications of these findings for development of gender based intervention strategies will be discussed. Tables, Figures, References. Adapted from the source document.

Prosecution of Women Arrested for Intimate Partner Abuse

Author: Henning, Kris1; Renauer, Brian1 Administration Justice, Portland State U, OR khenning@pdx.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60057229?accountid=14925

Abstract: Increasing arrests of women for domestic violence (DV) in the wake of mandatory arrest laws have generated significant concern among victim advocates, researchers, & practitioners. It is commonly believed that many, if not most, of the women arrested are victims who were acting in self-defense. Understanding how these cases are handled by prosecutors & judges is important if we wish to minimize the negative consequences of these errant arrests. The present study examines factors prosecutors in a large southern city considered when accepting or rejecting DV cases involving female defendants. The results indicate that almost one half (47%) of the cases involving women arrested for DV against a heterosexual intimate partner were rejected by prosecutors; another 16% were dismissed by a judge. Legal factors like a defendant's prior criminal arrests, use of a weapon, victim injury, &, most important, the type of arrest (i.e., dual vs. single arrest) all affected prosecutors' decisions to take these cases. Female defendants arrested for offending against a male intimate partner were treated more leniently than male defendants & women arrested for domestic offenses involving other types of relationships (i.e., familial, homosexual). The results highlight the need for a further study of officers' arrest decisions in cases involving heterosexual intimate partners. Tables, References. Adapted from the source document.

Psychosocial Correlates of Dating Violence Victimization among Latino Youth

Author: Howard, Donna E1; Beck, Kenneth; Kerr, Melissa Hallmark; Shattuck, Teresa1 Dept Public & Community Health, U Maryland, College Park dhowardI@umail.end.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60104500?accountid=14925

Abstract: To examine the association between physical dating violence victimization & risk & protective factors, an anonymous, cross-sectional, self-reported survey was administered to Latino youth (n = 446) residing in suburban Washington, DC. Multivariate logistic regression models were constructed, & adjusted OR & 95% CI were examined. Approximately 9% of Latino adolescents reported physical dating violence victimization. Overall, youth who reported carrying a gun, involvement in physical fights & suicidal thoughts were at greater odds of reporting dating violence. Among females, fighting was the sole risk behavior associated with dating violence. Girls who reported a stronger sense of self were less likely to report dating violence. Among males, gun carrying, but not physical fighting, & having considered suicide were associated with dating violence. Spending time each week with a mentor was also positively associated with male dating violence victimization. Dating violence appeared to cluster with other risk behavior engagement. Important gender differences in associated risk & protective behaviors were identified & should be incorporated into primary & secondary prevention activities. 3 Tables, 36 References. Adapted from the source document.

Addressing Intimate Partner Violence in Substance-Abuse Treatment

Author: Fals-Stewart, William1; Kennedy, Cheryl1 Research Triangle Instit, RTI International, Research Triangle Park, NC

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61381220?accountid=14925

Abstract: As the use of partner-involved therapies for alcoholism & drug abuse become more common in substance-abuse treatment programs, providers are more frequently encountering one of the most challenging & politically charged public health issues of our time: intimate partner violence (IPV). Recent investigations reveal 40-60% of married or cohabiting substance-abusing patients report episodes of partner aggression in the year preceding entry into treatment. In this article, the interrelationship between substance use & IPV is examined, with an emphasis on clinical implications & options for substance-abuse treatment providers who are often called upon to address IPV during the course of working with their patients. We also describe different intervention options for IPV, offer recommendations for substance-abuse treatment providers who work with partner-violent couples, & outline future research directions. Tables, References. [Copyright 2005 Elsevier Inc.]

HIV and Intimate Partner Violence among Methadone-Maintained Women in New York City

Author: El-Bassel, Nabila1; Gilbert, Louisa; Wu, Elwin; Go, Hyun; Hill, Jennifer1 Social Intervention Group, Columbia U School Social Work, NY

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61367289?accountid=14925

Abstract: Intimate partner violence (IPV) has been recognized as a risk factor for HIV & sexually transmitted infections (STIs) among women, particularly among those who are drug involved. This study examines the temporal relationships between sexual &/or physical partner violence (IPV) & sexual risk of HIV/STI transmission in a longitudinal study with a random sample of 416 women enrolled in methadone maintenance treatment programs in New York City. Two hypotheses are tested: whether sexual risk-related factors or risk reduction behavior leads to subsequent IPV (H1); & whether IPV decreases likelihood of subsequent risk reduction behavior (i.e., requesting to use condoms) or increases likelihood of certain sexual risk-related factors (i.e., inconsistent condom use, having unprotected anal sex, having more than one partner, exchanging sex for drugs or money, having had an STI, being HIV positive, having a partner who engaged in HIV risk) (H2). Participants were interviewed at three waves: baseline, six months & twelve months. Hypotheses were examined using propensity score matching & multiple logistic regression analyses. The prevalence rate of any physical or sexual IPV was 46% at baseline. Findings for H1 indicate that women who reported always using condoms at wave 2 were significantly less likely than women who reported inconsistent or no condom use to experience subsequent IPV at wave 3. Similarly, increased risk of IPV at wave 3 was associated with self-reported STIs (OR=2.0, p=.03), & unprotected anal sex (OR= 2.0, p<.01); always requesting that partners use condoms was associated with a significant decrease in subsequent IPV (OR=.18, p<.01). Findings for H2 suggest that IPV at wave 2 decreased the subsequent likelihood of always using condoms at wave 3 (OR=.41, p<.01) & always requesting that a partner use condoms (OR=.42, p=.02). The implications of the findings for HIV prevention interventions for women on methadone are discussed. Tables, Figures, References. [Copyright 2005 Elsevier Ltd.]

Women's Experience of Intimate Partner Violence in Haiti

Author: Gage, Anastasia J11 Dept International Health & Development, Tulane U, New Orleans, LA agage@tulane.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/59986282?accountid=14925

Abstract: This study examined individual, partner, & community characteristics associated with the occurrence of intimate partner violence among ever-married women of reproductive age, using data from the 2000 Haiti Demographic & Health Survey. Separate logistic regressions were analyzed to assess women's risks of experiencing emotional, physical & sexual violence & multiple forms of intimate partner violence in the past 12 months. Twenty-nine percent of women in the sample experienced some form of intimate partner violence in the past 12 months, with 13 percent having experienced at least two different forms of violence. Significant positive associations with all forms of violence were found for lack of completion of primary school, history of violence exposure in women's families of origin either through witnessing violence between parents while growing up or direct experience of physical violence perpetrated by family members, partner's jealousy, partner's need for control, partner's history of drunkenness, & female-dominated financial decision-making. Significant positive associations were found between men's physical abuse of children at the community level & women's risk of experiencing emotional & physical violence. Neighborhood poverty & male unemployment, number of children living at home, women's attitudinal acceptance of wife beating, & male-dominated financial decision-making were additional risk factors for sexual violence. Women's economic independence was a protective factor for emotional & physical violence, while relationship quality was protective for all forms of violence & multiple victimizations. Tables, Figures, References. [Copyright 2005 Elsevier Ltd.]

Does Pregnancy Provide Immunity from Intimate Partner Abuse among Hong Kong Chinese Women?

Author: Lau, Ying11 U Hong Kong, North Point, Hong Kong h9770024@hkusua.hku.hk

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60027130?accountid=14925

Abstract: The level of intimate partner abuse that occurs in Hong Kong Chinese families & the type, nature, & severity of abuse are primary concerns of those who are engaged in efforts to reduce & control abuse. A total of 1200 postnatal women were recruited at a university-affiliated regional public hospital to participate in a study with a retrospective explorative comparative design. The women were identified as abused or non-abused using the Abuse Assessment Screen Questionnaire (AAS). The type, nature, & profile of intimate partner abuse were investigated by using the Revised Conflict Tactics Scale (CTS-2). The vulnerable factors & predictors of abuse during pregnancy were explored in their demographic, psycho-socio-economic-cultural, & obstetric aspects. Analysis of the data indicated that 134 (11.2%) of the women had experienced abuse during pregnancy. The majority of this abuse took the form of psychological aggression (79.1%), & nearly half (49.3%) of the abused women had experienced sexual abuse. The husband was the perpetrator in the majority of cases (82.1%). Women who were younger, born in Hong Kong, single or cohabiting, had poor socio-economic status, a poor partner relationship, & an unplanned pregnancy were more likely to be abused based on multiple logistic regression models of various nature & type of abuse (p<0.05). The findings provide fundamental information for the prevention of intimate partner abuse among the Hong Kong population. The implications, limitations, & future research are discussed. Tables, References. [Copyright 2005 Elsevier Ltd.]

Attachment and Family Violence: Complexities in Knowing

Author: Bolen, Rebecca M11 Coll Social Work, U Tennessee, Knoxville

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61389895?accountid=14925

Abstract: After briefly surveying previous research on attachment among children in both abusive & nonabusive families, methodological complexities involved in the measurement of attachment are noted. A study by K. J. Sternberg et al (2005) that measured attachment indirectly using self-reports to capture individual perceptions of attachment among Israeli adolescents in families with histories of domestic violence is cited as "methodologically rigorous" as well as illustrative of the difficulties of such research. Sternberg & et al's findings -- particularly that witnessing domestic violence was not adversely related to adolescents' perceived attachment to their parents -- should be viewed with caution until they can be replicated & the attachment measures validated. Nonetheless, these findings offer hope that adolescents' attachments to parents can serve as buffers against the effects of witnessing family violence; implications for intervention are also discussed. References. K. Hyatt Stewart

Adolescents' Perceptions of Attachment to Their Mothers and Fathers in Families with Histories of Domestic Violence: A Longitudinal Perspective

Author: Sternberg, Kathleen J1; Lamb, Michael E; Guterman, Eva; Abbott, Craig B; Dawud-Noursi, Samia1 c/o Lamb -- Dept Social & Development Psychology, Cambridge U, UK

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/59984403?accountid=14925

Abstract: Effects of both childhood & teenage experiences of domestic violence on adolescent-parent attachments were examined. Israeli adolescents (M = 15.9 years) who were either victims of physical abuse, witnesses of physical spouse abuse, victims & witnesses of abuse, or neither victims nor witnesses of abuse were questioned about attachments to their parents using the Inventory of Parent & Peer Attachment [IPPA; Armsden, G. C., & Greenberg, M. T. (1987). The inventory of parent & peer attachment: Individual differences & their relationship to psychological well-being in adolescence. Journal of Youth and Adolescence, 16, 427-454]. Abuse status 5 years earlier was unrelated to the adolescents' current perceptions of their attachments whereas current abuse status predicted the adolescents' perceptions of attachment to their mothers. Adolescents who were victims of physical abuse reported weaker attachments to their parents than adolescents who were not abused or who had solely witnessed interparental physical abuse. Attachments to mothers were weaker whether or not mothers were the perpetrators of abuse. These findings suggest that victimization adversely affects children's perceptions of relationships with their parents, but that changes in the exposure to family violence are associated with changes in relationships with parents. These findings suggest that intervention can have positive effects on parent-child relationships despite violent histories. Tables, References. [Copyright 2005 Elsevier Ltd.]

Gender, Traditionalism, and Attitudes Toward Domestic Violence Within a Closed Community

Author: Shoham, Efrat11 Department Ashkelon, Academic College and Kibbutz Netzer Sereni, 70395 Israel

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/810631841?accountid=14925

Abstract: This research was aimed at examining the attitudes toward domestic violence of people living in communal secular and religious kibbutzim. The findings, disregarding gender or traditionalism, indicate that most of the kibbutz members examined view the kibbutz as almost totally lacking any problem of violence toward women by their partners. The belief that the kibbutz home is a secure place for women within the family framework appears, surprisingly, at a higher frequency among women than among men. There are two possible theoretical explanations for this finding. First, the kibbutz lifestyle has indeed brought about equal power relationships, which explains why domestic violence is not viewed as a social problem, mostly by secular women. Second, women, who play a relatively minor role in constructing the public agenda in the kibbutz, have not become aware of the existence of this issue in the closed community.

Understanding Domestic Violence Resource Utilization and Survivor Solutions among Immigrant and Refugee Women: Introduction to the Special Issue

Author: Bhuyan, Rupaleem1; Senturia, Kirsten1 U Washington

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60488041?accountid=14925

Abstract: Opens this special journal issue by reviewing the unique problems posed by domestic violence for immigrant & refugee women in the US. Contributions offer new insights generated by community-based participatory action research in 9 different cultural/ethnic communities in the Seattle, WA, area: African American; American Indian/Alaskan Native; Ethiopian; Cambodian; Filipina; Latina; lesbian/bisexual/transgender; Russian-speaking; & Vietnamese. Results add to knowledge gained from previous research on how both structural factors -- financial resources, social support, knowledge of rights, & availability of culturally competent helping sources -- & cultural factors -- language barriers; beliefs about women's status, roles, & rights; religious beliefs; & attitudes toward seeking help from outsiders -- affect a woman's response to domestic abuse. Recommendations are offered for helping immigrant & refugee women with this problem at the systems, service, & community levels. 13 References. K. Hyatt Stewart

"Women Must Endure according to Their Karma": Cambodian Immigrant Women Talk about Domestic Violence

Author: Bhuyan, Rupaleem1; Mell, Molly; Senturia, Kirsten; Sullivan, Marianne; Shiu-Thornton, Sharyne1 U Washington

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61550305?accountid=14925

Abstract: Asian populations living in the United States share similar cultural values that influence their experiences with domestic violence. However, it is critical to recognize how differential cultural beliefs in the context of immigration & adjustment to life in the United States affect attitudes, interpretations, & response to domestic violence. This article discusses findings from community-based participatory action research that explores how Cambodian immigrant women talk about domestic violence, what forms of abuse contribute to domestic violence, & what strategies they use to cope with & respond to abuse in their lives. The richness of this research lies in the stories that immigrant women tell about their struggle & their strength in addressing domestic violence. 25 References. "For Us It Is Like Living in the Dark": Ethiopian Women's Experiences with Domestic Violence

Author: Sullivan, Marianne1; Senturia, Kirsten; Negash, Tigist; Shiu-Thornton, Sharyne; Giday, Beruke1 Dept Sociomedical Sciences, Mailman School Pubic Health, Columbia U

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61491126?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article discusses the experiences of domestic violence among Ethiopian refugees & immigrants in the United States. A subset (n = 18) of the larger study sample (N = 254) participated in three focus groups with Amharic-speaking survivors of domestic violence who were currently in or had left abusive relationships. The research was conducted through a public health department, University, & community agency partnership. Findings show domestic violence as taking place within a context of immigration, acculturation, & rapid changes in family & social structure. Participants expressed a need for language & culture-specific domestic violence support & advocacy as well as education programs regarding U.S. laws & resources. 23 References. "No Way Out": Russian-Speaking Women's Experiences with Domestic Violence

Author: Crandall, Marie1; Senturia, Kirsten; Sullivan, Marianne; Shiu-Thornton, Sharyne1 Dept Surgery, Northwestern U, Chicago, IL

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60493477?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article explores the experience of domestic violence & utilization of domestic violence resources among immigrant women who were Russian speaking. Participants, many of whom came to the United States as so-called mail-order brides, reported diverse forms of abuse, including isolation & financial restrictions, & were reluctant to get outside help because of embarrassment about their circumstances. Survivors stressed the importance of language- & culture-appropriate outreach & services & urged that women receive information about domestic violence services & laws on immigration. Assistance with housing, child care, & job searches is integral to safe transitions out of abusive relationships. 24 References. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Inc., copyright 2005.]

"Like a Bird in a Cage": Vietnamese Women Survivors Talk about Domestic Violence

Author: Shiu-Thornton, Sharyne1; Senturia, Kirsten; Sullivan, Marianne1 Dept Health Services, U Washington

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60503889?accountid=14925

Abstract: In recent years, a growing literature has emerged that explores the role of culture in domestic violence for ethnic minority populations, including immigrants & refugees. This article presents qualitative data collected from Vietnamese refugee women through a research project in partnership with the Refugee Women's Alliance in Seattle, Washington. Through the women's stories, their own self-awareness of domestic violence as Vietnamese women residing in the United States is available for reflection & review. Issues of acculturation, changing gender roles, examples of strength, & cultural persistence constitute the thematic structure within which these women articulate their needs for creating & sustaining a life free of abuse for themselves & their children. 20 References. Participatory Action Research in Practice: A Case Study in Addressing Domestic Violence in Nine Cultural Communities

Author: Sullivan, Marianne1; Bhuyan, Rupaleem; Senturia, Kirsten; Shiu-Thornton, Sharyne; Ciske, Sandy1 Dept Sociomedical Sciences, Mailman School Public Health, Columbia U

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60497436?accountid=14925

Abstract: Participatory action research (PAR) is increasingly recognized as a viable approach to developing relationships with communities & working closely with them to address complex public health problems. In the case of domestic violence research, where ensuring the safety of women participants who are battered is paramount, participatory approaches to research that include advocates & women who are battered in research design, implementation, analysis, & dissemination are critical to successful & mutually beneficial projects. This article presents a case study of a PAR project that conducted formative qualitative research on domestic violence in nine ethnic & sexual minority communities. The article describes the specific ways in which a PAR approach was operationalized & discusses in detail how community participation shaped various stages of the research. Furthermore, specific actions that resulted from the research project are reported. 20 References. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Inc., copyright 2005.]

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=Participatory+Action+Research+in+Practice%3A+A+Case+Study+in+Addressing+Domestic+Violence+in+Nine+Cultural+Communities&amp;title=Journal+of+Interpersonal+Violence&amp;issn=08862605&amp;date=2005-08-01&amp;volume=20&amp;issue=8&amp;spage=977&amp;author=Sullivan%2C+Marianne%3BBhuyan%2C+Rupaleem%3BSenturia%2C+Kirsten%3BShiu-Thornton%2C+Sharyne%3BCiske%2C+Sandy

Services and Intimate Partner Violence in the United States: A County-Level Analysis

Author: Tiefenthaler, Jill1; Farmer, Amy; Sambira, Amandine1 Dept Economics, Colgate U, Hamilton, NY tiefenthaler@mai.colgate.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60547435?accountid=14925

Abstract: Despite the prevalence of intimate partner violence & its social costs, we show that many counties lack services to help victims & that community resources play a significant role in determining the likelihood of service provision. Because resources are often generated at the local level & state & federal funding usually require a grant proposal, services for victims of intimate partner violence are more likely to be available in well-resourced areas with a major college or university in the county. Analysis of the changes in the provision of services since the Violence Against Women Act was introduced suggests that funding needs to be specifically targeted to underserved areas. 4 Tables, 49 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=Services+and+Intimate+Partner+Violence+in+the+United+States%3A+A+County-Level+Analysis&amp;title=Journal+of+Marriage+and+Family&amp;issn=00222445&amp;date=2005-08-01&amp;volume=67&amp;issue=3&amp;spage=565&amp;author=Tiefenthaler%2C+Jill%3BFarmer%2C+Amy%3BSambira%2C+Amandine

Resources, Family Organization, and Domestic Violence Against Married Women in Minya, Egypt

Author: Yount, Kathryn M11 Dept Global Health & Sociology, Emory U, Atlanta, GA kyount@sph.emory.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60489825?accountid=14925

Abstract: I evaluate the influence of household wealth, women's socioeconomic dependence, status inconsistency, & family organization on physical abuse in the prior year & attitudes about wife abuse & divorce among 2,522 married women in Minya, Egypt. Household wealth is negatively associated with physical abuse. Women who are dependent on marriage because they have sons & less schooling than their husbands are more likely to have experienced physical abuse & to report marginally more tolerance for such abuse. Women who are isolated from natal or biological kin & living with marital relatives are more likely to have experienced physical abuse. Findings underscore the role of women's dependence & social isolation in enabling physical abuse among women of all economic classes. 3 Tables, 2 Figures, 63 References. Adapted from the source document.

The Reporting of Domestic Violence and Sexual Assault by Nonstrangers to the Police

Author: Felson, Richard B1; Pare, Paul-Philippe1 Dept Sociology, Pennsylvania State U, University Park

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60494224?accountid=14925

Abstract: We examine the effects of the gender of the victim & offender & their relationship to each other on whether sexual & physical assaults are reported to the police. We also examine the reasons victims give for not reporting assaults & whether reporting patterns have changed over time. The analyses are based on a sample of 6,291 physical assaults & 1,787 sexual assaults from the National Violence Against Women Survey. The results suggest that victims are just as likely to report domestic assaults as they are to report assaults by other people they know. Male victims are particularly reluctant to report assaults by their intimate partners, whereas third parties are particularly unlikely to report assaults by partners of either gender. Sexual assaults, particularly those that involve acquaintances, are less likely to be reported. These patterns have not changed since the 1960s. 3 Tables, 2 Figures, 47 References. Adapted from the source document.

The Interactive Construction of Narrative Styles in Sensitive Interviews: The Case of Domestic Violence Research

Author: Enosh, Guy1; Buchbinder, Eli1 School Social Work, U Haifa, Israel

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60011974?accountid=14925

Abstract: Awareness of the possible power relations & conflicts between interviewer & interviewee in qualitative research has grown in recent years; however, scant attempts have been made to analyze this phenomenon empirically. The current work uses interviews conducted with battered women & batterers to understand the co-construction of the narrative of domestic violence. The emergent narrative style is shown to be the product of the interaction between interviewer & interviewee. Four different narrative styles are identified: the narrative style (a) as a struggle, (b) as deflection, (c) as negotiation, & (d) as a self-observation process. The divergent goals & agendas of interviewers & interviewees are discussed, as well as the replication of relational patterns of the interviewee in the interview process. Emphasized are the implications for the study of sensitive domains & power relations in qualitative research. Tables, Figures, References. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Inc., copyright 2005.]

Children in the Crossfire: Child Custody Determinations among Couples with a History of Intimate Partner Violence

Author: Kernic, Mary A1; Monary-Ernsdorff, Daphne J; Koepsell, Jennifer K; Holt, Victoria L1 U Washington

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60015282?accountid=14925

Abstract: Although most states mandate considerations of intimate partner violence (IPV) in child custody proceedings, little is known about how often a preexisting history of IPV is effectively presented to the courts in dissolution cases &, when it is, what effect it has on child custody & visitation outcomes. This retrospective cohort study examined the effects of a history of IPV, further categorized by whether substantiation of that history existed & whether the court handling the custody proceedings knew of that history, on child custody & visitation outcomes. The findings from this study highlight several issues of concern regarding the reality of child custody among families with a history of IPV. These include two primary concerns: a lack of identification of IPV even among cases with a documented, substantiated history, & a lack of strong protections being ordered even among cases in which a history of substantiated IPV is known to exist. Tables, References. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Inc., copyright 2005.]

Child Custody Mediation in Cases of Domestic Violence: Empirical Evidence of a Failure to Protect

Author: Johnson, Nancy E1; Saccuzzo, Dennis P; Koen, Wendy J1 c/o Saccuzzo -- San Diego State U

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60007237?accountid=14925

Abstract: Mediation of child custody disputes is mandated in several states. Investigators have averred that victims of domestic violence (DV) are greatly disadvantaged in mediation. The present study empirically evaluated outcomes & found that mediators failed to recognize & report DV in 56.9% of the DV cases. The court's screening form failed to indicate DV in at least 14.7% of the violent cases. Mediation resulted in poor outcomes for DV victims in terms of protections, such as supervised visitation & protected child exchanges. Mediator capacity to focus on the child's best interest was called into question. Child custody mediation should not be mandated in cases of DV. Tables, Figures, References. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Inc., copyright 2005.]

Unidirectional and Bidirectional Intimate Partner Violence among White, Black, and Hispanic Couples in the United States

Author: Caetano, Raul1; Ramisetty-Mikler, Suhasini; Field, Craig A1 School Public Health, U Texas Houston, Dallas

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60045686?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article describes the rates & risk factors of unidirectional & bidirectional intimate partner violence (IPV) among White, Black & Hispanic couples in the US. Subjects constitute a multistage area probability sample representative of married & cohabiting couples from the 48 contiguous United States. Results indicate that most couples reporting violence engage in bidirectional IPV. Blacks are more likely than Whites to report bidirectional IPV. In addition, severe unidirectional & bidirectional IPV are more common among Blacks & Hispanics. The results also indicate that predictors of IPV vary depending on whether it is unidirectional or bidirectional. Unidirectional female-to-male IPV & bidirectional IPV are more associated with the characteristics of the female. In contrast, the occurrence of unidirectional male-to-female IPV is associated with a single risk factor, male childhood physical abuse. These findings highlight the importance of distinguishing different types of IPV & have important epidemiological & prevention implications. Tables, References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=Unidirectional+and+Bidirectional+Intimate+Partner+Violence+among+White%2C+Black%2C+and+Hispanic+Couples+in+the+United+States&amp;title=Violence+and+Victims&amp;issn=08866708&amp;date=2005-08-01&amp;volume=20&amp;issue=4&amp;spage=393&amp;author=Caetano%2C+Raul%3BRamisetty-Mikler%2C+Suhasini%3BField%2C+Craig+A

The Response of Christian Clergy to Domestic Violence: Help or Hindrance?

Author: Shannon-Lewy, Colleen1; Dull, Valerie T1 Dept Psychiatry, Oregon Health & Science U, Portland

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61703291?accountid=14925

Abstract: This paper reviews the literature regarding Christian clergy as a resource for victims of domestic violence, a common threat to women. Although assistance is available, there are more victims than resources, resulting in many women having limited to no help at all. One resource for victims is the clergy, who are often perceived as respected members of the community & have the authority to make suggestions or even interventions about family issues. However, the literature suggests that cleric theological beliefs may hinder their ability to counsel victims, & the effectiveness of cleric ability to counsel victims varies greatly. Future directions for research are suggested in order to understand what factors influence cleric attitudes & counseling abilities. References. [Copyright 2005 Elsevier Ltd.]

Arrest and Intimate Partner Violence: Toward a More Complete Application of Deterrence Theory

Author: Williams, Kirk R11 Dept Sociology & Robert Presley Center Crime & Justice Studies, U California, Riverside Kirk.Williams@UCR.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61684371?accountid=14925

Abstract: Two decades of research have failed to produce consistent & compelling results that arrest deters intimate partner violence. This tradition of research is reviewed, concluding that little can be learned about the deterrent efficacy of arrest (or other sanctions) for this type of violence until a more complete framework of deterrence theory is specified to guide further research. The framework should delineate mediating influences besides deterrence, linking arrest to the prevention, reduction, or cessation of intimate partner violence, & factors that moderate those influences. Such factors bear on the differential sensitivity to sanctions on the part of actual or potential perpetrators of intimate partner violence. Recommendations for future research are offered, including suggestions for data needed to draw defensible causal inferences about these mediating & moderating influences. Figures, References. [Copyright 2005 Elsevier Ltd.]

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=Arrest+and+Intimate+Partner+Violence%3A+Toward+a+More+Complete+Application+of+Deterrence+Theory&amp;title=Aggression+and+Violent+Behavior&amp;issn=13591789&amp;date=2005-09-01&amp;volume=10&amp;issue=6&amp;spage=660&amp;author=Williams%2C+Kirk+R

The Gender Paradigm in Domestic Violence Research and Theory: Part 1 -- The Conflict of Theory and Data

Author: Dutton, Donald G1; Nicholls, Tonia L1 Dept Psychology, U British Columbia, Vancouver, B.C dondutton@shaw.ca

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61706356?accountid=14925

Abstract: Feminist theory of intimate violence is critically reviewed in the light of data from numerous incidence studies reporting levels of violence by female perpetrators higher than those reported for males, particularly in younger age samples. A critical analysis of the methodology of these studies is made with particular reference to the Conflict Tactics Scale developed & utilised by Straus & his colleagues. Results show that the gender disparity in injuries from domestic violence is less than originally portrayed by feminist theory. Studies are also reviewed indicating high levels of unilateral intimate violence by females to both males & females. Males appear to report their own victimization less than females do & to not view female violence against them as a crime. Hence, they differentially under-report being victimized by partners on crime victim surveys. It is concluded that feminist theory is contradicted by these findings & that the call for "qualitative" studies by feminists is really a means of avoiding this conclusion. A case is made for a paradigm having developed amongst family violence activists & researchers that precludes the notion of female violence, trivializes injuries to males & maintains a monolithic view of a complex social problem. Tables, References. [Copyright 2005 Elsevier Ltd.]

The Future of Research on Intimate Partner Violence: Person-Oriented and Variable-Oriented Perspectives

Author: Bogat, G Anne1; Levendosky, Alytia A; Von Eye, Alexander1 Dept Psychology, Michigan State U, East Lansing bogat@msu.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61368089?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article uses the distinction between the person & the variable orientations as a frame for structuring (1) research on IPV, (2) results of this research, & (3) an agenda for future research. The main tenet of this agenda is that causes, processes, & effects of IPV are person & context specific; therefore, results that relate variables to each other are of limited value if it can be shown that the processes that take place, the meaning of the variables, the profile of the transgressors & the women who experience IPV depend on time, environment, & research design. Examples of valuable variable-oriented research are given; examples of the growing contribution made by person-oriented research are given as well. Sample theories are sketched out, for the variable-oriented, the person-oriented, & the theoretical research arenas, the need for future research is derived from inconsistencies & shortcomings in both current research & theory. Sample research questions are proposed. The contributions made by the authors of this Special Section are embedded within the discussion of research & theory of IPV. It is shown that each of the contributions is valuable within a particular segment of the research landscape. Figures, References. Adapted from the source document.

A Theoretical Framework for Understanding Help-Seeking Processes among Survivors of Intimate Partner Violence

Author: Liang, Belle1; Goodman, Lisa; Tummala-Narra, Pratyusha; Weintraub, Sarah1 Dept Counseling & Developmental Psychology, Lynch School Education, Boston Coll, Chestnut Hill, MA liangbe@bc.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61354207?accountid=14925

Abstract: This paper suggests a conceptual framework for understanding the processes of help-seeking among survivors of intimate partner violence (IPV). A cognitive theory from general literature on help-seeking in "stigmatizing" situations suggests three relevant processes or stages of seeking help in the IPV context: defining the problem, deciding to seek help, & selecting a source of support. Individual, interpersonal, & sociocultural factors that influence decision-making at each of these stages are discussed & illustrated with case examples. Figures, References. Protective Factors against Suicide Attempt Risk among African American Women Experiencing Intimate Partner Violence

Author: Meadows, Lindi A1; Kaslow, Nadine J; Thompson, Martie P; Jurkovic, Gregory J1 c/o Kaslow -- Emory Dept Psychiatry & Behavioral Sciences, Grady Health System, Emory U School Medicine, Atlanta, GA

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61397118?accountid=14925

Abstract: Protective factors (hope, spirituality, self-efficacy, coping, social support-family, social support-friends, & effectiveness of obtaining resources) against suicide attempts were examined in economically, educationally, & socially disadvantaged African American women (100 suicide attempters, 100 nonattempters) who had experienced recent intimate partner violence. Significant positive associations were found between all possible pairs of protective factors. Bivariate logistic regressions revealed that higher scores on each of the seven protective factors predicted nonattempter status; multivariate logistic regressions indicated that higher scores on measures of hope or social support-family showed unique predictive value for nonattempter status. Further, the multivariate model accurately predicted suicide attempt status 69.5% of the time. Partial support was found for a cumulative protective model hypothesizing a linear relationship between the number of protective factors endorsed & decreased risk for suicide attempts. Implications of these findings for community-based preventive intervention efforts & future research are discussed. Tables, References. Adapted from the source document.

The Influence of Community Violence on the Functioning of Women Experiencing Domestic Violence

Author: Bogat, G Anne1; Leahy, Kerry; von Eye, Alexander; Maxwell, Christopher; Levendosky, Alytia A; Davidson, William S, II1 Dept Psychology, Michigan State U, East Lansing, An Arbor bogat@msu.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61390082?accountid=14925

Abstract: The relationships among women's experiences of domestic violence, community violence, & their mental health functioning were explored (N = 94). Social contagion theory was used to argue for the link between community violence & family violence. Results revealed that women's experiences of domestic violence were not related to community violence. Furthermore, women's mental health functioning was solely associated with their experiences of domestic violence, not with community violence. Results are discussed in terms of an ecological model of domestic violence & future directions for exploring linkages between neighborhood characteristics & individual experiences. Tables, References. Adapted from the source document.

Effects of Intimate Partner Violence on Low-Income Women's Health and Employment

Author: Staggs, Susan L1; Riger, Stephanie1 c/o Riger -- U Illinois, Chicago

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/59986505?accountid=14925

Abstract: Rates of both intimate partner violence & poor health are high among low-income women This paper examines relationships among abuse, health, & employment stability using data from a 3-year study of over 1,000 female welfare recipients in Illinois. Results demonstrate the importance of accounting for both recency & chronicity of intimate partner violence & understanding the mediational role of health in the relationship between intimate partner violence & employment. Chronic intimate partner violence is associated with poor health, whereas recent intimate partner violence is associated with unstable employment. Mediations analyses suggest that health does not mediate the effects of abuse on employment stability over a 3-year period. Tables, References. Adapted from the source document.

Domestic Violence and Women's Employment: Fixed Effects Models of Three Waves of Women's Employment Study Data

Author: Tolman, Richard M1; Wang, Hui-Chen1 School Social Work, U Michigan, Ann Arbor rtolman@umich.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61389867?accountid=14925

Abstract: Domestic violence can interfere with women's ability to work, & may result in loss of welfare benefits & poorer economic outcomes. Previous studies showing no effect of domestic violence on employment could be a result of the failure to control for some individual characteristics; therefore we use fixed-effects models with three waves of Women's Employment Study (WES) data to control for unobservable time-invariant individual-specific characteristics. Included in our analyses were 598 women, from an urban county in Michigan who were on the welfare rolls in February of 1997, all of whom completed 3 waves of interviews. Our fixed effects regression results show that domestic violence significantly reduced the annual work hours of a respondent. Mental & physical health problems do not completely mediate this relationship. Our results support efforts to address domestic violence within the welfare system. Tables, References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=Domestic+Violence+and+Women%27s+Employment%3A+Fixed+Effects+Models+of+Three+Waves+of+Women%27s+Employment+Study+Data&amp;title=American+Journal+of+Community+Psychology&amp;issn=00910562&amp;date=2005-09-01&amp;volume=36&amp;issue=1-2&amp;spage=147&amp;author=Tolman%2C+Richard+M%3BWang%2C+Hui-Chen

Neo-Liberalism and the Pathologising of Public Issues: The Displacement of Feminist Service Models in Domestic Violence Support Services

Author: McDonald, John11 Centre Health Research and Practice, U Ballarat, Australia j.mcdonald@ballarat.edu.au

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61390426?accountid=14925

Abstract: Using domestic violence support services as a case study, this paper examines how the ascendancy of neo-liberalism has individualised and pathologlsed public issues. Four perspectives are identified that have been influential in understanding the causes of domestic violence, determining responses to it and measuring the effectiveness of support services. These four perspectives may be categorised as: (i) victim-blaming; (ii) social movement; (iii) empowerment; and (iv) pathologising. From analysing the standard outcome measures currently used for government-funded accommodation programs, the author contends that the pathologising perspective dominates. This is partly attributable to the inherent methodological and ethical issues in evaluating programs of this nature. However, it is primarily driven by the ascendancy of a neo-liberal, managerialist ideology that has depoliticised and clinicalised domestic violence. This has effectively silenced structural analyses of domestic violence and displaced feminist service models. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=Neo-Liberalism+and+the+Pathologising+of+Public+Issues%3A+The+Displacement+of+Feminist+Service+Models+in+Domestic+Violence+Support+Services&amp;title=Australian+Social+Work&amp;issn=0312407X&amp;date=2005-09-01&amp;volume=58&amp;issue=3&amp;spage=275&amp;author=McDonald%2C+John

Marginality and the New Geography of Domestic Violence Policy in Post-Communist Poland

Author: Brunell, Laura11 Dept Political Science, Gonzaga U, Spokane, WA brunell@gonzaga.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60011643?accountid=14925

Abstract: The article explores the spatial distribution & institutional geography of domestic violence service provision in post-communist Poland. A new institutional geography providing services to victims of domestic violence is emerging in Poland as a result of NGO activism & new pro-woman policies implemented by the state. NGOs, often in partnership with local governments, are the most vital means of service provision in large & medium size cities, while in rural areas, public agencies predominate in the institutional geography of service provision. The assumption that NGOs will emerge to address the needs of victims of domestic violence is not realistic in rural areas. While urban Poland is developing an institutional geography to address domestic violence, state & NGO activists must focus on shrinking the rural margins of Poland's institutional geography. Tables, Figures, References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://up7af9tu5s.search.serialssolutions.com/?&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:&amp;atitle=Marginality+and+the+New+Geography+of+Domestic+Violence+Policy+in+Post-Communist+Poland&amp;title=Gender%2C+Place+and+Culture&amp;issn=0966369X&amp;date=2005-09-01&amp;volume=12&amp;issue=3&amp;spage=293&amp;author=Brunell%2C+Laura

Theorizing Police Response to Domestic Violence in the Singaporean Context: Police Subculture Revisited

Author: Narayanan, Ganapathy11 Dept Sociology, National U Singapore socng@nus.edu.sg

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60034367?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article focuses on the well documented, yet potentially contested concept of rank-&-file police subculture to conceptualize police response to situations of domestic violence in Singapore. It argues that the utility of the concept to explaining police behavior is often undermined by an all-powerful, homogenous, & deterministic conception of it that fails to take into account the value of agency in police decision-making & the range of differentiated police response in situations of domestic violence. Through reviewing the literature on police response to domestic violence, this study called for the need to rework the concept of police subculture by treating it as having a relationship with, & response to, the structural conditions of policing, while retaining a conception of the active role played by street-level officers in instituting a situational practice. Using Pierre Bourdieu's relational concepts of "habitus" & "field", designating the cultural dispositions of police subculture & structural conditions of policing respectively, the study attempted to reconceptualize the problem of policing domestic violence with reference to the Singaporean context. References. [Copyright 2005 Elsevier Ltd.]

Portuguese Immigrant Women's Perspctives on Wife Abuse: A Cross-Generational Comparison

Author: Barata, Paula C1; McNally, Mary Jane; Sales, Isabel M; Stewart, Donna E1 University Health Network, Toronto, Ontario

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60038328?accountid=14925

Abstract: This descriptive study seeks to understand what first & second generation Portuguese women believe about wife abuse & what actions they believe are appropriate for an abused wife. Eighty first generation & 54 second generation women participated. The researcher read the questionnaire items aloud in one-on-one meetings. Overall, participants defined wife abuse broadly, did not approve of wife abuse, were most likely to believe that women should seek external help, & did not hold strong patriarchal beliefs. However, a number of generational differences were found. Second generation women were more likely to label an abusive behavior as abuse, & first generation women were more likely to approve of abuse, endorse indirect or traditional options to deal with wife abuse, & hold stronger patriarchal beliefs. The study's implications for research & practice within Portuguese communities are discussed. 2 Tables, 1 Appendix, 40 References. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Inc., copyright 2005.]

Disjunctures for Women and Frontline Workers: Implementation of the Family Violence Option

Author: Lindhorst, Taryn1; Padgett, Julianna D1 U Washington, Seattle

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61310982?accountid=14925

Abstract: This research uses analysis of qualitative interviews with 10 battered welfare clients & 15 frontline welfare workers to examine the implementation of the Family Violence Option (FVO) under welfare reform. States adopting the FVO agree to screen for domestic violence, refer identified victims to community resources, & waive program requirements that would endanger the women or with which they are unable to comply. The analyses find that none of the 10 clients in this study received these services. This lack of services reflects four critical disjunctures between the formal policy & the policy experienced by the clients. It also reveals several more basic structural factors that provide conflicting mandates to frontline workers. Frontline workers' discretionary behaviors enforce core rules related to welfare eligibility & reduce welfare caseloads but do not provide violence-related services to victims. 1 Figure, 46 References. Adapted from the source document.

Does the Life History Calendar Method Facilitate the Recall of Intimate Partner Violence? Comparison of Two Methods of Data Collection

Author: Yoshihama, Mieko; Gillespie, Brenda; Hammock, Amy C; Belli, Robert F; Tolman, Richard M

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61335416?accountid=14925

Abstract: Respondents' recall ability may compromise the validity of their report of lifetime experience of intimate partner violence (IPV). This study compared two interview methods: a commonly used structured interview method & the Life History Calendar (LHC) method, which combines a visual calendar with a semistructured interview schedule to help respondents gain better access to long-term memory. Comparison of two studies conducted with samples drawn from the same sampling frame found that, as hypothesized, the LHC method elicited more reports of lifetime IPV -- especially abuse that occurred early in respondents' lives -- than a structured interview survey method. Adapted from the source document.

Intimate Partner Violence, Substance Use, and HIV among Low-Income Women: Taking a Closer Look

Author: Burke, Jessica G1; Thieman, Laura Knab; Gielen, Andrea C; O'Campo, Patricia; McDonnell, Karen A1 Dept Population & Family Health Sciences, Johns Hopkins Bloomberg School Public Health

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61382545?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article focuses on the intersection of intimate partner violence (IPV), substance use, & HIV status among a sample of low-income urban women (n = 611). Differences emerged by drug type, categorization of IPV, & HIV status. Rates of IPV did not differ between HIV-negative & HIV-positive women, but differing rates of substance use were found to be highly significant. The relationship between IPV & drug use appeared to be stronger for HIV-negative women. Descriptive data capturing temporal relationships between substance abuse & IPV support continued examination of types of IPV separately. These findings indicate the need to create comprehensive intervention strategies to address all three issues.

Abstract: Domestic violence fatality review is defined as the deliberative process whereby communities and their various domestic violence agencies identify homicides and suicides traceable to domestic violence and then, based on the evidence, suggest preventive interventions. The Nature and Impact of Domestic Violence across Age Cohorts

Author: Wilke, Dina J1; Vinton, Linda1 Coll Social Work, Florida State U

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/60545150?accountid=14925

Abstract: This study examined the nature & extent of domestic violence & its impact on psychosocial functioning among women of different age groups. No differences were found across age groups in the severity of violence, nature of injuries, use of alcohol or drugs at the time of the incident, attribution of blame, likelihood to report violence, or rates of childhood physical abuse & depression. However, the older women were more likely to have experienced violence for a longer time, to be in current violent relationships, & to have health & mental health problems than were the younger women. These similarities & differences are discussed in terms of interventions. 3 Tables, 29 References. [Reprinted by permission of Sage Publications Inc., copyright 2005.]
